The Argonaut publishes bright weekly letters from
New York, Paris, and London, from skilled correspondents
“Tlie Bulletin” says
who are in touch with the intense life of those great cities.
It publishes frequent letters from other points in the
old world, and from the older worid of Asia come occa-
sional letters from India, China, and Japan. Its short
the “Argon.ut” is
stories are famous throughout the world. It is hard to
describe their peculiar tinge, but an Idea may be gathered
from this, our standing injunction to our contributors :
“ Whatever you are, never be commonplace.” The re-
tire most readable
suit is that the Argonaut short stories have an individu-
ality of their own. The Argonaut endeavors to present a
weekly review of the literary movement. Brief notes on
American newspaper,
books, rather than lengthy reviews, together with notes
upon authors and journalists, will keep the reader well
up with the literary gossip of the day.

THE RED PAGE.
This is a passage from Maurice Hewlett’s
lately-published “Pan and the Young Shop-
herd,” a pastoral drama in the mode antique.
The scene is on a moor, overlooking the
valley ; Neanias enters carrying Aglae ; he
sets her on a fallen tree and stands looking
at her.
Neanias.
She keeps her eyes fixt on me; I think she is
going to cry. The corners of her mouth are down
—a bad sign. Her lips cling loosely together—a
worse. She hath grey eyes-—how deep and soft!
How far-searching! How seeming to ask for
help of me, like a dumb beast’s ! And her lips are
cold ; and she is dumb ! YV hat should a man do
with a girl at such a time ? Put his arm about
her, doubtless, and give her a kiss, or kisses.
There is that upon her which forbids a careless
handling. Besides—oh, it would be merciless ! I
have heard that of old time the Squire knelt be-
fore his lady to kiss her hand, or even her foot.
Shall I salute her so ? I have her hand, and can
see her sandall’d foot, with the little toes like
windflowers peeping up through the herbage.
The hand is nearer ; I will kiss her hand. How
small, how warm, how gentle a hand ! Her cheek
was cold as a stone—to be warmed some day by
my fire ! I have warmed her hand already in
mine : let me warm the other. (He has both her
hands.) Oh, Aglae, you are trembling ! What’s
the matter, my dear ? Art cold, art afraid, wistful
Aglae ? Why, girl, thou hast a lover, a sweet-
heart ; is it nothing to thee ? Never a lass
yet but hugged the darling thought, if the wisdom
of man speaks true. Cheer then, cheer then,
pretty maid, for I at least will be true, Ah, ah !
You cast down your eyes at that; ’fore God, you
blush—O Aglae, how soft-cheekt you are ! But I
say it again, I will be your true man, even as my
father that is dead loved my mother whom he
stole from a country far from here and taught the
use of love in a quiet valley. She was a sea-
bred woman, black-haired, swan-neckt, and
knew nothing of sheep or husbandry. But
my father taught her all that, and she loves
him yet, though he lies with a broken back
deep under Church mould. Even as my
father was to her, so to you will I be, girl :
even so. Ah, Saints, she looketh up again, with
beseeching and with dumb prayer ! Do her lips
move ? No sound, no sound at all! She must
speak to me ; she shall speak. Look, Aglae, do as
I do; move your lips now as I move mine. Now
thus: tongue to palate, that is L. Yes, yes.
Open lips to form broad O, full as my heart.
Wider yet, and fuller : L, 0 ! Now lower lip to
upper teeth for V, and E as mute as you like. Do
it now. It is done ! That is LOVE, Aglae, that
is LOVE ! . . . Aglae, I will kneel before you
and ask you—stay with me ! Look, now, I have
come over the hills for you, braving dangers, my
word ! and fatigues, and uncertain powers, possi-
bly of the Devil. Stay with me. Your eyes are
soft and very gentle, yet wide with waiting for fear:
narrow them that peace may come in. Do you
fear? You cannot fear me. Look at me, Aglae
—are you afraid ? You shake your head : you are
not ! Then I will tell you more. I am here and
you are here, none beside us, we two in the open
world. Oh, my dear, I love you well! The dan-
ger and sick dread are going with the night; soon
you and the day will smile together. The sun
will touch the hill-tops, and race down the flanks
of the high hills. He will kindle the trees in the
valley, and we shall see his light, and the blue
smoke make aspire to God. Witna wakened world,
the sounds of men and business about us, all
will be well. The ringing anvil at the forge, the
wash of the mill-wheel, the humming where the
wheat is a-threshing, and Merla calling the cows—-
day will come, day will come ! Hark, is that the
priest’s bell ? Not yet, not yet:, courage, wait
awhile, soon will be a wedding, and a bride carried
over the threshold to the hearth ! You the bride,
Aglae, Virgin Dawn ; and I the groom, I Neanias
son of Balkis. The folk will line the Church-way,
strewing flowers and branches; the men will
follow you with their eyes, and the women say,
“The sturdy lad ! of me whom hitherto they
have counted a baby, to talk their freedoms
before me without a blush ! Sturdy enough !
Eor though I am slim built I am very strong,
and could carry you, Aglae, under my arm.
My arm round your middle, I would carry
you over brooks in flood, and up the moor-side
on dewy mornings lest you should wet your pretty
toes. In the orchards I could hold you up to
gather the codlings and peach-apples from the
midmost branches. There should no brambles
tear you, and no thistle-spines nor sword-grass
that cuts like a saw. Oh, I would tend you well,
Aglae, and love you dearly! The homestead
should flow with milk and honey : I would get you
the firstlings of the year, apples and quinces and
mellow pears. Fine wool should be your bed-
clothes, all of first-shear lambs : you should be
drest in white wool and thin flax. My mother,
you must know, is a cunning woman, deft with
her fingers, and her spinning well-known in our
valley and beyond the high hills. Eear not to tear
your hands or harden them with coarse work ; fear
not to line your pale and gentle face with
dark thoughts or cares; fear not to - drudge, fear
nothing. I am with you, Aglae : I will teach you
love; and love, say they, is life. Happy love,
therefore, is blessed life. . . . O Aglae, will you
cry again? Sure, you cannot see me for tears.
You tremble again, your lips quiver, sobs arc in
your throat. Your head on my shoulder, dear;
there, that’s happy, that’s happy. Oh, what a
beating heart! Look now, Agla : ; cry your fill;
don’t he ashamed—I am not looking at you. I
felt a tear on my arm; I know your eyes are
brimming. Cry, cry, my bird, you will he
better after. Have no fear, 1 hold you close
- -side to side: we will be nearer yet—-heart
to heart some day. Ah, ah, you are mine !
I have made you mine. I am a man by now,
and you are mine, and mine, and mine, and my
wife for ever ! Come, Aglae, away. Come ! .
See, Aglae, the white dawn steals round the rim
of the world: soon spears of fire will spike the
East. Virgin Dawn, this is the day, this is our
wedding-day. Hark ! what is that far off and
below us in the haze? The priest’s bell, by our
Lord’s joy ! Sir Topas at his Mass. Come, my
bride, my well-beloved ! Come, heart of mine, to
whom love draws near. Let us seek Sir Topas at
the altar rail. Dawn ! Dawn !
(He takes her hand; they go down the moorside
together. Day-break.)
The fire kindled by Lawson is still smouldering,
but with less flame than ashes. These embers
have for the most part little to recommend them,
but they give a useful glimpse of public opinion on
the questions raised. -Henceforth, however, public
opinion will have to be a lot brighter if it wants to
be printed.

“ 6 * 8 ” = He«ry Lawson is our national poet. He si«4s
our songs! We care not who makes our laws ! And as
the singer of our songs, we have treated him shamefully '
It was right that Lawson unburdened his soul. The hush
men—and they are the people of Australia—worship Law-
son as only bushmen can worship. No mere hero-worship
but a deep, unaffected love for his powerful work Had
the bushmen known long ago how the man who has
brightened their lonely, hard and bitter lives, existed
miserably for years in third-rate Sydney boarding-houses
at a meal a-day, they would have, I am positive, acted in
concert and lifted him financially above the bitter neces-
sity of selling his great brain at the rate of ss. a column
to brain-sweating newspaper-proprietors !
Oiipps Clark :My mail, on a recent evening, con-
sisted only of a letter from my brother (who lives in
Lawson’s Mecca-London) and Thu Bulletin. For obvious
reasons, I opened the letter first, which reads thus : “I
have not heard lately from you, so am writing a<rain about
the stories. I had a letter froiii (literary agent), at
the end of November, saying he had an offer from Bar-
abhas and Co. of £l5 (!) for the copyright of the stories
and that he had at once withdrawn them, and was offer-
ing them in another direction. I wrote him saying it
was an absurd offer, and remarking on the lon«- time
it had taken that firm to decide on such a* mag-
nificent. offer.” )I relit my pipe and read the note
again to make sure that there wasn’tafaint “o”afterthe
£l5, hut devil a sign of it. Then I informed my do<r
who listened sympathetically with his head on one side’
that I was actually offered £l5 for about 400 of
original stories—each, in its way, “ the finest story in
the world.” I thought of the long nights I had spent
writing them, nights when the perspiration trickled down
my nose, and the ink-pot was choked up, and the table
strewn with the gnats that fell from the hanging lamp-
nights that grew longer and colder, when my feet ached
with the cold before I would drop my pen to throw
another log on to the embers in the wide fireplace and
discover that the other jackasses were already stirring up
their mates in the big apple-tree to welcome a new day.
I thought of the writing and the re-writing, and the
final clean copy, made a hasty calculation, of how much
I could have earned if the writing had been law-copy at
sd. per folio, and laughed. Then the kerosene bill and
the cheque I paid for the type-writing of those stories
obtruded themselves upon my memory, and 1 laughed
again. Then I opened my Bulletin and read the troubles
of Lawson and his commentators, and wondered why a
man never knows when and where he is well off. . . .
On a Day by the Sea.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We lay on the beach shaded by a cliff; and
our couch was warm still, though the sun-
shine had crept away. Jack scooped a hole
in the sand and put a cheek there, and told
us lazily it was delicious. Viewed in this
wise, the beach assumed a new aspect. A
rock in the distance, which had hitherto
worn a solemn face, put forth a nose and
lips that laughed ; and a nearer sandbank
took to itself the guise of a stranded whale.
Without voyaging remotely, it is possible to
change one’s landscape ; and I knew a boy
who had the simplest means in the world of
varying a monotonous scene. All he had to
do was to bend down and look between his
legs. He said that everything seen so ap-
peared topsy-turvy. I told him that was
all very well for lissom limbs and easy joints,
but when he grew older it would hardly
seem so pleasant to set the world awry.
* * * *
Down the beach a flock of gulls played
with the surf, chasing and retreating, and,
when it spread around them like spilt milk,
lifting their wings as a child raises her arms
for an “ Upsy-daisy.” A mob of porpoises,
those merry fellows of the deep, rolled
through the swell with melodious motion.
The sea was dim-tinted, as though white
smoke were prisoned there. It slept : for
velvet winds did nothing more than purr and
pass. Outside our nook golden light was
poured through everything ; and a salt mist
that was but the spectre of a spray rose from
the surf and floated inland like a gossamer.
Someone hinted swimming; and we sped to
the sea’s side and took a head-firster into a
roller that shone like clean salt, bobbing up
one by one on the field of foam behind it.
Then, with scarce space to breathe, another
wall fell on us, and we rose half-drowned
and all in love with life.
* * * *
“Did you ever see anything so beautiful ? ”
said Jack.
We lay in the swirl and the Sea rolled us
over and over, and played with us, and
bubbled at our lips, till we nigh forgot she
was the Sea—the silky cat that sheathes her
claws in velvet and pats you gently till her
time come. Afterward, when the sun had
dried us and salt was like powder on the
skin, we crawled on all-fours to the shade.
It was so like heaven there that we grew
mournful at the prospect of leaving it to
Bwelter between four walls. Jack said he
did not know how people could sleep under
a roof on summer nights. He could not.
Sleep and he were strangers. He had tried
various methods of wooing her. Counting
sheep was regarded as perhaps the most
effective of these ; and he had counted sheep.
But it was no use. He would count up to as
many as fifty at times, and then he would
forget sheep and everything and cruise away
to the South Pole, and feel just a trifle
cooler, when all of a sudden his mind would
wear ship once more and take him to the
Tropics, with a red sun overhead.
• * • •
Jack said that the man who invented
sheep-counting as a soporific must have been
a quiet humorist, because the sheep is an
annual that won’t allow itself to be counted.
With him they always started to go over
fences—ten at a time—and just when he
fell into their mood and started to count
them by tens they went over by fifteens, and
if he tried them by fifteens they increased
the number. He said it was curious how
they always went over fences. There was
never any need, because he always had
a gate prepared, but they preferred the
fence. Once or twice he had tried to
drive them, but they refused to be driven,
and they had a firm objection to going
through a gate. He had tried to picture
them without a fence, but then a log would
be bound to appear, and they went over
that. He couldn’t imagine a sheep apart
from something that it had to go over.
Sometimes they took the fence in a multi-
tude, and sometimes, when he was on the
right track and reckoning straitly, one would
get stuck on the ton rail and distract him
from the others. He found that sheep-
counting was unprofitable, and that it was
pleasanter to go voyaging—to the South
Pole for preference. It sometimes helpedj
too, to remember a green cave in the moun-
tains where ferns drooped everywhere, and
the water drip-dripped from twenty places
all at once.
• • • •
Jack said there was a tree in his back-
yard to which a native thrush came every
movping. It sang to a canary in a cage,
and the gold lyrist would be very silent till
the thrush ceased to sing—perhaps because
it loved such wild minstrelsy. Now and
again, at noon of night or thereabouts, it
happened that the leaves of this tree would
rustle, and then Jack would raise his head
and listen ; for the tree was so placed that
nothing but a southerly could disturb it, and
a southerly meant ever so much. But as a
rule the wind would only come, to die, to
fade like a whisper on the lips of Night, and
then his case would be worse than ever,
because the worry of meditating on what
might have been would be added. He had
tried other recipes. Fellows had told him
that lying magnetically, feet to the Equator
and head to the Pole, induced sleep. The
currents coursed the body and drew the
blood from the head. But the currents
failed in his case ; perhaps they deviated
before they reached him. So he gave them
up in disgust, and lay the other wav, because
in that position he could see the stars
through his open window. Jack loves the
stars ; he has fashioned a religion out of
them. ’Tis a sweet faith. He thinks that we
go from star to star spending a life on each,
till we come at length to one felicitous orb
set high away and away in space. And . . .
* * * *
Jack talked and the Sea talked too—
rumbled unheedingly on the beach. The
tide was rising, and at intervals a wave
crashed on the shoal, calling our eyes round
to notice if anything strange had happened.
But we saw only the usual sheet of foam
crawling up the sand, arid then, when it had
reached a certain point, racing back like a
child chased by fancies in the dai’k. At
times, too, we heard voices from the head-
land—wild voices that faded even as we
listened. It was hard to realise that they
did not come from men in a perilous plight;
that they were not the hoarse far-off shouts
of drowning sailors, instead of the outcome
of wind and wave struggling for mastery in a
wet cavern.
* * • m
After dinner we climbed a headland and
saw many ships at sea. The coast was hazed
by blue smoke, each point of land peeping
round its neighbour as though to get a view
of the little beach that cosied there. The
wind had no power to fret the sea, and not a
white-cap flecked it anywhere except on the
beach or near the rocks, where foam foun-
tains rose swiftly and fell lazily. The sun
was in the west, and its rays at an unpropi-
tious angle, else we should have seen down
into the sea, deep even as its green couch.
Clear mornings on these headlands are a
time of wonder. While the wind sleeps and
the blue remains unstreaked, if you should
happen to bend over and look down, you
will see the weeds waving sluggishly, and,
perchance—if luck be with you, and hunger
at his vitals—the great blue groper mouch-
ing up and down the ledges, and poking his
nose through fringes of seaweed. You may
see the native salmon, too, many as leaves
in the forest, and wonder why Nature
created so valueless a fish in such abundance.
With a very slight turn of the hand she
might have given every man on the continent
a fish for dinner. But perhaps man is not
her sole care. She may have other children
to consider.
* * * •
And here is a wonder of the sea. Some-
times the water is crystal clear, and the
salmon lie near the surface. At rare inter-
vals, slanting on a wave; they catch the sun
and flash out like a wall of silver. They
move together as sheep do. But—hush, and
listen ! —these sheep have preposterous shep-
herds. There to seaward of the shoal is one
of them, heading back the breakaways, and
there to shoreward is another moving up and
down between the fish and the sand; and
yonder at the rear is a third, swimming to
and fro and rounding up the stragglers. It
is the old story of the shepherd and the
sheep repeated, only in this case—open your
eyes and wonder !—the shepherd is the
Shark. And it is very strange that the
salmon obey the shark just as sheep obey
the man. Indeed, the fish is very docile and
does not fear the shark half so much as the
sheep fears the shepherd. The shark moves
about among the salmon and they hardly leave
his path, perhaps because they recognise that
he is there to rule and keep them together. It
is only when a sick fish falls out and floun-
ders that the shark takes his wages. Other-
wise the shoal goes unharmed, shifting on
from pasture to pasture like travelling stock.
Now and again, however, disaster befalls it.
That is when porpoises, like wild dogs, burst
upon the fish, sending them all ways in con-
fusion. Then the shark loses his likeness to
the shepherd, and rends his charge till the
sea reddens. But this is only a moment’s
savagery that disappears with the disturber,
and the shepherd goes up and down the
sea driving in his silver sheep till once
again they are a compact shoal.
• • • •
We went down to the beach to see if any
salmon had come in with the tide. Sure
enough they were there in the second roller,
lying layer over layer without a move of fin
or tail. The western sun sent its rays in
among them like so many gold fingers, and
where one lit upon a silver side there was a
sudden glitter that almost pained the eyes—
it was so brilliant. But of the drovers of
the deep we saw nothing, that being a sight
vouchsafed only to the watchers of the coast
who keep a ceaseless vigil by the sea.
RODERIC QUINN.
THE SKY-SHIP.
In the soft wind that blows,
Yon cloud-ship of the sky
Spreads a white sail and throws
A shadow where I lie.
And with my dream is blent
A breath of spice and gums
Out of the Orient,
Betraying whence she cornea.
Unto a land remote
To fill its rich bazaars
Sails this Arabian boat
Amid the island stars,
And in yon harbor calm
Of Heaven’s ocean blue,
Empties her freight of balm—
The twilight’s fragrant dew !
F. D. Sherman.
“PatO.” : 1 protest against encouraging your fellOw-
countryuien to go to London (have you not encouraged
Becke and others ?). Why not keep a band of your young
men with souls above eating and drinking, like Old Ire-
land had one time ! They will live somehow, and they
have plenty of work to do if they would only speak
through the ear of the soul. Australia has wrongs to be
righted ; there are the malign-strong to be cheeked T’m
a n l- a n„rr tr -T man ° f ' VOU, ' s ' but I cr fe y ont, loud and long
against the strong-swift horse and the strong-souled man
leaving this country while I am here.
UNDER HER BALCONY.
Up, slender vine, your love is mine;
I watch you as you go.
A lyric budding line on line
With blossom-rhymes a-row!
Up, up, until her window-sill,
Like Heaven’s gate in sight
Makes all your heart with hope to fill
And bloom with its delight.
And when her eyes’ soft twilight lies
Upon you nestled there,
When all about you is surprise
And all below, despair,
Then to my Sweet, my love repeat;
Yield her one perfect bloom,
Which, though it perish at her feet,
May, ghostlike, haunt her room.
But if her mind and heart be kind,
And grant you gracious rest,
And for this gift a pillow find,
And fold it to her breast . . .
Up, up ! I burn my fate to learn
From her who waits above ;
Let but a leaf to earth return
Her answer and her love !
F. D. SHERMAN.

SPECIAL NOTICE.
In consequence of past complaints, and in order to protect
the interests of Clients, “ Tatter sail ” will not in future
Voluntarily send Wires to Drawers of Likely Starters.
“ Tatter sail ” also declines to communicate to Owners of
Horses on behalf of Drawers more than One-Fifth (1-sth) of
Prize in any Sweep. When, however, any such communication
is requested, it will be sent where practicable to the Secretary of
the Racing Club; but “ Tatter sail ” does not take any responsi-
bility on behalf of Clients in such matters.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Digger Dick.
We’ve pegged a claim for Digger Dick beyond the
gold-lead valley;
His final shaft we’ve sunk him, and he s resting
down below.
The river ripples near him, and the song-birds o’er
him rally,
Where the box-trees murmur music and the native
roses blow.
No coffin coops his body, and no vestments for
adorning,
His wash-dish forms his pillow and his tools lie
close at hand
So he’ll find his kit all ready on the resurrection
morning
When he goes to seek his spirit in the everlasting
land.
He was human, as we all are —so he had his little
failings;
He was not a saint in seeming, nor a hero in his
ways ;
But he fought life’s battle bravely, though his
strikes were mostly tailings,
And his hopes sprang fresh each morning with the
dawning of the days.
He had little scope for doing, but within his
sphere of being
He sought to help and comfort any brother-man
in need —
And I think that in the court to which his spirit
now is fleeing
They will judge him by his actions, and o’erlook
his want of creed.
On the terraces of heaven he will prospect, I’ve
been thinking,
As he did along the rivers of the earth in days of
old;
For of course it stands to reason they must keep
the picks a-clinking
Where they all wear crowns of glory and the
streets are paved with gold.
But I’ll wager the salvation of a lone and weary
hatter
If old Dick can journey earthward when he gets
his golden hat
That he’ll pound it in a mortar, and the golden
fragments scatter
Where some fossicker will strike them when he s
surfacing the flat.
N.S.W.
GEORGE POWICK BROWN.
Blanket Flat.
I’ve padded on the Murray
And the Murrumbidgee, too,
And wasn’t in a hurry
Round the stations that I knew.
I’ve padded in the ranges,
And I’ve perished on the plains,
And “ travelled ” minus “changes ”
In the country where it rains ;
But I’ve killed the trait nomadic
That within my brain had sat,
And I’m ploughing stumpy paddocks
For a man at Blanket Flat.
He hasn’t education,
And he isn’t very young,
And he “ gives away ” his nation
By the burr upon his tongue ;
The burgoo set for breakfast
And the pictures round the place
Will spur the mind to make fast
Calculations of his race.
He’s cautious and he’s canny,
And he’s rather proud of that ;
Has the finest land of any
On the whole of Blanket Flat.
Gee, Punch ! you lazy cow—there,
That’s another blessed root;
If I lose another ploughshare
I’ll most surely get the “ shoot.”
Well, there’s only one thing for it—
Hook on, Gip, and pull it back—
Hang ploughing ! —I abhor it,
But it’s better than the track.
And ’Liza —well, I love her,
Though she is so round and fat;
There’s none I prize above her
On the whole of Blanket Flat.
Way, woh ! that’s Sandy calling out.
“ Come in till dinner, iad.
What mak’s your furrys fall aboot?
You’re plooing awfu’ bad ;
And, losh, mon, where’s your plooshare '!
You’re a carfu’ falley, hoot !
Anither costly new ’share ; losh !
Could ye no mind the root ?
Now just ye talc’ a warning ”
How my spirit falls at that,
For clinging lips this morning
Gave a charm to Blanket Flat.
Old Sandy’s aye complaining
In a burr that bites the ear,
He’s growling when it’s raining,
And he’s girding when it’s clear ;
The erops are good - he’s groaning
That the price of grain will fall ;
The season’s bad—he's moaning
That he has no crop at all.
I little reck his reaping
Whether scanty, scarce, or fat,
A stronger reason’s keeping
Me a slave at Blanket Flat.
I never mind the ending,
Though I know it’s coming near ;
While love has leave for spending
Let enjoyment banish fear.
For willing lips are winning
And are worth a lot of pain—
If sorrow waits the sinning—
Be it so, I’ll sin again !
But time defer the morrow
(For I’m sore afraid of that)
When I’ll “ pad the hoof ” in sorrow
From a job at Blanket Flat.
Yic.
BEN SUN.

IN AMERICA.
The Legend.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Payette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Payette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
them. Marching with drum and life
was an old man, Jeremiah Wynton, and
his son, and grandson. The old father,
with silvery flowing locks, sturdily strode
through the field. The son with ban-
daged head fifing lustily, the youth,
catching the. spirit of his grandsire, rolled
out with him the soul-inspiring rattle of
the drum. Father, son, and grandson
had accompanied the troops as volunteers,
and were ever in the forefront of the
fight. The fierce light of battle was in
their eyes, they knew no fear, and many
a yvild cheer arose, as on that fateful
day Yankee Doodle was heard as never
was heard before or since.

The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, MARCH 11, 1899.
The Globular-Parallelogram Party.
The loud remnant of the old anti-Billious
clan has broken out afresh in New South
W ales, but mainly in Sydney, and is strug-
gling with all its might in the same old cause
of Disunion-at-any-Price. It is an amusing
set of small bigots, with little sense, and no
logic, and a stentorian bray. Every member
of it declares himself a pure Federationist,
on certain impossible conditions, and, if the
party only gets all its impossible conditions,
its enthusiasm will be simply unlimited. It
is anxious to federate above all things, pro-
vided that certain matters are done which
never can be done, either here or hereafter ;
and if arrangements are made so that a man
can be in two places at once, and an irresist-
able force can run against an immovable
object without anything unusual happening,
and there is a square circle and a globular
parallelogram, and boiling icebergs become
quite,common, and a few other things, it is
prepared to back up the cause of Australian
nationhood for all it is worth. And when it
is called an anti-Federal party it is very
angry indeed. The square circle and globular
parallelogram party wants Federation as
much as anybody—with proper precautions.
The principal precautions are that seventeen
miles shall be 2s. 6d., and that a £l-note
shall have four legs and a rich tenor voice.
• * * *
The chief complaint against the old
Federal Bill was that it contained a “ cast-
iron Constitution.” The cast-iron Constitu-
tion, and the want of any proviso for alter-
ing the same at the desire of the majority,
were grievances which filled New South
Wales with rage and howl and bitterness. It
was also complained that there was no stipu-
lation that the capital should be in New
South Wales. The Premiers’ Conference
agreed to put the capital in New South
Wales, and made the Constitution rather
easier of amendment. But now the air is
filled with the plaint that the concession as
regards the capital is nil because there is
nothing to prevent the other States carrying
an amendment to the Constitution later on to
shift the capital out of New South Wales again.
The globular parallelogram party wants a
Constitution which can be easily altered at the
desire of the majority. And it also wants a
Constitution which can’t be altered by any
possibility, or at any price, by the biggest
majority in Australasia—if the alteration is
one which its little cult doesn’t ap-
prove of. At the same time, it says that
the provisions for altering the Constitution
are insufficient as well as excessive—that a
simple majority of the people of Australia
should decide. It says, also, that the great
danger is that the Federal Parliament, after
holding its first meetings in Melbourne, may
refuse to select any capital in New South
Wales at all, and simply settle down at Mel-
bourne indefinitely, the reason assigned
being that a majority of the people—the
populations of Victoria, Tasmania, South
Australia, and Westralia—all favor that city.
The party with the square circle and the
seven Sundays in the month demands that a
bare majority should have power to alter at
its sole pleasure the compact under which
the provinces entered the Commonwealth.
And the shadowy fear that a bare majority
may alter the compact in this particular
respect is so sliuddersome that the red-hot
snowball party wants New South Wales to
stand out altogether.
* * * *
Also, the crowd which demands that ice
shall grow on matches, and that all triangles
shall be round-square, says that the capital
must be fixed at Sydney, and that New
South Wales must have larger powers over
“ its own ” rivers. It has already set forth
that the proposed Constitution is so easily
altered by the base conspirators of the other
provinces that these concessions would be of
no value—they could simply be taken back
again. And it has also proved that the Con-
stitution can’t be altered nearly so easily as
it should be. The same upside-down mob,
with its hat on its feet, while it wants the
majority to decide in matters relating to the
alteration of the Constitution—except in
alterations which it, the said inverted mob,
personally objects to—refused to let the New
South Wales majority decide on the accept-
ance of the Constitution last June. The
majority accepted it, and Parliament peace-
fully disqualified the majority. And it is
now getting up a petition requesting Parlia-
ment to reject the Bill altogether, and not
let the majority have any say in the matter
at all. And it is already starting an agitation
with a view to providing that, should Federa-
tion be accomplished, the present New South
Wales Premier will be entrusted by the
Governor with the formation of the first
Federal Ministry and be boss thereof, whether
the majority wants him or not. But, all the
same, the right of the majority to rule is a
thing which the anti-Billious crowd insists
on tremendously.
• * * *
Also the Haynes and Heydon and Jaw-
bone Neild push—the gang which won’t be
satisfied unless it can get far enough round
to bite its own ear—has got another griev-
ance. It would be unfair, it holds, simply
to put to the electors the question : “Are
you in favor of the new Bill or not ?” Such
a proceeding, it says, might induce many
people to vote “Yes” when they didn’t
really mean to. So it desires to confuse the
issue by putting three questions at the next
Federal referendum : (1) “ Are you in
favor of this Bill?” (2) “Are you in
favor of a Bill containing all the amend-
ments submitted by the New South
Wales Parliament ? ” (3) “Are you against
both ? ” This is very different from the
way the same party put the matter to
the people last year. On 3rd June the
people were asked to say : “ Are you for or
against the old Bill ? ” and the people
accepted it by over 5000 majority, which
majority Parliament disqualified. Then at
the general election the New South Wales
community was asked : “Are you in favor
of Freetrade, plenty of borrowed money, a
reformed Upper House, George Reid, and
this list of Federal amendments ; or will you
simply say you are against these Federal
amendments without any chance to say
whether you want a different lot, or whether
you are against Federation, or where or
what you are ? ” The country responded by
leaving the direct Reid party with its amend-
ments in a miserable minority, but, by the aid
of the Labor members, Reid just managed to
carry on. And now, in its anxiety to give
the province an opportunity to fully state its
views the Haynes and Holman crowd
desires to put the Federal question for the
third time in these terms : (1) “ Do you want
any Bill at all?” (2) “Do you want this amend-
ed Bill ? ” (3) “Do you want the amended
Bill suggested by the N.S. Wales Parlia-
ment, which wasn’t the amended Bill that
Premier Reid put before youatthe election ? ”
(Memo. : If you want the old Bill, which
you accepted by a 5000-majority last year,
you aren’t allowed to say so. If you want
some kind of Bill quite unlike either of them
you aren’t allowed to say so either). This
anxiety to split the pro-Federal vote in two
under the fraudulent pretence of giving the
people a fuller opportunity to state their
views is very touching indeed.
• • * •
The anti-Billious league, in the wild mani-
festo wherein it demands that two quarts of
beer shall go simultaneously into one pint-
pot, insists most emphatically that the capi-
tal shall not, under any circumstances, be at
Brisbane, Perth, Adelaide, Hobart, or Mel-
bourne, or in any of the provinces there-
unto attached, and it curses something
terrible in paragraph V . of its manifesto
about the “undeserved stigma” cast upon
Sydney by the stipulation of the other pro-
vinces that the capital shall not be there
either. As a concern which started by cast-
ing exactly the same stigma not only on all
the other capitals but also on all the other
provinces, the anti-Billious push should at
least be an authority on stigmas. And the
anti-Billious O. G. Heydon writes pointing
out that there are really fears that New
South Wales will be “outvoted” in the
Federal Parliament; Heydon wants the
majority to rule, and is afraid at the same
time that the 1,300,000 people of New South
Wales won’t have as many votes as 2,000,000
in the other five States. And in general
the anti-Billious party is dead sure of
one thing —it enters on its head with
a deafening footmark to take its solemn
oath thereto quite unanimously ; The
80,000 minimum at last Referendum (the
minimum which the anti-Billious party fought
like a cataract of tigers to get inserted) was
a mistake. It kept the anti-Billious multi-
tude from voting because they knew the
Billious crowd couldn’t get the required
80,000 ; therefore the anti-Billious patriots
stayed at home. If it hadn’t been for the
80,000 minimum, which the anti-Billious
men fought so desperately to get, there
would have been a great anti-Billious majo-
rity. This time, there seems no chance of
getting an 80,00# minimum ; it will ap-
parently be just that square fight which the
anti-Billious said they wanted so badly last
time, and which they dodged so carefully.
And instead of rising up with their great
alleged majority, they are imploring Parlia-
ment not to let the people vote at all—to
throw the measure out bodily and smother
the national referendum, which, in theory,
they mostly say is a thing they especially
approve of.
* * * *
Above all things, the parish-pump gang is
very grieved about the ingratitude of the
other provinces in view of the fact that New
South Wales is making greater “ sacrifices ”
for the Federal cause than any of them. As
the present agreement stands, each province
keeps its own lands, railways, and public
debt. They all pay the same taxes. Every
one gets back its own surplus. Every one
takes the same risk of having its fiscal policy
reversed by the Federal Government. New
South Wales doesn’t propose to help the
other provinces with money or credit, or to
take any of their. burdens, or do anything
more than the other provinces do. But it
claims to own the capital; to get all the
trade of the capital; to have sole possession
of the only great river system in Australia,
though half its waters come from Queensland
and Victoria ; and it begins to talk of its
right to own the first Federal Premier. And
the anti-Billite mob, in addition to all these
things, is simply broken up at the ingrati-
tude of the other provinces in not recognis-
ing the extraordinary “sacrifices” of their
province. Altogether, the anti-Billious are
a weird crow’d, and their wobblings are
strange to contemplate.
The Land-Tax Superstition.
An article headed “ The Land-tax Supersti-
tion,” in The Bulletin of 14th Jan., has
roused about three dozen Single-taxers to reply
in various phases of indignation. The follow-
ing from Mr. J. E.. Phillips, of Auckland
(M.L.) puts the Single-taxers’ case about as well
as any of them :
The contention is that the land of a State belongs by
right to all the inhabitants of that State—the Socialist
says in equal proportion. If, therefore, the land belongs
by right to the whole community, is it not common jus-
tice for anyone who has the exclusive use of any piece to
pay to the rightful owners (the whole 00011110011}') a fair
rent for the use of the said land ?
“ The land-tax should not be an end, but a means to an
end.” Quite right. The land-tax should be a means to
an end, but it will be necessary to keep it in force till the
crack of doom, for, remove the tax, and the end or object
attained would immediately vanish. The primary and
whole object—the Alpha and Omega—of the land-taxis
the abolishment of land monopoly, which, in our opinion,
is at the root of most of the evils of our present-day social
system, and this, we believe, would consistently follow
the gradual increase of the land-tax, for, if all improve-
ments were exempted from taxation and bare land were
more heavily taxed, the speculative value would be a
thing of the past, and it would not pay a land-owner,
large or small, to keep land out of use and pay the tax
thereon ; he would therefore either cultivate the land him-
self, and thus employ labor, or he would sell it to others
and let them cultivate it.
We have often been told that bald statement is not
argument, and this we certainly consider applies to the
following statement in yours under review : “ The land-
tax is only a cumbrous, most difficult, and expensive
means of raising revenue,” and against this we emphati-
cally state, and will proceed to prove, that it is a simple,
easy, and cheap means of raising revenue compared with
the present complicated and many-armed method. The
idea in land-tax is ultimately to'abolish not only Customs
duties, but every other means of raising revenue both
local and colonial, which include customs duties, income-
tax, general city rates, charitable-aid rate, library rate,
all stamp duties, road board rates, water, harbor board,
etc., etc. —that is, simplify matters as much as possible ;
the struggle for existence is quite hard enough without
burdening a fellow with numerous unnecessary loads.
Although you take great exception to land-tax on
account of, as you say, the difficulty and expense of
raising it, you do not give us any idea of what you pro-
pose in place of it which will not be difficult and expen-
sive. So far as we can see, its greatest charm lies in its
great simplicity. Here in M.L. we have land-tax, income-
tax, and God knows what other taxes, and how the col-
lection of one of these taxes, not a new tax but simply
one out of the many now raised, is going to be more diffi-
cult and expensive than raising all the lot, is something
which we have yet to learn.
* • * »
We think you are very wide of the mark when you say
that the land-tax has no great advantage in the way of
furnishing work for the people, and you later on quite
contradict this hy saying that “ it may compel the big
landlord whose land is lying idle to hire a lot of men at
low (?) wages to cultivate it for 14 hours (?) a day.” As
the land-tax was gradually increased (at the rate of, say,
one penny in every five years until such time as the
revenue from this tax ;lone would cover the total ex-
penditure of the colony, both local and colonial), land-
-wners would either decide to cut up and sell their lands
or put on men for the purpose of cultivating the land and
increasing their income therefrom, the result in the
former case would be that land would come down in
price considerably— that is, it would lose its present in-
flated speculative value, and reach a fair usable value.
This would induce numerous employes to become owners,
and, in the latter case, the demand for labor would be so
great that it would be impossible to procure men to
“ work at low wages for 14 hours a day.”
One would imagine from your article that the farmers
are the only people who would pay land-tax, whereas it
is the city and suburban land-owners who would pay the
bulk of the tax. To prove this, take an example here in
Auckland (M.L.). : The land on which the Bank of New
South Wales stands in Queen-street was valued a few
months ago for taxation purposes; the assessment was
disputed and brought before the S.M. for his decision.
After hearing a large amount of expert evidence, he de
cided that the unimproved value of the block, which
measures 39ft. front to Queen-street by 94ft. deep, was
£6320. That is at the rate of £73,733 per acre, therefore
one acre in the main street of Auckland would pay as
much in taxation as a whole county away back—the
taxation, of course, being on value, not area.
Be your closing remark, “if coupled with Freetrade it
would be merely taxing our own potatoes while the
foreigners’ came in free, and it would tend to starve the
potato-grower out of employment.” Now, let us see if
this will stand the search-light. The land round Auck-
land, which produces most of the potatoes, is valued for
taxation at an average of, say, £l2 per acre—that is, un-
improved value. The land-tax on this, at, say, 4d. in the
£, would amount to 4s. per acre per annum. This land
grows on an average, say, eight tons potatoes per acre;
that means the farmer would pay, what you are pleased
to call an excise-duty of 6d. per ton, while the imported
potatoes would have to pay freight of from 10s. to 20s.
per ton. And why cannot our own potato-growers produce
tubers at the same low price as our neighbours?
* * * *
The regrettable feature of the above, and all
the other letters, is that so much space is
devoted to proving that this paper was wrong in
saying the thing it never said at all. The land
should be the property of the people; there-
fore, The Bulletin proposed that every man
who only owns his fair share should escape
taxation, and the man who owns more than his
share should be -so heavily taxed on the sur-
plus that it will pay him to cut up the surplus
and sell it to small owners. In this way the
small holding which is free of tax will have a
value ; the large estate won’t, till it is cut up
into small holdings. It never suggested, or
dreamed of suggesting, that the land-tax should
not be kept in force till the crack of doom, or
hinted at its removal. Of course, a land-tax
which was specially designed to crush out the
man who owns more than his share would yield
less and less revenue as that man was crushed
out, but no such idea as the removal of the
land-tax was mentioned. The Bulletin never
said that the raising of land-tax alone was
more expensive than the raising of a dozen
different taxes ; it simply said the land-tax was
an expensive mode of taxation (especially as
compared with customs duties), and it certainly
is. The perpetual cost of valuation and re-
valuation makes it so. It didn’t say that the
land-tax had ho great advantages in the way of
furnishing work ; it said that the land-tax,
after it had achieved its object in breaking up the
big estates, had no great advantage in this re-
spect.
♦ * * *
Also, by the way, the “city and suburban
landowners ” wouldn't pay the bulk of the tax,
because, despite the solitary example of the
B.N.S.W.’s Auckland block, they don’t repre-
sent half, nor a quarter, the land-values of the
country. (In Maoriland, according to the
Official Year-Book for ’9B, page 221, country
lands, <£58,000,000 ; town lands, £18,000,000
and the patronising single-tax gentleman never
took the trouble to learn this small fact !) And
if all the taxation was put on the unimproved
value of land, the Auckland spud-grower
would have to pay, not 4d., but fully Is. in the
<£—equal to 125., not 45., per acre. Maoriland
doesn’t average eight but tons of potatoes per
acre, while Australasia in general averages less
than four tons, making the excise duty 3s. in-
stead of 6d. per ton. And the local man has
also to pay freight, for potatoes don’t grow in
Queen-street, Auckland, or Princes-street, Dune-
din ; often, because railway carriage is dearer
than sea carriage, he has to pay nearly as much
freight as the importer.
m m m m
* But the important features of the case are
these :
(1) The unimproved value of all Maoriland lands (pri-
vate and state-owned, occupied and vacant), according to
the latest valuation, was barely £76,000,000.
(2) If land is to bear all taxation, local and general,
Maoriland has to raise at present about £4,500,000 per
annum, railway revenue supplying the balance. There-
fore the land has to yield nearly six per cent, on its capi-
tal value. The private landlord only averages about five
per cent, out of it, so there will be no interest left to him.
It amounts to an absolute seizure and confiscation. Thb
Bulletin isn’t raising the slightest objection to the con-
fiscation ; it is merely trying to reason the thing out.
(3) It is utter drivel to say tha.t such a tax would make
the owner of unimproved land “either cultivate the land
himself or sell it to others and let them cultivate it.” He
eouldn’t sell it. When money is worth 5 per cent a tax
equal to 6 per cent, per annum of the capital value means
putting a mortgage of £l2O on every £lOO worth of soil.
When a man has £lOO worth of land with £l2O mortgage
on it he can’t sell it. Also the only reason why he
must cultivate it is to escape having it forfeited for un-
paid taxes—in fact, to escape losing it. But when it
won’t fetch a cent, because the tax absorbs its full value,
why should the owner of unimproved land—the man who
has no valuable improvements but only the utterly un-
saleable land to lose —care a straw whether he loses it or
not? If he wanted land at any other time he could rent
land quite as cheaply as he could go on owning his own
land. In fact, he can rent land from the State at 5 per
cent, of its capital value, which would be cheaper than
possessing his own land at 6 per cent.
* * * •
(4) Therefore the first result would probably be not so
much a great increase of cultivation as a wholesale aban-
donment of unimproved land to the State. The State might
let it all in course of time for as much as the land-tax,
but it would take time. The owner who had valuable
buildings on his land would doubtless stay and pay the
tax rather than lose his buildings. But the one who had
only land would throw it up as often as not. And it
must be remembered that the estimate that a tax of 6
per cent, per annum on the value of all lands would cover
outgoings is based @n the assumption that every avail-
able acre in Maoriland went into use immediately.
Allowing for temporarily vacant land, uncleared land,
land that is inaccessible for want of roads, and bad debts
(it would be impossible to sell the land to recover
arrears, for it would have no selling value over and above
the tax) it might easily require a 7 per cent, tax to cover
expenses. And what is the advantage of piling an ex-
cessive rack-rent on the producer so that the town-trader,
importer, banker, publican, pawnbroker, &c., may go
free, and grog may be untaxed ?
• * • •
And then the old, never-answered question
crops up again : Seeing the people, by their
elected rulers, sold the land originally, and
took the money for it, and the Single-tax cult
proposes to confiscate that land again (for
putting on a tax which swallows up every
penny of the selling value and leaves only the
waste-paper title is confiscation, whatever it
may be called) is it proposed that the people


should hand back the purchase money ? And,
if so, how is it to be raised ? Having sold
their birthright and spent the proceeds,
and now realising that it was wrong to
sell their birthright, should they undo
the wrong by taking back the land and
keeping the proceeds—or how ? Is it proposed to
compensate the M.L. Government Insurance
Department and the A.M.P. Society and simi-
lar institutions for the destruction of their land
mortgages, or are they to write the loss off
their policies—say .£3O or .£4O off every £lOO
policy? What is it proposed to do with the
B. N.Z. and other banks and all the building
societies ? Where an owner has mortgaged his
house and land (there are about 60,000 mort-
gages current in Maoriland) is it simply pro-
posed, the selling value of the land being
destroyed, to let the mortgagee get as level as
he can by seizing the house, or will that part of
the mortgage referring to the land be cancelled ?
The Bulletin doesn’t profess to say that these
people should be compensated at the cost of
very many millions, or that they shouldn’t. It
simply asks, as a matter of languid curiosity—
what the Single-tax cult proposes to do ? And
it never gets an answer.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Premier Reid’s Land Bank.
New South Wales, if things go well, is at
last to have the beginnings of a State Land
Bank. The Retd Government proposes to bor-
row £500,000 at 3J per cent, and lend it out at
4 per cent., in sums not exceeding ,£200, to
certain classes of settlers — holders of con-
ditional purchases, conditional leases, settle-
ment leases, homestead leases, and homestead
selections. The idea is an excellent one in its
own small way, and, though it is proposed as a
mere temporary expedient—a kind of Rail-
Painting Job —there is reason to hope that the
proposed small experiment may be the begin-
ning of a large and permanent institution. At
the same time, there is room for a good deal of
hard thinking about the details. For leasehold
properties and only partially-paid-for selec-
tions are not the best of security, and there
is hardly enough margin for expenses and
none for bad debts in the 10s. per cent, differ-
ence between the borrowing and lending rate
of interest. There was a splendid opportunity
just here for a courageous financier to get out of
the old rut and make a new departure, if only
the right financier had been at the helm.
The £500,000 could be raised without borrow-
ing in the ordinary fashion, without paying 3J
per cent., with no special effort, and without
the least injury to anyone. The currency of
N.S. W. includes .£1,278,000 of paper-money
issued by the banks, and the province’s note-
issue never sinks anywhere near .£1,000,000.
That is to say, there is always a million of
paper in circulation for which the public doesn’t
want gold, and doesn’t ask it. There is abso-
lutely no reason, therefore, why N.S. W.
shouldn’t absorb .£500,000 of State legal-tender-
notes without the slightest difficulty. They
would only cost the State 2 per cent. ; that is
to say, they would probably reduce the note-
circulation of the private banks by <£500,000,
and, as the private banks pay 2 per cent, tax
on their note-issue, the money would cost the
State that amount. Under these circumstances,
it could be lent out at 3J per cent, instead of 4,
and still leave three times larger a margin for
expenses, bad debts, and for the formation of
a reserve with which to take up the notes some
day in the dim future, than Premier Reid’s
scheme does. So long as the State is willing to
take its own legal-tender notes in payment of
taxes, there is no possibility of them depreciat-
ing in value, even if the issue is a good deal
larger than .£500,000. At present the situation
is this : There are over £1,000,000 of notes cir-
culating in N.S. W. against which no gold re-
serve is required, for that amount of notes
keeps constantly in circulation. The banks get
the loan of this .£1,000,000 or more at 2 per
cent.—and the State might as well have the
loan itself, instead of borrowing at 3| per cent.,
and then it could start its cheap money scheme
on a cheaper basis than any other cheap money
scheme up to date. Premier Reid, with over
a million lying at his door and crying to be
taken in at 2 per cent., is throwing away a
great opportunity. He could lend money more
cheaply than he proposes doing, and yet make
his land-bank a sound business institution. As
things are it is likely to be partly business and
largely charity and advertisement, for with
only about £2500 a year to cover working ex-
penses, bad debts, interest on money during the
casual periods between the time it is borrowed
and the time it is lent out, or between the time
it is repaid and the time it is lent out again,
there is hardly a chance that the institution
can get on without large assistance from the
Treasury. Neither Maoriland, Victoria, nor
S.A. ever attempted to run their State land-
banks on so narrow a margin.

Policeman Dung'ey and His Mate Offer to
Compromise with the Law.
Last week’s record for the scandalous South
Melbourne Conspiracy Case is calculated to
shock even the hardest believers in the majesty
of Victoria’s criminal Law. On Monday, the
persistent lawyer (Dr. Evans) who desires to
drag policeman Dungey and his “ phizgig,”
Dora Bromley, to trial, sought a Supreme
Court warrant for their arrest. His applica-
tion to Justice Holroyd was based on the
palpable fact that magistrate Panton had put
obstacles in the way of bringing Dungey and
Bromley to book. Dr. Evans pointed out to
the Judge that Mr. Panton, P.M., had granted
four adjournments of the case for no conceivable
purpose, save the purposes of delay, vexation,
and expense to the prosecutor. Justice Hol-
royd said, in effect, that it wouldn’t be
respectable on his part to assume the possibility
of an attempt to burke justice on Mr. Panton’s
part, and he referred Dr. Evans back to the
police court for the coveted warrant. On
Wednesday the baffled barrister did go again
to the police-court, with the result that Mr.
r, 4NTON calmly adjourned the case for a further
fortnight, although the defendant side hadn’t
the impudence to ask for a longer adjournment
than 24 hours. Thereupon, on Friday, prose-
cutor’s grievance was brought back to the
Supreme Court, and the whole disgusting story
was related to Judge Hodges. Judge Hodges
heard how Judge Holroyd had refused to
believe Magistrate Panton capable of blocking
the case, and how Mr. Panton had subsequently
blocked it more determinedly than ever, but
nevertheless Judge Hodges was content to let
the scandal go on stinking. Said he—“ I
am not prepared to say the magistrate
was wrong in adjourning your application ;
although it certainly looks as if he is not
in a hurry. You want me to issue a
warrant while the matter is still proceeding
O'.e., being blocked.— Ed. B.] in the lower
court; I must refuse the application.” By this
time, though, the Crown was beginning to feel
that the game of bluff on behalf of Dungey
and his nasty accomplice could not be continued
for ever. It was necessary to make a show of
fair dealing lest the sluggish Melbourne public
should be moved to utter a howl of indignation.
So the solicitors for Dungey and Bromley pub-
lished a letter to the effect that Dungey and
Bromley will kindly “ undertake, on receipt
of one week’s written notice, to attend and
plead to any indictment that may be filed
against them at the forthcoming sittings of the
Criminal Court.” Thus the matter stands at
time of writing. Two alleged criminals, sum-
moned by the Grand Jury to stand their trial
for conspiracy, coolly state that they will obey
the summons if allowed to obey it in the par-
ticular way they have chosen. The majesty of
the Law is flouted, and the Crown looks on
with an approving eye.

A Bishop on Light Literature.
The R.C. Bishop of Sale writes a specious
letter to Melb. Argus in defence of his recent
outcry against light literature. He starts off
with the story of how Lady Burton destroyed
the valuable MSS. of her late husband’s great
work, “ The Scented Garden,” on the ground
that its publication would do more harm to the
prurient-minded multitude than good to the
noble cause of science—which story has nothing
to do with light literature, anyhow. And the
Bishop is evidently unaware that another trans-
lation of “ The Scented Garden ” was put on
the market within a year, and, thanks to the
gorgeous advt. given by Lady Burton, was sold
in hundreds at a very high figure to people who
might never have dreamt of purchasing if the
sensational story of the burnt MSS. had not
whetted their curiosity. Bishop Corbett,
who is evidently a novel-reader himself, goes
on to attack the simple Truth as it is told by
Thackeray. “There is not,” he says, “a
generous or unselfish character in Thackeray’s
novel who is not almost a simpleton, at least in
those things in which this generosity is dis-
played ” —poor Colonel Newcome ! “ The
direct tendency of the writings of Thackeray
is to create a painful scepticism, if not an utter
disbelief, in the existence of genuine virtue
among men. No man is more successful
in denouncing vice and laying bare its
turpitude. But while he does so he ex-
hibits it as universal, and by a natural
inference, unavoidable,” &c., &c. Thackeray
was worldly in his conception of his least
unvirtuous characters, because he stuck to
the Truth that was in him, but how does
Bishop Corbett’s estimation of those characters
pan out ? The ecclesiastical critic denounces
them as fools in their generous, unselfish ac-
tions. Thackeray’s idea of virtue in a man
is the Bishop’s idea of stupidity, so again the
question arises—what is virtue ? As for
Thackeray’s women, they, in many cases,
said their prayers every night and read their
Bibles regularly, so Bishop Corbett can’t be re-
ferring to them when he writes—“ The Catholic
faith teaches us that we are unable to form a
good thought, much less perform a meritorious
work, without the grace of God. What wonder
then that the heroes and heroines of fiction, writ-
ten by those who disbelieve all this, should be
represented as the sport of passion which they
are unable to resist, helpless victims of fate,
driven to their own ruin and degradation ? The
entire system of morality with these writers is
like an arch without a keystone ; the one great
essential is wanting, the Divine aid, and the
motives adequate to meet and control the in-
fluence of passion.” Novelists, it seems, are
necessarily disbelievers in “all this,” because
Bishop Corbett believes they are, or thinks he
believes so. Every novelist is driven to ruin
and degradation, like his heroes and heroines
before him, owing to his awful ignorance con-
cerning the origin of good thoughts. The
“ grace of God ” is a spiritual something that
can only be procured through the Divine aid of
churches, and the intellectual improvement of
the human race depends entirely upon profes-
sional religion, and Bishop Corbett knows all
about it.
Divorce in Maoriland.
The Queen’s tardy assent at last gives Maori-
land something dimly resembling a decent
Divorce Law. Under the new law either hus-
band or wife can get a divorce on the ground
of adultery ; formerly, the husband could get a
divorce for adultery, while the wife couldn’t.
Desertion for the ridiculously long space of five
years is also made a ground for divorce—the
more humane law in some provinces this side
makes the period three years. If the husband
has been a drunkard and has habitually been
cruel to, or neglected to support, his wife for
four years, or if the wife has been a drunkard
and frequently neglected to clean up the house
and empty the ash-bucket for the same period,
divorce may be granted ; and also if either
husband or wife has been sentenced to not less
than seven years for attempted murder of the
other. Even the greatly reformed new law
reckons that if the husband only gets five
years for the attempted butchery of his wife,
the sacred bond of matrimony must continue in
force. And it seems that a perfectly sober
husband who doesn’t commit adultery may
practise almost any kind of cruelty on his wife
for almost any period without the divorce law
taking any notice; drunkenness and cruelty
are recognised, but plain cruelty apparently
isn’t, and even the combined drunkenness and
cruelty must spread over four solid years of
battery before the statute will condescend to
cast its eye on the bruised applicant. Maori-
land has long possessed an inhuman and utterly
obsolete divorce law, and even its new law,
though an improvement on the old, is 10 years
behind the times.
Constructive Murder.
“ Danton ” to The Bulletin on “ Constructive
Murder ” :
It is infamous that technical murder, i.e., murder
which is only murder because of a legal fiction, should be
punished in the same way as actual murder.
The fact that a community tolerates the continuance of
such a juristic monstrosity is sufficient evidence of the
rawness of its conscience to explain its acceptance of the
much more subtle capital punishment fallacy.
Indeed, it may be seriously doubted whether the advo-
cates of the abolition of capital punishment will (until
they first stimulate the popular conscience to recognise
and remove the palpable wrong of hanging a man for
committing premeditated murder when he admittedly
doesn’t premeditate it) have any success in persuading it
to admit the less apparent wrong of removing from the
world a man who intentionally removes another man from
it.
Whenever a brutal murderer is to be brutally dealt
with by the State, a constant agitation proceeds with the
object "of saving the State from degrading itself. This is
quite proper. But it is not proper that a much more sus-
tained agitation is not kept up, that much more enthusi-
asm is not on tap, that much more potent methods of in-
fluencing public opinion and bringing the blush of shame
to the public cheek are not employed by the same agi-
tators when a constructive crime threatens a man with a
real scaffold. If we are - to have the hangman still, if we
can’t be happy without him, let us have a constructive
hangman for constructive murder.
In a country where the lonely hut, and the lonely swag-
man, and the lonely bank-manager, and the lonely woman,
and the lonely Indian hawker dot in extremely sporadic
fashion the vastnesses, it will be extremely difficult to
demonstrate the absurdity of jerking people to Mahommbd
for murder and rape. But surely the Australian con-
science can, in small doses, digest the fact that if a burg-
lar escaping out of a second-storey window drops on and
kills accidentally a man in the street, he hasn’t really
committed a premeditated murder at all. And so with
the average abortionist, who, above all things, and for his
own sake, desires that death should not result from his
treatment.
They appear to do these things better in England. For
as a result of the press and popular crusade against the
death sentence for constructive murder (as, for instance,
in the Lieut. Wark case), it may be said that no Home
Secretary who values the life of his cabinet will in the
future dare to carry out the sentence. The N.S.W.
Sheridan case smells very badly alongside the just cited
English Wark case. So much the worse for the quality
of the N.S.W. conscience.
Actual murder implies malice and a premeditation
which has to be strictly proved. So much is this the case,
that the most revolting homicides, if committed in the
heat of sudden passion, become manslaughter, for which
offence the convicted person escapps the death penalty.
Constructive murder, that is homicide committed unin-
tentionally while the accused is engaged in committing
certain crimes, by its very nature excludes the notion of
premeditation ; but the law, in one of its frequent bar-
baric freaks, presumes premeditation and inflicts a
penalty which it shrinks from inflicting in the most
brutal cases of intentional but sudden slaying.
The Yarra melo-tragedy affords an excellent oppor-
tunity, by reason of the almost absolute certainty of
final reprieve, and of the extraordinary public interest
in the case, of setting in motion such a crusade against
the continuance of the constructive murder theory in
Australian systems of criminal jurisprudence, that the
Capital Punishment Abolition people will be more than
failing in their duty if they don’t seize the occasion.

Bush. Sarcasm.
Dear Bulletin. —l have not the luck to be a
“ big ” draper, a flourishing florist, or the very
eminent manager of a very first-class hotel,
therefore, I don’t want to hang up my little
Sabbath advt. on the Federal capital. lam
merely a bush person with no starch in his
collar, but, all the same, I am deeply concerned
at the awful things that will happen to eminent
ice-cream, banana, and pea-nut merchants if
the Federal Government is not lodged in the
centre of Sydney, and I wish to offer a few
unselfish suggestions for “ keeping the money
in the country ” —that is in Sydney :
1. That., for the future, none of the roads-and-bridges
vote shall be expended in the country, except upon the
main roads to Sydney.
2. That all country residents with money to spend shall
be granted a free pass to Sydney.
3. That all selectors, farmers, shearers, miners, and
other bush “mugs” in Sydney who have “done in”
their cheques be given a railway-pass and 24 hours’ notice
to clear out until they have gathered more cheques to
spend—in Sydney.
4. That any wool-grower or farmer found guilty of
swindling Sydney brokers by sending his produce to any
other city be sent for six months to Darlinghurst—in
Sydney.
5. That a heavy poll-tax be placed upon all commercial
travellers, except those representing eminent firms—in
Sydney.
6. That country stores be supplied only with bare
rations and the year-before-lasl.’s “ shipwrecked bar-
gains” of the “big” drapers. This will encourage
“ shopping in Sydney.
7. That all newspapers be heavily taxed—except those
published in Sydney. This will put a stop to the unfair
competition of country rags that snatch subscribers from
Sydney.
8. That all civil and criminal trials, land-board meet-
ings, elections, and other public shivoos by which bush
hotel-keepers profit largely be held in Sydney.
9. That it shall be made a punishable offence for any
circus, theatrical troupe, steam round-about, fly-by-
night auctioneer, or any other spieler to travel the bush,
taking the cash that would be otherwise spent in Sydney.
10. That a special Act be passed changing the name of
New South Wales to Sydney. This would settle the row
about the Federal capital not being in Sydney, and
satisfy the “ Sunday Snoozer.”
I have travelled up and down the rivers eliciting
opinions upon the Federal capital, but am sorry to say
that the one clearly-expressed opinion I gathered was,
“To hell with Sydney ! ” This ought to come under the
Treason Felony Act. —C.C.
P.S. —The cheque I get for this will be spent at Phah-
lert’s —in Sydney.

Maoriland Supreme Court has decided that
the State’s right to seize the Midland Railway
holds good against the debenture-holders, so,
presumably, the case will now go ’Ome and
take refuge in the serene atmosphere of the
Privy Council. It has been a hideous confusion
from the beginning. The Act provided that
the Midland Co. should build the railway in a
certain time, and receive certain liberal land
grants as it went along ; if the railway wasn’t
built in time, the State could take possession of
as much of it as there was and cancel the agree-
ment after certain formalities. It also provided
that the co. could borrow on debentures which
would be a first charge on its (the co’s) property.
The line was seized, the co. having broken
all its conditions, but the debenture-holders
claimed it on the ground that they had a first
charge on the co.’s property. The M.L. Go-
vernment replied that they had—but that the
line wasn’t the co.’s property unless the co.
fulfilled the conditions attached to the owner-
ship. So far the Government has won, though
the local Tory party solemnly warns it that it
is doing awful damage to Maoriland’s credit
in Hingland. But as the new loan has hint.
floated at a higher price than either Victoria or
b.A. realised for the same class of security,
Maoriland in general reckons that its credit is
getting along well enough.

For February, 1899, Westralia lost 830 popula-
tion by excess of departures over arrivals. This
makes a loss of 6515, or about 4 per cent, of
the whole population, in five months. Westralia
is now the greatest gold-producing province in
the group—and the poorest. In the boom-days
the Forrest Govt, encouraged in every possible
way the selling of W.A. mines to English syn-
dicates—it called it “ introducing the blessings
of British capital.” Wlien a man found a big
gold-show he sold it in England, and then as
quickly as possible he left the dry, accursed
land with the proceeds. And by reason of- the
sale and the “introduction of British capital”
the metal that comes out of the mine goes to
England almost to the last copper. Hungry,
starving Westralia sees the procession of wealth
passing by, and is the biggest gold-producer in
Australasia, and has hardly a crust of its own.
The British capital for which it sold its mines
is gone, and the mines are gone, and W.A. is a
Gibeonite digging up its riches for other people.
The blessings of foreign capital always end like
that.
Mr. Colin M‘Kay Smith to The Bulletin :
Will you permit the “ litigious Colin M'Kay Smith ” a
few lines in reply to a paragraph which appeared in The
Bulletin of 18th ult. That the sale of Crown Lands is an
authorised act on the part of N.S.W. Government you will
not, of course, dispute. And were you to question the
propriety of the proceeds being appropriated in the lump
towards current revenue you would, I think, have a fairly
arguable case. But it is at the least a certainty that they
go into the public till of the colony, in other words, into
the “ Consolidated Revenue that they pro tanto lessen
the need to increase oiU-ide indebtedness; that they are
largely applied to make the land itself an accessible asset;
and that by lessening the need of alternative taxation
they indirectly increase the reserve of power which makes
for the security of the lender. Now, in the supposed case
of Federation we not only purpose inviting persons out-
side our boundaries to help us in directing the expendi-
ture of our moneys, but, save as regards such remnant as
may eventually fall to us after stoppintr leakages all over
island and continent, they do not come into ourtHl at all.
To me, at all events, the application of the term “ Con-
solidated Revenue of N.S. Wales” to moneys so collected
and expended appears extravagant, and I am unable to
see that it is greatly helped by your parallel case.
[Whereunto The Bulletin desires to re-
mark : (1) It does and always has questioned
the justice of using land-sale money as ordinary
revenue; (2) the land-sale money doesn’t lessen
the need to increase outside indebtedness.
There is no need at all to increase indebted-
ness—outside or inside. (2) The land-revenue
is not “ largely applied ” to make the land
accessible. The whole of the money spent
out of N.S.W. revenue last year on roads
(to make lands accessible) and on all other
public works was ,£597,000, and the
land - revenue was £1, 976,000. (4) When
N.S.W. issued the famous invitation to the
other provinces to come in and federate,
she was asking them to let people outside their
boundaries help them in directing the expendi-
ture of their money : in other words she asked
them to let her have in a hand in the spending
of their money ; (5) And there is nothing in the
contract about N.S.W. helping with her cash
to stop leakages all over Australia ; if the man-
ners of the anti-Bill crowd are any criterion,
N.S.W. is much more likely to get at some
other province’s cash and use it to stop her own
leakages.— Ed. B.j
A truly horrible story of cruelty to an adopted
child was lately told at Bulli (N.S. W.) court.
It included frequent beating, leaving the
youngster (aged less than three years) practi-
cally naked and unwashed for days, locking it
up in a dark, empty house for periods varying
from one to six hours, rubbing its face in filth,
driving a needle many times into its body,
leaving it covered with vermin, and other
horrors. The defendants, Elizabeth Capon
and her husband, made no defence. Man fined
<£lo or one month—he would have got anything
from three months to two years for stealing
20s. worth of goods. Alleged woman—the
member of the firm who drove the needle into
the child “ many times ” —fined £5, but no
alternative imprisonment, and, as there were
no goods to levy on, the fine remains unpaid,
so the she-horror practically escapes punish-
ment altogether. The Bulletin is firmly con-
vinced that the average Australian beak has an
innate sympathy for cruelty; his invariable
anxiety to let off the most revolting kind of
ruffian with merely nominal punishment can’t
be accounted for on any other grounds.
