Anti-Bill.
The Sydney Anti-Billious push gathered together
on Monday evening at the Australia, when G.
Republican Dibbs took the chair and talked un-
utterable rot to a few wealthy traders. The old
man has a vast capacity for drivel and hysterics,
and he got off some very fine samples :
The meeting had been called to enable the citizens of
Sydney to exercise their undoubted rights as freemen, in
reference to this Bill which had been rushed through
Parliament during the last few days. (Cheers). He never
knew a more important matter dealt with in such a short
space of time. It appeared as though the Premier labored
under a certain sense of pressure, because he knew that
a movement was being inaugurated in Sydney for the
purpose of petitioning both Houses of Parliament with
regard to that particular Bill, which settled our hash as
an individual colony to all eternity, and brought about a
union which could not be dissolved without the shedding
of blood. It became a question whether they were to be
disposed of like so many sheep sent to market. The idea
of sitting up all night, then breaking off until 10 o'clock
in the morning, and then sitting all night again to dispose
of our destinies, and that there should be no criticism on
the part of the public, was to his mind contrary to all
principles of right. The object of this meeting was to
see whether the citizens of Sydney were willing to sit
under this yoke which had been forged for them, or
whether they were going to open their mouths and, in-
cidentally, their pockets, to fight. (Loud cheers). . . .
In such an important matter involving everything one
held dear, it was outrageous to whip the thing off as
though one were putting on a halfpenny tax. Occasions
of that sort were such as justified rushing things through
in order to get on with more important business. But on
this all-important question of Federation, we were quietly
told that we were to have no voice in the matter.
(Cheers.) He had yet to learn that in June last the voice
of the people was not given against the Bill, and that the
proposals of the conference of last year were not disposed
of by the public will. (Cheers.) Yet this Bill was now to
be crammed down their throats, because their representa-
tive who had been sent to Melbourne to confer with the
other Premiers, and had been backed up by
both Houses of Parliament, had, to use a well-
known phrase, been “ nobbled.” (Loud cheers.) ....
He did not try to disguise the fact that he was speaking
as a New South Welshman. The whole difficulty in the
way of Federation for the last eight or nine years had
been a blot of this kind, and the different colonies had
been hanging back until some big colony could be found
to pay the piper, or until they could catch us asleep and
nobble us. Now, as to the capital. People who would
not accept everything that Vieroria laid down were called
provincialists. We were expected to bow down to the
feet of our almost youngest child, or have no Federation
at all. What this colony had not realised yet was that
one colony had all along been struggling and fighting
and grasping for supremacy over all the others. . . .
Sydney was the centre of Australia, and, presuming that
Queensland came in, Sydney was about the centre of
Australia. (Cheers.) New South Wales had been given
greater advantages and greater resources than all the
other colonies put together, and Nature had endowed
Sydney with similar gifts. . . . The people would refuse
to have this Braddon blot. (Cheers.) They would refuse
to have the rivers taken from their control, and they
would refuse to have the capital disposed of by people
whose interests it was to fix it 100 miles from Sydney.
The whole force of their strength must now be devoted
to strengthen the hands of the Legislative Council.
(Cheers.) What remained of the old anti-bill committee
of June last had been at work within the last 10
days or so,.and they had at the present moment over
10,000 signatures to a petition against the Bill. . . .
They settled the affairs of the nation offhand ; then came
back, and rushed the Bill through Parliament in the
middle of the night. Because Mr. Reid was the dictator
for the time being, he thought he had got the mob in the
hollow of his hand. He would realise that the thinking
portion of the community would not have this Bill. The
only and obvious way of dealing with the other colonies
was to say—“ Here is our plan of Federation ; come in
with us if you like on equal terms, but we are not going
to give up to you everything with which God has endowed
us.”
Poor old bungling, cursing Dibbs must surely be
ashamed of his own yell when he sees it in print.
Think of any politician being fool enough to rave
about “ being told we are to have no voice in the
matter,” when the whole question of the accept-
ance or rejection of the Federal Constitution is left
to be decided by the voice of the people ! And
the announcement that the little crowd of impor-
ters protest “as freemen” against a Bill which
simply refers the whole question to the decision of
the free men of N.S.W. ! Also, the shriek about
“the yoke” and about the outrageousness of
“whipping thethingthrough ’’ —the only thing that
was whipped through being a Bill declaring
that the whole question should be left to the
popular decision! Dibbs’s tremendous hysterics
about the way the Bill was “ rushed through ”
the Bill which refers the matter to the people and
to the people alone to say yes or no upon—would
almost suggest that Dibbs thinks it a very dan-
gerous matter to trust the people. Also, the long
ex-Republican lays down these three points : (1)
That last June, when the Anti-Billious crowd was
licked by 5367 votes, the said Anti-Billious crowd
was victorious; (2) that the Anti - Billious
crowd will be victorious again at the next
referendum ; (3) That the conduct of Reid
and Barton in rushing through with the
Bill providing for the holding of that re-
ferendum at which the Anti - Billious crowd is
going to be victorious is a shameful injury to the
Anti-Billious crowd. And the business of the
Ahti-Billious crowd now is to strengthen the hands
of the Council in dealing with the Enabling Bill,
so as to undo the mischief done in the Assembly,
and get the Bill chucked out, in order that the
referendum at which the Anti-Billious crowd is
going to be victorious won’t come off at all. There
is something very like abject cowardice in the
Anti-Billious attitude. A party which alleges
that the are with it, and yet fills the air
with entreaties that the matter won’t be referred
to the people at all, looks a pretty badly scared
crowd. Its obvious motto is that he who runs
away without fighting may live to bolt another
day. The Bill party is willing to leave the matter
to the straight-out decision of the people. It
doesn’t want to load the dice like any low-down
political spieler by asking that the Anti-Billious
crowd must show 80,000 votes against the measure
to ensure its rejection. It doesn’t ask that Parlia-
ment. shall accept the Federal Constitution
without letting the people have a say in the
matter, though the other side asks Parliament to
reject it without letting the people have a say. It
simply asks for a popular vote, the majority to
decide. If there is a spark of democratic senti-
ment, or pluck, or faith in its own vapoprings
about the Dibbs push it should be able to do
likewise.

The lacerated tale of “ East Lynne ” fills
in the latter part of the MacMahon Brothers’
season at Sydney Lyceum. It is a new version of
the old fragment—the 125th new version or there-
abouts ; and there are suspicions that it is even
sadder than some of the old versions. In some
previous renderings it was quite a common thing
for the story to include Carlyle’s early life, and his
marriage, and a few scenes dealing with the way
in which the young wife was cruelly trodden on by
Aunt Cornelia, and as a result the wife’s elope-
ment with Levison didn’t arrive till well on in the
play. But in the new Lyceum version the whole
married life of the Carlyles, and the cloud that
came over it, and the busting-up of the ’appy ’ome
are all crowded into one act. This leaves two
solid hours of woe—two long, moist hours during
which Lady Isabel fades slowly amid a driving
rain of tears. There are glimpses of cheap lodgings
in different places, with the broken-hearted female
slowly perishing on a brown carpet, or dropping
her broken-hearted head on a brown tablecloth, or
gazing with a vacant brown eye at the cheap orna-
ments on the mantelpiece. Also, she drifts, in-
sensible with grief, through some of the finest
scenery in Europe, and she and Little Willie die
at great length under circumstances which would
fill a jug with tears. The most frivolous item on
the programme is the great railway accident
wherein the moaning lady is dropped upon by a
hot locomotive, and nearly comes to the end of
her troubles. The Lyceum co. tackled “East
Lynne” on Saturday in a rather disjointed
spirit. It is a very good but very worn-
out co., which never gets its proper sleep
through having to get up a big new drama every
Saturday, and on the first night a good deal of it
didn’t know its part a bit too well. Miss Ethel
Grey, as a crisp and decisive Aunt Cornelia, gave
it a great lift, but until her appearance the drama
was creeping along in the gutter with its tail down,
and looked like utter failure. If Aunt Cornelia
hadn’t taken hold of it anything dreadful might
have happened. Miss Burdette, Diver, and Bert
Bailey in an extraordinary wig, began to get the
hang of the story after a while, and did some good
work ; and the latter gentleman was finally sen-
tenced to imprisonment for life at the same faded
street corner which has done duty in almost every
Lyceum drama for as long as anybody can remem-
ber. The rest of the people got through somehow,
the veteran J. A. South, whose share of the
trouble was happily ended in the prologue by
death, being one of the best joints in a very long
tail.
* * * *
The great benefit show tendered to the
MacMahon Brothers a few weeks ago is being
kept fresh in the public mind by sundry proceed-
ings on the part of somebody who wants to
garnishee the takings. Also, somebody else has
been suing for unpaid wages, and the latter days of
the old Lyceum management are extensively adorned
by blue paper and other ham-frills and complica-
tions. The Saturdaynight gods, who have always
regarded the MacMahon firm as a personal friend,
grow even more riotous than usual in these latter
days, and, when “East Lynne’’was going very
slow on the opening night, their advice to the
company to take somebody’s Bile Cauliflower for
that tired feeling grew almost deafening. Even
the gods begin to make disparaging remarks now
about the huge boot advertisement on the curtain,
and, if new manager Holloway can see his way to
remove that painted lamentation, this petitioner
will ever pray, &c., &c. The MacMahons start a
country tour by opening: at Newcastle at Easter,
and the 51 pieces which they have produced
during their 20-months management at the
Lyceum will come in handy to astonish the bush
agriculturist with.
** * #
The “ Geisha ” boom is almost as strong
now as it was on the first nights of that little Jap
row, and as patient investigation has revealed
absolutely nothing in the Japanese shindy itself to
account for the extraordinary violence of the out-
break, it is set down once more to the splendid
staging, and the costumes, and the multitude of
agreeable girls, and the doings of Chinaman Wun.
There are people in Sydney—sober, dull, fat,
respectable people who wear the bell-topper of a
spotless career, and put on a clean collar daily,
and strongly disapprove of Federation, and hold
religious and moral conversation over the huge,
blazing-hot mass of beef at dinner —who have been
to see the “Geisha” as often as six times and
are still going. The Bulletin knows lots of such
people and seldom gets in sight of a corner with-
out seeing the front waistcoat button of one or
other of them coming round it. For some in-
scrutable reason, the “ Geisha ” has swept the fat,
serious person who eats tophets of hot beef all
through the summer, right off his two brief legs,
and a play that can do that has a future before it.
There is no knowing in advance what will strike
this large, solid, moneyed class. It doesn’t go by
any fixed rules and regulations. But when there
is a sound of hard-breathing on the theatre stairs,
and the kind of people who are much troubled with
swollen feet begin to muster strongly in the vesti-
bule, it is a good sign.
The “ Shadows of a Great City ” are to be
revived at Sydney Royal on Saturday. The great
city in this case is New York, and the “shadows ”
spread over four huge acts and many crimes.
Charles Holloway’s stay at the Royal is near its
end, and apparently that wily manager is saving
up his new dramas to make a splash with, when
he opens the Lyceum at Easter. Anyhow, the
“Shadows ” ought to be good enough to keep the
Castlereagh-street house reasonably full for the
remainder of the Holloway season there. It is a
strident play wherein Sin has a terribly bad time
in the end, and is put in gaol, or disembowelled,
or falls over a piece of fluff and breaks his neck, or
something.
*# * *
Dante, the illusionist, returns to Sydney
Palace on Saturday for a short farewell season,
during which the six live ducks will again emerge
from the burnt hat, and the spectral lunch will be
found in the drain-pipe as aforetime. Dante, as a
professional deceiver and a man who can produce
pianos and omnibuses from his tail-pocket, needs
no recommendation. Sydney still remembers him
as the best prevaricator-by-hand who has come its
way for many years.
** * *
“ The White Heather,” in its fourth week
at Melb. Royal, seems to crowd the house more
than ever, so if Bland Holt was intending to close
his season at Easter lie has probably changed his
mind. Melbourne likes “The White Heather”
for several reasons, but particularly because it is
the best-dressed drama that the city has seen and
the least vainglorious on the subject. The adver-
tisement thus far has left the costumes to tell their
own tale, with the result that the local female sex
is much more inclined to enthuse over the trim-
mings and things than if the management had
made them a leading feature on the bill. The
present writer, who has merely a man’s taste in
millinery, owns up to a rather hysterical affection
for the clothes in Blandolt’s show. You get them
in so many different styles and splendors—from
the airy nothing of Lady Godiva’s riding-habit to
the rich Elizabethan architecture of Mrs. Holt’s
gown in the last act —and the total effect is con-
vincing. You feel that although Miss Ross may
not be a real Lady Hermione she is superior to the
average earl’s daughter as regards what she stands
up in, and would pawn for a larger amount.
Beauteous Miss R. is a trifle less stagey than she
was. Also, the rest of the performance moves
faster, and the behaviour of the scenery, under
trying conditions, is almost everything that could
be desired.
* a» * *
The idyllic little comedy with the Urchin
in it didn’t flourish exceedingly at Melbourne
Princess’s, and the broad, effective patch upon
that small boy’s pants has doubtless disappeared
ere now. Anyway, “One Summer’s Day” was
announced to be withdrawn after Tuesday, in
which case a revival of “Lady Ursula” is now
taking place. “ One Summer’s Day ” was a comedy
that seemed fresher and brighter the more one
looked at it, but the average person doesn’t go a
second and third time to give a fragile entertain-
ment a chance to grow upon him. He goes only once
and doesn’t think much of a tale that consists
largely of a boy swilling.five bottles of ginger-beer
behind the scenes. Neither does he perceive all
the dramatic consequences of Miss Noble forget-
ting to put the salt in the picnic-basket. “One
Summer’s Day ” is a comedy of important trifles
and fleeting matters for reflection during the
intervals, which intervals might as well be cut
shorter all the same. Next Saturday the variable
Brough will stage a quite new and recent farcical
comedy—“ The Brixton Burglary.”
** * *
The ground landlord of Melb. Opera House
has finally closed with Rickards’s terms for a
lease, and a new Opera House (or Tivoli, as it is
more likely to be called) will come to pass within
the next 12 months—a beautiful structure, with a
dozen luxurious private-boxes, and a refreshment-
bar on each of the three floors. So that’s all right.
** * *
Lalla Miranda is engaged for the approach-
ing grand opera season at La Monnaie theatre,
Brussels, and may be expected to make her mark
in Paris next year. Lalla Miranda, after all these
years of studying and waiting, is not older than
Melba was when she landed in Europe.
At Poverty Point.
A western squatter was consuming the harmless B. and
S. in a very large Sydney hotel when a well-known
theatrical manager wandered in. His glossy topper and
long frock-coat arrested the eye of the jumbuck man.
“ What in the name of goodness is that t” he asked. The
barman looked where the unsteady finger pointed.
“That’s Mr. O’Boy,” he said, in respectful tones.
“Theatrical manager, you know. Great man!” “Is
he?” said the pastoralist, with his fascinated eye still
fixed on the top-hat. “ Great heavens ! What a lovely
scarf-pin he’d make !! ”
With regard to the death of Mrs. Hudson, wife
of the well-known entrepreneur, Indian Daily News
writes“ The cause of death was the nervous shock
which Mrs. Hudson experienced when her little boy met
with the terrible accident which has already been re-
ported. The poor little child’s skull was split right open,
and the brain laid bare. As regards the dates of these
occurrences and the incidence of the fateful number 13,
the circumstances are almost without a parallel. When
Mr. Hudson sailed from Oolombo in the spring to go to
London in search of a company, the date was the 13th
May. He arrived in London 31st May, the same combina-
tion of figures inverted. On 13th June, just 13 days after
Mr. Hudson arrived in' England, the accident happened
to his little son. The policeman who picked him up from
the pavement was No. 13 of his division ; the cab in which
Mr. Hudson conveyed the child to the hospital was No.
13. The child was blind for 13 days after his accident.
On returning to Australia with his wife and children,
Mr. Hudson, who had written for rooms in an hotel in
Adelaide, found that No. 13 had been reserved for him.
When leaving for India for his present tour, the ship
sailed on the 13th Oct. On arriving at Colombo, Mr.
Hudson changed into the s.s. Yaletta to come on to Cal-
cutta. He had engaged berth No. 12 for himself; curi-
ously enough, however, this berth was swamped by a sea
that came aboard through the open port, and Mr. Hudson
had, in consequence, to sleep in berth No. 13. The news
of Mrs. Hudson’s death was brought him by a wire ar-
riving here on 13th Nov. Mr. Hudson’s birthday is the
13th Dec.”
“ S.S.” : Sat in a Melb. theatre, t’other night, watching
the posturings, oglings, and little-woolly-lamb methods
of an imported actress on the stage, and the expressions
of two Australian actresses in the audience. The latter
artistes are rarely seen behind the footlights, but both
are capable women who would have put intelligent work
into the character the squeaking doll was mishandling on
the boards. Left the theatre devoutly wishing Australia
could be cut off from everywhere for a few years, and left
entirely on her artistic own for good, bad, or indifferent.
A light of other days, actor Arthur Sterling, died re-
cently in England. His widow is the Miss Cleveland (of
Melbourne Royal in the sixties) whose first husband,
Henry Vincent, died at Filzroy through an accident.
Sterling, who left over £15,000 to his widow, ordered his
remains to be cremated.
Era (Lend.) Almanac has a yarn by Nellie Stewart re
a Hawkesbury River flood, which stuck-up the opera co.
“of which 1 happened to be prima-donna,” says Nellie,
at Maitland. The yarn goes on to say that a young man
at the hotel had quarrelled with his best girl, who lived
at a farm-house a mile or so away. The young man was
very drunk when Nellie and co. arrived at the hotel, but
he sobered up, three days later, in time to see his girl’s
residence getting swallowed up by the rising waters. So
he swam out to the rescue, across a mile or so of raging
torrent, and in due course his drowned body was found
wrapped in the arms of his dear damp girl, who was
likewise very dead. “He reached the goal he fought
for,” says authoress Nellie.
About naming any one particular note in music, a com-
petent correspondent writes : “I remember often hearing
this done by a sister of Fred Ralfe, the tenor, who un-
hesitatingly and unerringly named any note struck. I
have heard the ‘ Infant Mozart ’ do it, but the young lady
(a clever pianiste) was much readier.”
One to the credit of Wilson Barrett. A good while ago
he concluded his sojourn at London Princess’s owing
£40,000. He scorned bankruptcy, and faced the task of
paying it off. Now he is free of debt and making a for-
tune.

sang to the Queen, knowing that the latter was rather
deaf, the Australian contralto kept one eye on the music
and the other on the ‘ widow of Windsor.’ She was
much amused to hear the Queen ask Princess Beatrice :
‘ A beautiful voice. Who is she ? Ada Crossley' ; I
never heard the name before. She sings like an artist.
Comes from Australia, does she ?’ All these apparently
sotto voce remarks and the replies were distinctly audible
on the platform.”

At the pionic down Hobson’s Bay given t’other day to
the ancient buskers now retiring in the friendly
shadow of the Theatrical Benevolent Association, one old
battler produced a pla.y-bill of a performance at Hobart
in 1838. The players’ names are probably fictitious—but
writer has a list of theatricals of these times, and finds
one recorded in this old bill. The play was “ Billie
Taylor,” and the performance took place on the last day
of the New Town races. It is likely that Sir John Frank-
lin was present, with Major Domville and the officers of
the garrison. The supers in those times were usually
soldiers (by gracious permission, &c.), who received half-
a-crown each, or three glasses of rum or a quart of
Walker’s beer. ’Tis a far cry !
Miss Julia Franks (nde Loel), a Melbourne girl, who
recently won the Sainton-Dolby prize for singing at the
Roy'al Academy of Music, London, has made a successful
professional debut with the Esty-Marsh National Grand
Opera Co. in Dublin. Yet another tuneful Jewess!
Some of the ’Murkan papers that are doing their best
to hook a local heiress (Miss May Goelet) for the Earl of
Shaftesbury', credit the pugnacious-looking tenor with an
“ engagement” to a certain deceased actress well-known
in Australia. It was not a regular, but only an occa-
sional, engagement, so far as is known.
Tenor Newbury is most enthusiastic in his talk of the
London prospects of alto Rodda Perry, who is to accom-
pany him to England. Much depends, of course, upon
the permanency of Perry’s vocal equipment, but there is
always a fine opening for male altos and sopranos in the
London R.C. churches. Card. Vaughan concurs with his
predecessor in banning female choristers. The most
marvellous boys’ voices are those gathered under Meyer
Lutz, at St. George’s Cathedral, Southwark, to which the
outside denominations flock like bees whenever the
“ Stabat Mater ” or Mozart’s “ Twelfth ” is produced.
Melba to the interviewer : “ Even as a child of three or
four I was so passionately devoted to music that I re-
member frequently crawling under the piano and remain-
ing quiet there for hours while listening to my mother’s
playing. Yes, my mother sang also, though she had not
a particularly notable voice. But her sister, my ‘ Aunt
Lizzie,’ as I called her, possessed a soprano voice of extra-
ordinary beauty and quality. To this day I can remem-
ber my aunt’s absolute control of her voice, and the beauty
and ease of her execution even in the highest pianissimo
passages. Indeed, I feel sure my Aunt Lizzie would have
enjoyed a brilliant career as a public singer had she
adopted it.”
Sydney Telegraph heads its report of the
doings of the crowd which wants N.S.W. Leg.
Council to reject the Federal Enabling Bill and
take the decision out of the hands of the people,
“The Federal Counter Movement.” Meaning,
evidently, the Anti-Federal Shop-Counter Move-
ment, or else the Provincial Counter-Jumpers’
Movement. Ex-Republican Dibbs was dug out
of his grave to sit in the chair and, says the Tele-
graph, “with him were such men as ”by this
time the reader is worked up into a fearful state of
excitement, for it seems that all the intellect of the
province is to follow, and he takes hold of some-
thing lest the flood of brains should knock him
over. And then it turns out that the men are
Slattery, draper Riley, Alderman Dymock, George
Pile, the mild old gentleman with the dog who
writes letters to the papers, and a few other small
has-beens—the sort of people who go to nearly
every funeral, and have their names in the
papers afterwards. The dull obscurity of the
crowd stands out all the more conspicuously when
the Telegraph assures the public that it is such
men as these who make up the extinct Dibbs’s
following. They described themselves in their
own circular as a “preliminary meeting of com-
mercial and business men and shopkeepers,” and
S.M. Herald gets home on them by quoting their
own description of themselves. It was the up-
heaval of the shopkeepers—the insurrection of the
beautifully hair-oiled man with the invisible-soap
who tells you the way to the remnant-counter and
the ribbon department.

lately made for the Ballarat Woollen Mills by the Austral

formally started on their useful career by the Hon. Com-
missioner of Customs and christened “ The Progress ”
The engines are fitted with a modified Lulzer tvpe of
valve gear, and indicate 700 horse-power, the fly-wheel
weighs 16 tons, and the total w'eight of the engine is up-
wards of 120 tons. They are now working in a most satis-
factory manner. The Otis Co. deserves every credit for
turning out such a fine sample of the modern steam-
engine.*

During February, N.S.W. spent <£163,407 of
loan money—£2l,6s6 more than during February, 1898.
For the eight months of the financial year which ended
on 28th February, 1899, N.S.W. spent £1,311,221 of loan
money, or at the rate of about £2,000,000 a year—which
is £188,033 more than the loan expenditure for the cor-
responding eight months of last year. And the ordinary
expenditure is at present £418,626 ahead of revenue, so
there is a pretty large deficit to overtake in the remaining
four months of the financial year.
Victoria publishes no monthly expenditure ac-
counts, but its loan expenditure is almost nil. The re-
venue boom this month is mostly in the railways, which
supplied £64,522 more cash than in February, 1898.
Queensland’s revenue is, so far, £398,108 more
than the expenditure. The loan moneys are going out
gaih T , £701,953 having been spent during the past eight
months, which is £64,651 more than the loan expenditure
for the corresponding period of last year. The blessed
revival is mostly built on loans.
Westralia’s expenditure for February is not yet
announced, but for the seven months to 31st January,
1898, the outgoings were £137,141 greater than the re-
ceipts, and the accumulated deficit was £323,844.
* * * m
Since Maoriland led the way by floating the
first Australasian 3 per cent, loan in 1895, the Seven Pro-
vinces have fourteen times appealed to J. Bull with 3 per
cent, loans—five of them from Westralia, three from S.A.,
two from N.S.W., two from Maoriland, one from Queens-
land, and one from Victoria. There were only three
failures among them—very bad failures, too—arid they
were all Westralian loans. In ’96 Westralia floated a 3
per cent, loan at the best price realised up to date, and
in ’9B it floated another at the worst price recorded so
far. Maoriland’s recent loan, by the way, was mostly for
the purposes of the State Land Bank. The whole of the
£1,500,000 originally raised has been lent out, and there
are no losses up to date. Even the most violent of Maori-
land Tories have dropped their prophecies about the “in-
evitable failure ” of the State Land Bank.
* * * *
The Victorian Government has put a little
3 per cent. loan of £250,000 on sale at the Treasury in
sums of £5 and upwards to suit small investors, and the
result is awaited with interest. Three per cent, isn’t so
much as it was a few months ago, for rates have risen a
good deal in the interim, and Australia, whose methods
of finance are somewhat primitive, doesn’t understand
the off-hand methods of raising money practised in other
British-speaking countries. In the U.S., every bank is
compelled to hold £lOO in State securities for every £9O
worth of notes issued, which makes a pretty good'com-
pulsory market for State bonds, and the State issues notes
of its own in addition. Canada makes its banks hold
40 per cent, of their cash reserves in State legal tender
notes. In India the State issues legal tender notes, the
amount regularly afloat being about £16,000,000, and
Ceylon does likewise. The time will probably come when
Australia will also adopt some of these devices, but at
present it merely offers its little loan to the public in a
respectful tone of voice and hopes for the best.

The general result is better, yet three out of the seven pro-
vinces show a decrease. Last year N.S.W., for the first
time, came within an ace of overtaking Victoria, and was
a very good second on the list. This time, though the
harvest is not very much worse than last year, she is down
fourth again. During the year the W.A. Government has
made stupendous efforts in its own rather clumsy way to
“settle the people on the land,” but though W.A. has a
good extent of well-watered country in the south-west,
and practically the same population as Tasmania, it does
not yet seem able to get within gun-shot of the little
island in an agricultural sense.

The Colonial Bank (Melb.) announces its in-
tention of holding its long-postponed cleaning up. It is
proposed to write off £5 10s. per share (£425,029), which
will leave the ordinary capital, according to last balance-
sheet, at about £60,000 and the preference capital at
£'304,044.
* * * *
Reported that a new British industrial life
assurance office, to be called the National Unity, is likely
to take over the dreadful Provident and Industrial In-
surance 00. of Maoriland. Tim Bulletin can only say
that it would be unfeignedly sorry for any co. or any man
who took the Provident and Industrial at a gift; it would
be sorry even for a dog that took it, unless it had very
strong reason to dislike the dog. Last year, by its own
figures, the Provident and Industrial spent £1694 more
than its earnings ; previous year about £460 more. At
the end of ’9B it partially disguised the awful-
ness of things by putting its office into the
balance-sheet as worth £4508 where it had previously
stood at £1268. And even after thus creating a paper
asset of £3240 it could only show an assurance fund of
£BB5 clear, after eight years’ work. And the amounts
which it covenanted to pay under its policies in Maoriland
alone were about £185,000. The co;’s founder and man-
ager, Kirkcaldy, the gentleman in the short coat and the
very tall hat, whose Equitable Fire Insurance Co. is still
a solemn memory on the other side, died recently, and
his co. would certainly be well-advised to follow him. But
if the National Unity or any other guileless young concern
proposes taking it over, there should be pleasant times
ahead for somebody.
* * * *
In reference to a par. in the “ Wild Cat ” column
last week, the Citizens’ Life Assurance Co. desires to state
that when it pulled down its old office in Castlereagh-
street, Sydney, it wrote off the whole value of the old
building, and that the increase of £58,000 under the
heading of “freehold property” in the balance-sheet is
represented by new property purchased and put in at
cost price.
* * * •
The Victoria Insurance Co. publishes a half-
year’s interim report to 31st Dec., 1898, showing that, so
far, the receipts have been £5240 more than expenditure.
If things keep on at this rate the Victoria will do much
better this year than last (last year’s loss, £23,336, and
dividend suspended). It doesn’t look well, all the same,
for any more of the tremendous 35 and 40 per cent, divi-
dends that the Victoria used to pay .so recklessly, and
which did so much to pull down its ancient glory. The
Victoria’s small offshoot, the Victoria General Insurance
and Guarantee Co., is £1863 to the good on its half-year’s
work (last time it showed a loss of £5Oll for the year and
paid £2OOO away in a 10 per cent, dividend in addition).
If it keeps on at this rate, and isn’t struck by any more
large disasters, a 10 per cent, dividend should be fairly
earned by next June.

The N.S.W. Mont de Piete (that institution
which Sydney people generally describe as the Monty
Peet) reports a profit for the half-year ended 31st Dec.,
’9B, of £3589, which compares with some previous profits
as follows :
June, 1894.. ..£4314 December, 1896 ..£4284
December, 1894 .. 4470 June, 1897 .. .. 4225
June, 1895 .. .. 4239 December, 1897 .. 4297
December, 1895 .. 4765 June, 1898 .. .. 4189
June, 1896 .. .. 4699 December, 1898 .. 3589
The usual 10 per cent, dividend absorbs £3689, so for the
first time in many years the “ Monty Peet ” hasn’t quite
earned its div., and the profit and loss balance is reduced
from £B5O to £750. The falling-off is ascribed to the fact
that a considerable part of the earnings were set aside to
write down doubtful debts. Also, owing to further pro-
vision for depreciation of real estate, the balance at credit
of contingency reserve has been reduced from £4706 to
£1965. Altogether, it is the worst half-year the Mont de
Pi6t<s has had for a very long time, though the profits
have shown something of a downward tendency ever since
Dec., ’95.
* * * *
Victoria Coffee Palace Co. (Melb.) reports a loss
of £284 for 1898, the result being set down to an unusual
outlay of £lO9B for new sewerage connections. In 1897
it made a profit of £69o—equal to £3 9s. per cent, per
annum on the paid-up capital of £20,000. Its last srleam
of prosperity was when it paid a 4 per cent. div. in ’92.
The beginnings of a new prosperity are clearly visible
round the public-houses of Melbourne, but the tide rises
very slowly in the place where strictly moral bever-
ages are dispensed. The Melbourne men who are begin-
ning to re-appear at their old haunts have a good many
solemn recollections to drown, and the skeletons only
float in coffee.

Ed. Bulletin,—Your “Cobar Note” man is
most unjust to the Chesney 00. Says nothing is being
done at the mine, and talks about its “ limited calling
power.” Now, the wages-sheet is £5OO a month, and
with “ a good manager ” there must surely be something
doing at the mine. Also the Chesney’s calling-power is
£24,000, to say nothing of 20,000 reserved shares. I have
good grounds for believing that arrangements are in pro-
gress for putting the mine on a producing basis without
many further calls. The Chesney is so good-looking a
mine, and so much money is being spent on it, that it
surely deserves to have the exact truth told about it.—S..
Melb. ’

White Cliffs opal-field is in the midst of about
the most drought-distressed section of far-western N.S.W.
Itself desperately hard-up for water—XOOgals. of slimy
liquid costs up to 10s.—the tracks to it are practically
impassable save by camel-teams.
* * • •
B.H. South took nearly 10 years to get into
clover, but seems to have struck a fair patch of it now
The mine was opened in ’B7, and the co. fooled itself into
erecting smelting furnaces three years later ; was in
trouble up to the neck in ’9l ; talked about liquidation in
93 ; accepted metallurgist Greenway’s advice and put up
a concentrating-plant at the end of that year ; began to
make microscopic profits in ’94 ; saw them swamped by
the cave-in underground in ’95 ; and was only able to pay
a first sixpenny div. in ’97. The ore became harder and
harder ; but, as it is heavy in lead, and the lead market
firmed, and as continuous improvements in concentrating
processes have been made (still under Greenway), profits
have steadily increased until last half two shilling divs.
were paid, and now another of sixpence, with a shilling
bonus, is announced. (A shilling means £10,000.) The
directors, however, only aim at quarterly sixpences, with
bonuses when possible ; and, as faces are full of ore,
while the lead market keeps firm they ought to be pos-
sible pretty often. The directors intend, though; 'to
build up a substantial reserve—and perhaps it may be
wanted ere long to build a zinc-treating plant. The
South has been a first-rate “ sticker,” and something
better than even its present moderate luck is -deserved
Or perhaps it isn’t luck, but just the result of hard, con-
tinuous battling.

Extract from letter sent to shareholders in a
now-notorious N.S.W.-formed g.-m. co., asking them
whether they weren’t disposed to contribute shares to
the legal manager as a gratuity for his services
“ From my personal knowledge of his endeavors to pro-
mote the interests of all who are connected with this
Company, he has given away his last 700 shares to the
editor of one of our leading newspapers in order that good
reports and the latest information from the mine may
constantly be kept before the public’s eye every week for
a period to extend over three months.” This will account
for many things. Now, who can be the “editor” who
got those 700 shares ? Or is it all a lie ?
* • * *
The Critic, London, has compiled and pub-
lished a list of “ A Hundred Wild Cats,” adding that it
would be well if its readers cut out the list and hung it in
a convenient place to save the trouble of continually
answering questions about such concerns. In the 100
editor Hess includes the Cobar Gold Mines. Now, from
the chief block of the group forming this co. over 20,000
tons were crushed at a profit before it was sold, after-
wards the tailings were cyanided at a profit of some thou-
sands, and nearly 4000 tons were profitably crushed by
the vendors before the co. was formed. True, a huge
mistake was made in trying to treat the ore by a 100-head
stamp mill alone—but to-day it would be easy to sell the
Cobar Gold Mines’ property in Australia for more money
than its total cost to date. Mr. Thos. Buckland, late of
Charters Towers, is now managing, and hopes to see the
big mill and cyanide-plant to match at work sometime
before the crack of doom.

Mt. Reid, Tas., is managed in London, where a
cable from the manager lately gave the average value of
sulphide ore for 4ft. across as 34 per cent, lead, 370 z. gold
and HOOoz. silver per ton. Directors wired to get the
manager to confirm the information re gold contents, and
he cabled : “ 370 z. gold confirmed.” Now, why have the
shares gone down if there is 4ft. of this sort of stuff in the
mine ?

From Croydon : Pumping machinery erected on
Croydon Consols mine at cost of £37oo—largest mine
pumping plant in N.Q.—started work about 12th Feb.
Adjoining, mines contribute towards baling expenses £32
per week. When Consols is free of water sinking will
commence with almost certainty—a mining certainty—of
striking rich shoot, and further divs. Golden Gate
Consols recently got exemption because a big shareholder
couldn’t pay his calls. And yet Warden Towner sat on a
local paper for daring to protest. Croydon Goldfields
Ltd., newly formed in London by Elmslie, Ltd., absorbs
the True Blue Block, Lady Isabelle, and N. Croydon
Consols. Capital provided to further test those promis-
ing properties, and operations begin forthwith. At
Ophir Block, a local co. is progressing with shaft going
down on slide to look for the famous Iguana reef. Pro-
mising indications continue. No. IG. Gate crushed
just as the mail was leaving a small parcel for a trifle over
loz. per ton. The sale to Elmslie, Limited, is “ off.” Good
property if properly managed. True Blue Extended
Block to be worked again. Improved machinery being
put in mine to test it at depth. Owned by Croydon Gold-
fields Limited, a Bull co., Elmslie local managers.
Ophir Block shaft still being sunk in the slide, which is
going down nearly vertical. Local capital only available,
as Govt, declines to subsidise on geologist Jack’s recom-
mendation. Yet Mr. Jack’s theory at the Golden Gate ro
a similar fault was disproved, and a 6oz. reef found below
the slide. At latest, No. 7 North Golden Gate was put-
ting through a 500-ton crushing. And other large lots,
aggregating oyer 1000 tons, were being treated simultane-
ously. Feb. yield expected to be a record for many years
past.

A recent delectable mining par. in Brisbane
Courier stated that Vic. Chief Secretary Peacock had
acquired 200 acres of ground adjoining the Mt. Morgan
Co. s property, with the intention of forming the property
into a syndicate in Melb. and subsequently floating it in
London under the name of the Mount Morgan Proprie-
tary. “ Mr. Peacock is confident,” said the par., “that
the Linda lode in the Mt. Morgan Co.’s property
extends into the ground he has acquired.” The real
Mt. Morganites and all the wild-catters who have ever
been in the district held aloof from those 200 acres in
order that Peacock might come in after years and take
em up and confer another boon on the London market.
Some men have all the luck.

• • • m
The payable portion of Kalgoorlie goldfield lies
within a parallelogram about a mile long by f-mile wide
having the Brownhill on its north, Gt. Boulder on the
south, Boulder N. on the east, and the Hainault on the
west. Probably nowhere in the world is there a richer
gold-producing area; even now the magnitude and variety
of the machinery on the various mines is worth going far
to see. Outside of that area, the present prospects of the
myriad of mines whose poppet-legs locate their position
are decidedly flat, the Maritana on account of its elevated
site being the most conspicuous failure. Though upwards
of £20,000 has been spent on it, it cannot sport a set
of poppet-legs. Possibly some of the mines outside the
proved-nch belt may have a future when their lodes are
tested at depths below 600 ft. Poor as these outside
mines undoubtedly are, with cheap water and fuel some
of them would pay handsomely. But what use talking of
conditions that can never exist ?
* * » *
More may yet be heard of diamond-deposits in
N.W. Westralia. The existence of diamonds at Nulla-
gine seems to have long been a known fact, and a pro-
specting party, lately sent out by some Perth people, o'ot
enough stones to convince the W.A. Government of the
payableness of the find. A reward claim of 320 acres was
taken up, also a large area under ordinary leases. The
formation is alleged to exactly resemble that of Kim-
berley. The diamonds found so far are all surface stones •
among them is one of a beautiful green tinge, said to
be of considerable value.

Major-General Tulloch, late of Victoria, cut a
poor figure in the witness-box in the W.A. Gold District
trading Corporation case (Goodman, promoter). Said
he joined the Trading crowd at the start, got 100 founder’s -
snares from Goodman for nothing, never saw or inquired
tor the articles of association of the co. which he was to
direct, never saw the secretary, found himself in the chair
at a meeting, did not know how or why he got there, had
no idea of the number of shares allotted to himself, or in
the concern altogether, remembered seconding a resolu-
tion for the declaration of 100 per cent. div. without
knowing whether it had ever been earned. Also, ad-
mitted that he had been a director of a good many other
companies-* 1 all of which had come to grief.” At this
probably much of the British public will express surprise
and consolation, as well as a determination to follow the
General if he goes in again. The military guinea-pig
busmess has been going on for years and carried on in <
the broad light of day. The War Office has never forbid-
den it. But when poor piper Findlater attempted to
earn a living at London Alhambra in a representation of the
storming of Dargai Heights that Office consulted its
dignity, and brought pressureon the management to cancel
Fmdlater’s engagement on the ground that “ his appear-
ance on the musical stage was not in accordance with
the best traditions of the Arrnv.”

Among the numerous mining experts and others
who recently landed in China in anticipation of a Celes-
tial boom, is Bill Adams, better known at Broken Hill and
Coolgardie as “Arizona Bill.” After leaving W.A., Bill
went to Klondyke, and not liking it, he crossed over to
China. Luckily for him, Pritchard Morgan, M.P., was in
Peking, and, as Bill had laid Morgan’s partner, G. W.
Hall, on to “ the Sons of Gwalia” (thereby putting many
shekels into the little Welshman’s pocket), he promptly
secured the services of the festive William.
* * * *
Pritchard Morgan, M.P. (writes a correspondent
to The Bulletin from China), has left China with a whole
province in his pocket, which he intends floating into a
huge company in London. The concession, obtained
directly from Li Hung Chang, consists of the right to
delve for all minerals in the western province of Szechuen •
area, 160,000 sq. miles ; population, 70,000,000. Among’
Anglo-Chinese the scheme is laughed at, but little
“ Prit.” is sanguine. In the event of asuccessful flotation,
several Coolgardie men are to be sent for, including Dick
Stewart and Charlie Porter.
Some explanation of the extraordinary rise in
copper, without the supply decreasing, or any marked
increase in consumption, comes from Boston, U'.S., which
is largely interested in the copper-mines of Montana and
other States. Estimated that 25 million dol. went into
copper stocks lately. Boston’s heavy buying was brought
about by the story that the Standard Oil Co., probably
the strongest organisation in the world, had been for
some time working its way into, .the world’s copper busi-
ness ; that it had acquired a big interest in Calumet and
Ilecla, the world s second copper-producer, and proposed
to. consolidate it with the few other great mines, fix the
price the world must pay for its copper, and thereby
make money by the million. Whispered that this co. is
about to try the experiment of the French syndicate, but
in a different way. When operations started the French
crowd had money enough to buy all the big copper-mines
of the world, but, instead of doing that, it entered into
contracts for the outputs of the big mines, and when it
tried to limit these trouble began. To take the Anaconda
product at the price was too much for the Frenchmen
and, rather than accept the syndicate’s limit, Daley (chief
owner of the great mine) declared that he would land
copper in London at £28 a ton, and yet make money. It
may be true that, forewarned by the syndicate’s collapse,
the Standard Oil Co. is quietly buying up the mines them-
selves ; but to control the copper market of the world is a
mighty contract.

Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 4 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8, 4/8, in N.S.W. only. To all the other colo-
nies 1/8, 2/8, 3/8,5/2. Beware of imitations. Only
address: Senior, chemist, 246 George-st., Sydney.*

SOCIETY
The over-righteous clergy who raised such
a howl about the Sunday departure of Gov.
Hampden were deficient in generalship. They
should have carried out their protest to the bitter
end by rolling up in full force at the station and
publicly praying to Heaven to pardon the noble
lord for the great sin he was committing by taking
his hook on “the Loard’s Daay.” Anyhow, it
was a very paltry squeak : Lord Hampden made
no splash, and not even an artillery man was on
duty that would otherwise have been free to sin
with his best girl by roaming through the park
and stamping the seed out of the grass with his
large, flat feet. And in the great roar of eternity
there is so little difference between the enthusiastic
yelling of “Hooray” and the discordant bellow-
ing of “ Hosanna” that it will never be noticed.

The boys of All Saints’ Anglican school
and of St. Stanislaus’ R.C. school, Bathurst, have
taken up in their own way the newspaper feud
lately waged by Card. Moran and Bishop Caimdge.
Cricket and football between the two schools have
had to be suspended, as the youngsters cannot be
trusted to meet without fighting.
* * • *
“8.E.” : Two lines in a last week’s par.
inveigled me into “ farewelling ” the Lancers.
“The fine, lumpy young Cornstalks .... the
shapely young man from Jamberoo.” Was it
sarcasm? Not one of imposing girth or height,
weedy as to body, scrawny as to neck, mostly (it
seemed) knock-kneed, stoop-shouldered, and, as to
feet —well, any two to the yard ! “ Shapely ! ”
ye gods ! Yet, by the holy wombat, they looked
as if six months’ stiff training might make them
look something like men !

Adelaide dailies are being deluged with
odes on the appointment of Lord Tennyson to
S.A. Governorship, but none have so far been
published. The great question is whether, if
Tennyson himself breaks out on the subject of the
dying stockman, the editors will be sufficiently
plucky to decline.

The “ Mary Magdalene ” discussion in the
Melb. daily papers is painfully puerile from an
agnostic point of view. The Argus, for instance,
solemnly quotes from Chambers’s Encyclopaedia
—“Mary Magdalene, a woman out of whom
Jesus cast seven devils, and who believed in Him.
To her He first appeared after His resurrection.”
Does the editor of the Argus accept the Encyclo-
paedia dogmas, and, if not, why didn’t he add an
editorial footnote ? Shades of the candid Watts !

SHE (to “ traveller, ” who has ashed for rations):
“ Right you are, my man. Put in a couple of
hours at the wood heap, and I’ll fill your bays
and give you a shilling.”
TRAVELLER: “Madam, I regret my inability to
avail myself of your hind offer of employment.
Fact is, I’m begging my way round the world
for a reaper, and the articles strictly forbid that
I engage in manual labor.”
SHE: “ Indeed! Very sorry, but I’m a member of
the anti-betting league — so, of course.”

They tell us now the Magdalene
In saintliness did sit,
And was forgiven for the sins
That she did not commit.

fall,
I made her heartstrings quiver on the flat
When the pony missed his take-off, and we crashed
into the wall;
WeU, she simply had to have me after that /
It awoke a thrill of int’rest when they pulled me out
for dead
From beneath the shattered ruins of a horse ;
And although she looked indiff’rent when I landed

the poor girl know
That a monkey could have ridden it and won l

often that occurs !

For I showed her all the blood-marks where I jabbed

And the whip-strokes where I hit him with the

red.

A LOYAL
DEMONSTRATION.
HER GRANDAD: “Do you
hear the thunder?”
LITTLE FIVE-YEAR-OLD : “Oo, yes! Who
is letting them off?” (In an awe-stricken whis-
per). “ I suppose it’s Dod’s birthday !”
The tracing-out of that murdered Indian
hawker at Mount Mary (W.A.) was a rather
brighter piece of detective work than the ’Groper
bobbies are usually guilty of. Their attention
was first attracted by a very affluent drunk, who
was run in on the usual lines. They found on
him about 24 cheap silk handkerchiefs, which
seemed unusual, as he hadn’t any worse a cold
than the average ; besides they were all new.
Also, his pockets were full of cheap brass jewel-
lery, and he had a good deal of money. Inquiry
revealed that he had never been a hawker, and
that, until a few days before his arrest, he had
been stone-broke and suffering terribly for want of
his proper beer. From the handkerchiefs and
brass jewellery they assumed that an Indian hawker
had been robbed, and as there was no Indian
clamoring for redress at any adjacent police-
station they further inferred that he had been
murdered. So they started to track down the
various Hindoos who peddled trash in that district
until one track ended in a heap of ashes and a
decayed head and a grave. The beneficent in-
fluences of drink had brought the crime to light.
So far the police are no nearer proving a murder
case against their drunk than at the beginning;
nobody saw him commit the murder, and the
Indian might have been killed by someone else,
or lie might have died from natural causes, or he
might have burnt himself for reasons unknown.
Still, the manner in which they inferred a dead
body and a grave from 24 cheap silk handkerchiefs
has made the local police so proud that they won’t
even drink with ordinary people now.
* • * *
Ward, the colt-breaker, charged with
murdering a Hindoo hawker up-country in South
Australia, had evidently been studying the Bur-
gess case and the vast importance which the des-
perate Q. police tried to attach to every one of
the suspect’s casual remarks. So when officer
Burchill arrested him and demanded if he had
any remarks to offer, Ward smiled and observed
that it was a nice day. And now that is printed
as clear proof of the villain’s callousness.
* « • «
Re undiscovered murders in Q. In Mackay
district alone, between ’92 and ’95, nine murders
were-committed. Only one was cleared up.
Chiefly the work of Tommy Tanna and co. Most
of the murdered ones were hacked with cane-
knives, and in one or two female cases there were
horrible accessories.
* * * *
The recent struggle for Sydney City-Solici-
torship was a degrading thing. Whatever dignity
the law may have left, little of it remains in the
ranks of the attorneys or, to be more precise,
among such attorneys as competed for the prize in
question. Of all the applicants, it appears, three
only maintained any appearance of independence.
Nearly every man Jack circularised or privately
wrote the aldermen explaining whom he had
gone to school with, and so on ; and most of them
whined piteously in the most approved cadging-
deadbeat style. It was also a most melancholy
exhibition, even for Sydney, of resort to sectarian
influences and lodge-dodgery.
A fond Tasmanian mother christened her
last girl by the aboriginal name “ Yolla.” When
they afterwards looked in Mulligan's Vocabulary
of Aboriginal Dialects they found it meant “a
young muttonbird ! ”
* * * *
A curious legend about a quack doctor
keeps cropping in Melbourne, despite vigor-
ous efforts to sit on the yarn and keep it
down. An old lady had an incurable com-
plaint, and her medico had paid his last
visit—said there was nothing to do but
lot her die comfortably, and he was
going home to make out the bill.
Then the sorrowing family called
in an advertising doctor, and he
looked at the comatose patient,
and said it was a very bad
case, but if his antibilious
horse-ball was taken regu-
larly in water she would
pull through. He col-
lected his £2 2s. and
left. A minute after
he was gone.
the relatives
got scared by
the coldness and dead
silence of the patient,
and dragged in a
physician who lived
three doors away. He
said that he wouldn’t
prescribe because the
lady had been dead more
than half-an-hour. Then the
family called on the advertising
doctor, and threw the anti-bilious
horse-ball at his head, and de-
manded back the £2 2s. he had col-
lected for prescribing for a corpse, and £1 for in-
jury to their feelings, and £3 10s. for saying
nothing about it. And even then the story leaked
out. It always does, somehow.

Force of habit strong in death. The
Maorilanders, M‘Kain and his wife, after agree-
ing to die together, put out their boots to be
cleaned at the hotel where they were staying, and
the woman carefully did up her hair before lying
down to her last sleep.

“Tui Tea” : Curious how little noise is
made by the discharge of firearms in a closed room.
When Maorilander legislator Larnaoh shot him-
self a few months ago in a committee-room during
the sitting of Parliament, with messengers, clerks,
&c., all around, no noise was heard save a slight
thud as though a book had fallen. .So also in the
Palmerston tragedy, in which three shots were
discharged. People asleep in the next room were
awakened, but had no conception that shots had
AUSTRALIAN HISTORY.
“ What great event took place in the year 1889?”
“ Oh ! that was the year that I was born !”
been fired, though there was probably an interval
of a minute between the first two shots and of 20
minutes between the second and third. A pre-
vious suicide at Masterton, committed in the day-
time with a rifle, made so little noise owing to the
deed being enacted in a closed room that a man in
the next room took no particular notice of it.
* * • •
Kelly, who failed to kill himself after
having fatally shot his inamorata, Maria Flynn,
at Paddington, Sydney, was not aware that he
had dispatched his “love” until 16 days had
elapsed. He survived the shock, however, of
beinj' told that his presence was required at the
inquest. How Kelly must have regretted the
change from the fresh, airy ward at St. Vincent’s
Hospital to the gin-case structure known as the
Coroner s Court! No Chinaman’s den was ever a
fouler place. 70 to 80 people elbowed each other
in what is hardly double-bedroom space. Looking
round, it was hardly possible to distinguish the
iurv panel from the general public, and Kelly
hadn’t even eibow-room between two policemen.
Llie bench is elevated, and as the Coroner leans
forward over his sardined audience he seems to be
an animated portion of the coiling; also, the
deposition clerk blots up as much perspiration as
ink as he turns over each sheet of evidence. The
aii is as thick and as noisome as that of a mortuary
awaiting a funeral train. Would-be assassins, for
whom the hangman has no terrors, are warned
what to expect if they are caught killing in Sydney.
At the Coroner’s Court, Kelly wore the aspect
of a man who didn’t think it was loaded. He
evidently felt he was detained on false pretences.
He has the appearance of an energetic grocer, and
looks just as if he had been snipped out of a har-
mony of tinned-fish and soap-bars. He is as much
bkfi a desperado as a Sunday-school teacher is like
a Chinese comedian.
A “ Christian Clergyman ” writes to Bris-
bane Courier re recent outrages in Bananaland.
“ Christian Clergyman ” has a cure for the villainy
that flourishes in Australia just now. “ The
best plan would be to flo°- these brutes periodi-
cally for three years, and then hang them to
save the cost of keeping them.” Why not flog
them periodically, and starve them between
times to save cost of tucker? . . . Why
doesn’t some howling Atheist, pugnacious
Freethinker, or notorious scoffer and blas-
phemer come to the front with some
scheme of torture ? Somehow, they’re
always in the background when these
little humanitarian proposals are
brought forward, and the square-
jawed, scowling apostle of the lowly
Nazarene is left to swing the cat,
dangle the noose, and work up a
little Hell on his own account.
* « *
Some people’s virtue is in-
deed a fearful and wonderful thing.
Sometime ago a gay and giddy
banker had a selection made
from Burton’s “Arabian Nights,”
neatly typed, in violet ink, on
parchment-like paper, and care-
fully bound in white and yellow.
It cost a pretty figure too, and he
had hardly read it through when he
died. His widow, a very proper lady
of a pious turn of mind, was unspeak-
ably shocked to find this erotic frag-
ment among his private papers, and
determined to destroy the unclean
thing—till she found the bill. Then
she carefully packed it up, fumi-
gated the house, and sent the
horrible thing to Melbourne to be
sold. Reserve price, £lO.
* * *
The most difficult item of
dietary to force upon Norfolk islan-
ders, when transferred from Pitcairn
thither, was cows’ milk. They had had
a couple of cows at Pitcairn, but had
never tasted their milk, and believed it
unnatural for human beings to drink it.
Even infants on Norfolk Island irsfcinc-
tively rejected it, and when mild pres-
sure was employed the milk made them
sick. Mrs. Selwyn, wife of Bishop Selwyn,
who lived on the island for some years, took
the women and children in hand, and suc-
ceeded at last in making milk the principal
breakfast beverage.
Since the name of Dubberke’s “soldier-
friend ” leaked out, nobody is surprised to learn
that the boot-box tragedienne has decided to
abandon her old life and become a quiet, steady
Thekla. The old life must have been awfully
trying, if she had many admirers like her “mili-
tary-friend.” But will the police give Thekla a
fair chance to live quietly anywhere in Australia?
So many policemen devote most of their time to
discovering suspicions of giddiness in girls and
adopting sneaky means to hold the damsels in
fear.

“ Cockney ” to The Bulletin :
Surely one of your correspondents holds a brief for
young Tod. He says—“ The charge against Tod for
murder is the first that has ever been laid in Vic. against
the man who ‘ got the girl into trouble.’ Had Tod simply
continued to sit in his office when Dubberke announced
the girl’s death, and taken no part in the boot-box
burial, the law would have done no more than ask his
evidence at the most.’’ This is on a par with most of the
press inferences and suggestions concerning the culpa-
bility of the three boot-box packers, of whom Dubberke
was selected as the newspaper scapegoat. What had
Tod s subsequent conduct to do with his legal responsi-
bility for Mabel Ambrose’s death ? The whole gruesome
business arose from the willingness of three women to
oblige young Tod. The girl Mabel having given him
the use of herself, consented to suffer much agony—to
please Tod. Madame attempted the abortion to please
Tod, the rent-collector who was not squeezing any rent
from her. And when the girl Mabel had fallen a victim
to the Tod-dictated policy of herself and madam, the girl
Dubberke came forward and gave her advioe and assist-
ance to Tod in the disposal of the body. Tod was the in-
stigator of the attempt at abortion. Dubberke—the
“ callous ” Dubberke—was the only one of the quartet
who made any protest against the maltreatment of Mabel.
This much was admitted by Dr. Gaze under cross-exami-
nation, and he at that time had no cause to speak kindly
of the damsel who “ blew the gaff.”—P.

A PARABLE UP TO DATE.
TFor The Bulletin.]
There were ninety and nine safe-housed in the fold ;
But one was still out in the dark and cold ;
And the face of the Shepherd grew keen and bright
As he faced the storm and the hilltops white.
“ For the sinner that’s lost is more loved than all
The righteous many that fail not nor fall,”
He said, and His kind face shone. Next day
The ninety and nine all went astray !
Constance Clyde.

The publicans gain by the trouble in
Samoa —at least those in Apia do—for two of them
having been fined for serving drink to those who
would talk war after 11 p.m., the Municipal
Council met forthwith and amended the Licensing
Ordinance by giving the pubs, an extension till
midnight. As the hotels are also open from Ip.m.
till 12 p.m. on Sundays the man with a thirst has
little to complain of.
* * • *
Samoa runs a peculiar church called “ the
Apia Foreign Church,” founded as far back
as 1848, and opened in ’49. At its jubilee service
in January, the Rev. Huckett, the officiating
clergyman, alluded to it as a church in which
Catholic or Protestant might worship, and termed
it “ a visible witness to the common religion of
the Anglo-Saxon race.” That being so, the Apia
church is, no doubt, furnished with a golden calf.
• • * *
It is now a case of do or die with some
Melb. churches. On Saturday week a band
paraded the streets of Carlton with a large calico
sign on a lorry, and in big red letters the words :
“‘Harvest Festival,’ To-morrow, Sunday,” &c.
Why, even Dan Barry hasn’t yet stooped to this
mode of advertising !

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 7th, 1899.
My Dear Myee—
Sydney’s very latest good-bye to the Hainpdens
came off at Redfem station on Sunday afternoon.
The gathering on the platform was small, and
mostly black-coated and bell-toppered, only about
a half-dozen women presenting themselves. I
noticed Mrs. Edmund Barton, Mrs. W. R. Camp-
bell (whose signature is so familiar in charity
announcements), Miss Darley, Mrs. Mat. Harris,
and Mrs. French, all dressed in black, black and
white, or some shade of complimentary mourning.
Quite a study in men was offered by the little
gathering. There was the Admiral, in white
uniform, with a sort of “I must precede ” gleam
in his stern eye, and jolly flag-lieutenant Stewart,
looking as if precedence was never mentioned at
Admiralty House. Then there was the majestic
and always elegantly-frock-coated “Chief,” with
his fine old Irish visage and his testy way of say-
ing “I can’t heah you.” Then there was a cer-
tain railway light, who had stepped right out of
Trollope with a pink flush on his cheek, bushy
whiskers, a cut-away coat, and a sweet, old-
fashioned smile that you seldom see out of the
novels of ’6O. Among the big men, I noticed
Dr. Roth, once the idol of every ladies’ school in
Sydney—that was in the days when he taught
ladies’ schools where their hearts beat—and re-
gretted he was not allowed to come in his beauti-
ful new uniform. A timid-looking Minister, who
shrank into the shadows of the posts, was the
educative Mr. Hogue, who has been so rushed
with cookery committees that he is beginning to
look on every woman as a deputation.
The leave-taking was melancholy and quiet.
Valets, ladies - maids, and luggage were the
only vice-regal representatives until a little
before five, when cheering broke out in the
crowded streets near the station, and the party
proper came on to the platform. Miss Alice and
Miss Dorothy Brand, in black suits and sailor-
hats, took tearful good-bye of their particular
friend, Miss Darley. Lady Hampden, looking
persistently cheerful, said a few words to everyone
who came near her, but Lord Hampden seemed to
feel the strain very keenly, and once in the train
was not seen to look out again. Her Ex. smiled
bravely to the last, bowing from the window as
the train moved off. The aides and the Hons.
Dorothy and Alice waved from the little platform
till the train was out of sight. Everyone cried,
and the one touch of humor was lent by a lot of
white-ducked police who came in too late and
solemnly did their manoeuvres whep everything
was over.
Another peculiar feature was the absence of
clergymen. Not even Bishop “Bob” put in an
appearance, and the only two representatives of
Registered Holiness who braved the sin of going
to a railway-station on the Sabbath were a Catho-
lic priest and a Jewish Rabbi. I hear that Lord
Hampden was bitterly hurt by the evil-mannered
clerical howl against his Sunday exit. Nothing
that happened in his time of governing vexed him
as much as that ill-bred interference in a free
man’s personal affairs.
Altogether, the week was under a cloud. Pre-
sident Faure’s funeral service at St. Mary’s Cathe-
dral rather deepened the gloom on Friday morning.
Thousands of people thronged to this ; the French
residents out of respect to M. Faure, the mass
of Sydney people because there had been an
announcement that Lady Hampden would be
there. What a horrible thing it was to see a
crush of people falling over each other at the
doorway to get a glimpse of the Governor’s face,
and, as they said loudly enough, “see how he
looked ! ” Lady Hampden spared herself this by
staying away, but Mrs. Admiral Pearson, who sat
in the front seat in deep mourning, looked so like
Her Ex. that the crowd worked off a little of their
diseased curiosity on her.
But saying good-bye to the Nineven was really
the wettest function of a very teary week. Women
of all ages from three to seventy rushed down
Kent-street soon after noon on Friday, and by two
o’clock not only the wharf and the boat itself were
crowded, but all the wool-warehouses near had
windowsful of fluttering figures, and some bold
women even swarmed the rigging of a boat close
at hand.
Talk about Pre-eminent in Grief ! This was a
regular orgie of tears, and sobs, and kisses. In
fact, I noticed many of the Lanceahs taking heart-
broken farewells—of several women. Flags flying,
bands playing martial airs, weeping women,
apguished kisses —do you know, it was quite hard
to believe that our gallant Lanceahs were not
walking straight into a cannon’s jaw. Most of the
coffee-colored brigade were weedy-looking youths
who cried into handkerchiefs without any frill as
the Nineveh moved away.
On the same boat I noticed Mr. Gullett
and that bright, fascinating personality, Mrs.
Gullett, starting on a trip to Europe with two of
their children. Lots of people came down to say
good-bye to the popular S.M. Herald man,
among them Miss Curnow, Mr. Geoffrey Fairfax,
and Mrs. Alex. Oliver and one of her three pretty
daughters. Mr. and Mrs. Edward Sievers were
also farewelled with much vegetable blossom by a
big crowd of friends.
Captain Fielden, Hampden’s Private Secretary,
is going to stay on with the Darleys till the Beau-
champ regime begins, and possibly will then be
taken on the new suite. One young Englishman,
who had been offered a Sydney aideship by Lord
Beauchamp, refused it lately, preferring to go
a\yay to foreign parts and die of fever : which he
did. But surely to be an aide is not as bad as all
that. To keep the fat butcher’s wife from dropping
* n P rom i s 9 u °u B ly to luneh at Govt. House, and to
finesse with impossible people who want to be
asked to vioe-regal dinners, and eat peas with a
knife, and call out “Pass the wine, Jersey” (as
overheard in the Jersey days), is not pleasant work
for a young man. But the atmosphere of great
glory that surrounds him wherever he goes makes
up for forty butchers’ wives—to most young men.
Certainly, it didn’t make up to Arthur Galton ;
but then he was classic and religious, and we don’t
understand that brand of aide out here,
Mr. James Ashton’s marriage with Miss Helen
Willis came off at Sutton Forest last Monday,
Miss Willis, only daughter of the Rev.
Willis, of Sutton Forest, is well-known in Sydney
as a flower-painter, and has often herself figured
on canvas as “ Spring ’’ and other idyllic figures,
She is a fair, nice-looking woman, while the bride-
groom is a dark, Gibsonesque man with a chin,
Another quiet wedding was that of Mrs. Wolsten-
holme and Prof. Anderson on Friday, March 3.
This happy pair are spending their honeymoon in
a house-boat on the Hawkesbury.
Mrs. Theodore Boesen’s friends have fixed on
Thursday, March 10, tor their farewell evening to
that popular lady. The event comes off at Needs’
rooms, and is to be chiefly musical, I understand,
Another fixture is Miss Darclie’s (of the Sydney
D.T.) lecture on the Mental Science Movement, at
Vickery’s Chambers, Tuesday, March 14.
