“ The Work Girl” at the Theatre Royal is a
s t ran g e SO rt of drama, in which a girl who has had
a great deal of trouble always comeson with a
llt | le L It looks as y if her trouble was
wrappe P d up in that litt i e bit of brown paper, and
1 dee J ly disappo inted to find that she Aever
opened it p A the au dience. Miss Ida
Gresham gets the only frock in the play—a white
satin bridal gown, without any distinct features,
Agile Amy Gourlay seems to come into the piece
by accident, but the audience could have done
with more of her clever, dainty dancing, in a black
and scarlet frock. Miss Ada Guilford, who
dropped dead in an old brown frock and bonnet,
and Miss Marian Medway as a wioked French
dressmaker in slate silk, owe their first appearance
to this play. The former, like most people who
die early, promised well. But the shadow of a
huge Hebrew, undertaken by Mr. J. P. O’Neill,
rests heavy on the piece, and after the Jew in
“After Dark’’and the Jew in “Formosa,” and
other Jews in other recent plays, who all say
“Slippery Solomon” and “Jumping Moses,” as
no good Jew ever does, one gets weary of Israel,
Maroh is pointing at the winter Fashions rather
derisively. All sorts of extremes are going to
rule. Hats are to be small and trimmed with soft
bunches of quills combining plumage from the
breast and tail of different birds, or with ostrich-
feathers. Small white felts, and a round shape
worn well on the forehead and trimmed with
black feathers, are commendable, and the felt
sailors surrounded with rows and rows of
black velvet will take on chicness from black
and white waved wings. Dark purples, pale
mauves, peaoh, turquoise and very deep red, may
all go on to a toque together, while the
queerest dead blues and impossible browns will
mix up with tomatoes and a variety of green tints.
In writing, these look raw. In reality, they are
the fulfilment of the season’s most aesthetic
dreams. Lace and jet embroideries will appear
on all smart confections, and the applique cloth
on glac6 is another note that is going to be loudly
struck. In evening sleeves there is a choice
between two —one small, round, and very short;
and the other of lace cut in one with a mitten,
and held on the shoulders by straps of ribbons or
jewels. Shot, shaded velvet and ehirui ribbons
will be used as waistbands, cravats and revers,
until the ribbon craze will be as dead as only a
past fashion can be. In materials, plain cashmeres,
cloths, and braids, and fabrics stripped in bayadere
fashion with velvet take the lead. The general out-
line of the female figure has now to become narrower
and narrower, giving a winding-tower effect to
those whose avoirdupois permits. Stitching is
still one of fashion’s dearest idols. On the blouse
it takes the form of tuckings flat, round, or fanci-
ful. Indeed, the blouse of the hour is a simple
thing of glace or corded silk, tucked all over its
front, its back, and on the top of the sleeve, and
with no other decoration than a set of round cut
crystal buttons down the front, shirt-fashion. In
jewellery, pearls still hold their own, but the deep
collar has driven out the simple pearl rope. To
sum-up, if you want to be smart this winter your
hat must owe its charms to a fowl-yard, your
bodice must be connected with as many arctic
animals as possible, and your skirt mus+ —
positively must —suggest snakes.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The Madden - Osborne wedding was gone
through with on the advertised date, smoothly but
inglonously. Beforehand those interested industri-
ously circulated the statement that, spectacularly
speaking, the function was to be as theatrical and
expensive as the turning-off of sister Sylvia. In
answer to this, old hands said all along, ‘ Once
bitten twice shy ! ’ More than one Melb. mother
has never smiled again since the bills for the
first trousseau in her family came in.
“As a rule, when there’s a quiverful of
daughters, each bride of the bunch is got rid of
more cheaply than her predecessor, till at last the
youngest of all gladly compounds for an evening
bridal, ‘ ’cause you know one’s things look so much
better by artificial light. ’ The more recent Mad-
den bride changed her name in white satin, its
silk-embroidered chiffon overskirt vandyked and
edged with silk fringe. The usual lilliputian lisse
frills overflowed the underslip, tulle ruches lined
the not-unfamiliar satin train, and a bow of tulle
and silk-fringe adorned one corner thereof. Em-
broidered chiffon sleeves were cut short at. the
wearer’s well-nourished elbows, and her nine
maids also pursued traditional Madden tactics and
bared their upper chests to the breezes. These
last carried be-daisied (it was a ‘ Daisy Wedding ’)
gold-tipped, white Empire sticks, wore wreaths
of white roses and heather, and buckled white
satin ribbon round their throats, with the bride-
groom’s pearl and turquoise souvenirs.
“The Chief Justice’s eldest daughter showed
herself a believer in the ‘blood-thicker-than-water’
adage. Every one of her nine bridesmaids was
either a relative or a connection. One of them,
Miss Aileen Rowan, her cousin, is a gentle, rather
demure-looking damsel, fair as a lily and possessed
of the daintiest hands extant. Her mother, Mrs.
Andrew Rowan, was the dark-eyed belle of the
Stephen family in her day ; but at this stage, Lady
Madden and Mrs. Bingham, comparatively slight
and youthful of figure, have the best of it.
“ Never was there such a jabberwocky bride as
Chief Justice Madden’s eldest duckling. There
was none of your blushing, bashful coyness about
Miss Daisy Madden. She chattered to her father
all the way from the carriage to the altar, and she
chattered to her brand-new hubby all the way
from the altar to the carriage. She seemed to
take it all as a matter of course, as who should
say, ‘A wedding? Certainly! What a lark!
Let’s go and have one ! ’ Being a chubby, sub-
stantial, jolly-looking woman, she more than filled
the bill, and as all her friends say she is the best-
tempered girl in the world, her spouse should
regard himself as rather a lucky dog.
“Be recent wedding; no ‘maidens’ tribute’
was forthcoming this time. ‘ Girls of Victoria ’
skulked in their fastnesses, and never let the word
‘ tiara ’ pass their lips. However, a doating bride-
groom supplied the deficiency; his bride bought
herself a necklet with her various presentation
cheques; some sassiety matrons clubbed together
and gave her a gemmed ‘Daisy’ pendant, so,
despite the stingy spinsters aforesaid, Mrs.
Osborne need not face the world unbediamonded.
“We are, shortly, to have a genuine member of
the British Beerage here, on a long visit to Gov.
Brassey and, probably, as a member of his staff.
Lord Hindlip (real name Allsopp, head of the
‘ pale ale ’ family) is a young fellow of 22, whose
grandfather was made a peer because he had made
a fortune out of his brewery. It is understood
that he is coming in cask, and will be bottled and
labelled by Brassey, Nevill and Co., the importers.
Hindlip is unmarried and is a millionaire, so there
is a chance for somebody who is not above taking
on a brewer. Up to the present he has done
nothing to distinguish himself, though a son of
Allsopp’s ale ought to have ‘a head on him.’
“Grace Noble, in face and figure, is strikingly
like Eva Moore (wife of H. V. Esmond, the author
of the comedy) who played the leading part in
‘One Summer’s Day,’ in London. Everybody
notices the improvement in Miss Noble since she
was last out here. Her voice is not so up-on-the-
roofish ; it is even a beautiful voice now, that
gives pleasure to a fastidious ear. She always was
graceful, and is a kissy little person all through.
“It seems that some women have found in
Carne, of the Brough co., a successor to Julius
Knight, Charles Thursby, and Charlie Ryley.
The present scribe sat beside a group of three in
the Princess Theatre stalls during ‘ One Summer’s
Day.’ One opened fire: ‘Oh, I sy, Carne isn’t in
the piece. ’ ‘ Isn’t he ? Oh, what a shyme. ’ ‘ Isn’t
he a love ? ’ ‘ He’s a darling of a man.’ ‘ Lovely,
I think.’ ‘Yes,’ said the other girl, capping the
comments, ‘ and they sy he’s quite a gentleman—
not like an actor at all.’ There, now ! Also, his
front name is Joseph.
“ How would matinees fare for audiences if
there were no mummers dying to kill their spare
daylight time? Frances Ross sported powder-
pink blouse, with lots of black baby ruches, and
plumed Trelawney hat, at that Bijou charity
function t’other Saturday. Styan, of same co.,
was on view, too, and Beauteous Baker, in tan
vest and dull-blue tie. Napoleon Boffard, giving
a hand with the programme, affected cameo sleeve-
studs, pale-grey pants with sad-seam stripes,
diamond ring, and moire-antique vest; this last
calling up thoughts of Wilkie Collins’ plump
foreigner Fosco, of the wonderful waistcoats.
“Benefit entertainments fall heavily on a cer-
tain section of the community. And the plot
thickens. Not only is a generous public asked
to assist a beneficiare to develop his or her'talents
abroad, but circulars invite recipients to state how
much they are prepared to contribute to the sup-
port of the absentee’s dear ones.
“Peacock, of the steam-hooter laugh, took the
chair when Sir Evelyn Oakeley, the English In-
spector-General of Training Colleges, lectured to
the Melb. teachers on education. Being Minister
of Education, and presiding over a meeting of
educational experts, Peacock was, naturally, more
than usually incoherent, vague, rambling, and
ungrammatical. He called the lecturer ‘ our dis-
tinguished visitor ’ thirteen times. Once he made
a dash for the lecturer’s name, and called him ‘ Sir
Evelyn Wood,’pronouncing the front name with
the first syllable like the beginning of ‘ heavenly ’
or ‘heavy.’ Then, when everybody laughed, he
returned to ‘ our distinguished visitor, ’ and stuck
to it. ‘ Good Lord ! ’ groaned a school-teacher,
‘ and that’s my Ministerial chief ! ’ It really was
rather a sad exhibition.
“The Dental College, whose founder, E. L.
Oldfield, has had to play a lone hand against a
combination of all the other leading dentists in
Melbourne, can now show particular cause for the
rancour of its male opponents. Oldfield started
with the intention of leading Australian women
dentists into the field, and four of his fair students
have got there at last, duly qualified for the fray.
This, in spite of a recent Act of Parliament, which
looks uncommonly like a paltry device for ‘ killing
the nerve ’ of Oldfield’s College, and clearing the
way for a later college, set up by the combination
of rival practitioners aforesaid. It would seem
that the influence operating against the original
Dental College was not so strong as the influence
at the back of the four lady pupils. They were
able to pass their exam, with the Melb. Dental
Board ere—only a few hours ere—the Board could
get its anti- Oldfield Bill passed through the
Assembly.
“Motives apart, women are bom cultivators,
while men in a garden shine only as destroyers.
Poor Mother Eve ! Fancy her having to‘honor
and obey’ that stupid Adam, while he fussed
round ‘ pruning and restraining ’ (vide Milton) the
beauties.
Y ours affectionately,

A Melbourne scribe
tells » touching anecdote
ef “Buck Montgomery,” the
bold burglar hanged at
Sydney some years ago, in
cpmpany with his mate, Wil-
liams, for a brutal attack on
the Union Co’s safe—and for
hitting a policeman on the head
in his struggle to escape. The
scribe was present one night at
» big fight at the Cumberland
Club in Exhibition-street, and,
after the final tap of the gong,
he stayed behind with the
manager to write up his copy.
The “gate,” amounting to over
-2GOO, had been stowed away in
the iron safe in the manager’s
room, and only heand the inkist
remained in the building. After
some minutes the manager
went out to keep an appoint-
ment previously made, and did
not return for about an hour—at the
end of which time the scribe had
written his last slip. He left for
the office just as the manager came
back, and 50 yards up the street- encoun-
tered Montgomery with two other men.
Buck, ’ with whom the pressman
was acquainted, stopped him with
a gesture, indicating pity rather than
anger. “Well, Mr. Blank,” he re-
marked, “you’re. a dead nark, and no
mistake.” The journalist was puzzled ;
“m W y° n mean ? ” he inquired.
Why, me and my two mates were just
goin in to bust that bloomin’ tank, and here
yon go and spoil the whole thing. One of the
ehaps was on for giving you a tap on the ’ead, but
1 wasn t going to have anything like that. But
you beat us out of a good haul, and no mistake.”
And Montgomery went off shaking his head and
meditating on the hollowness of life.
Premier Kingston's friends are anxious
about his health. Always “ a tiger for work,” his
extra duties over the big Harrold insolvency case
have been such that his medical man forecasts
breakdown unless he takes rest. With a big
Assembly election pending, Kingston’s health and
vigor mean much to S.A. Democrats.
Wilson, the teacher and ex-clergyman,
suspected of Oxley murder, comes of one of the
best county families in Westmoreland. The name
Larus is one that has been used in the family
for generations. Charlotte Bronte was at school
at Oasterton. and Mr. Brooklehurst, in “Jane
Jtyre, was the Rev. Carus Wilson, of Casterton
HaU, grandfather of the arrestee.
When an Oxford student, N.S. Wales’
incoming Governor plumed himself on theological
proficiency and was remonstrated with for some of
, opinions by the Bishop of Worcester,
where the Beauchamp seat is situated. The
young theologian stood to his guns, however, and
proved to his own satisfaction that the Bishop
knew little or nothing about the matter in dispute.

Premier Reid is seldom in the Chamber
when members are attacking him. On such occa-
Mons he may be found in the refreshment-room
swallowing oysters. Then he returns to the
Chamber with the air of a man who has demolished
all opposition. Under criticism, the Premier is
an inveterate interrupter—he cannot help it, he
says. He adds that, aware of his failing, he often
leaves the room so that the other man may have
his say.

Among the English New Year Q.C.-ships
was a certain Butler Cole Aspinall—a relative of
the Butler Cole Aspinall who furnished Australia
with a repertoire of forensic and other anecdotes.
James Ashton, M.L.A. (N.S. W.), who the
other day married Miss Helen Willis, takes for
father-in-law the most pronounced clerical advo-
cate of Henry George’s land-tax ideas in Aus-
tralia. The Rev. Mr. Willis spent a generation as
Anglican parson in Manly.
Gov. Lamington, holidaying to Tasmania,
is a travelled Thane who loves frequent change of
scene. He has “done” the Far East minutely,
and America from North to South. He is still
under 40. His father wrote a heroic poem, called
“The Morea,” described by critics as the very
worst in the English language.
AT THE ART SHOW.
ARTIST (explaining) : “ Of course, you will per-
ceive that the trees in my picture are all young
ones.”
CRITIC (with a wink): “No harm in that, old
man. They'll probably be old enough by the
time you sell it. ”
Marshall Hall has legally abandoned the
title of “Hymns Ancient and Modern ” for the
giddy little book of “ pomes ’’ —wise and foolish—
that set the Melb. parsons shrieking for his blood
six months ago. The original edition of 400 or
500 copies bearing the pirated title, which were on
sale for a few days only, will be valuable in
the dim future as curiosities of Australian litera-
ture. By the way, why doesn’t the Prof, re-
christen the book “Hymns—and Hers?”
The Marquis of Normanby, when Governor
of Queensland, once gave a more-or-less Parlia-
mentary dinner, at which—men were topers in
those days—one noted Leg. Councillor laid his
ears back at every drink that came along. Just
as the ladies left the room, he became quite un-
conscious of where he was, kicked off his shoes,
tilted back his chair, and propped his “ stocking-
feet ”on the mahogany. All that the shocked
Marquis could say was, “Shank God, the ladish
have left.”
Obit. At Vale Head, Molong,
N.S. W., through an accident, Henry Samuel
Marsden Betts, grandson of parson Mars-
den of the “System” time. Vale Head
was a grant to and was farmed by the par-
son, per deputy. The house in its earlier
days was a pub., kept by Cobden, brother
of the Corn Law reformer; he lies buried
in a neglected spot on the bank of the
Molong river.
A certain modeller, who did well out
of his artistic likeness of old-man Parkes on
a plaque which sold freely, got Premier
Reid to give him a sitting, and modelled
a companion-plaque of Parkes’s successor.
But the poor artist knew nothing of poli-
tics, and so made the mistake of offering
his work for sale in the wrong quarters.
Utterly depressed by the receptions he
got, he turned up at a Government office
with—“No more Giorgio Reida. He no
sella at all. I offer ze plaque eferywhere,
and they all wanta kicka mea.”
One Australian gaol-doctor who has always
been agog for information from prisoners, cease-
lessly plied with questions a certain smart youth
when the latter was under remand for murder,
introducing each “ loaded ” query with some such
formula as—“ In the interests of medical science,
can you tell me? ’’ or “ As a medical man I would
like to know. ” For a while the accused fenced
with him, but the sneaky Sawbones was not to be
denied, and was finally referred by the youth to
“my solicitor.” Needless to say, the doctor’s
curiosity had to remain unsatisfied.

back to her friends.

Gossiped in Adelaide that Judge Bundey’s
probable early retirement will leave an opening
for John Downer to step up There has not been
a vacant S.A. judgeship since the popular Bun-
dey s appointment in 1884.
The other afternoon, Rev. Dr. Roseby, on
leaving Farmer’s baths, Sydney Domain, found
that his watch, worth .£5O, had disappeared. Soinl
scoundrel has probably taken and sold it and
given the proceeds to the poor.
How many Bendigo pioneers are left ?
Another has just gone—Richard Smith, who
quitted law to dig for gold, and with some of the
proceeds bought a few acres in what is now the
heart of Bendigo. Then he quitted mining for
winemaking, left that for broking, and at 70 en-
rolled as a student in assaying, etc., at the local
ochool of Mines. He got his “pass ’’ certificate,
too.
N.S.W. Justice Mathew Henry Stephen,
now in his 71st year and in indifferent health, was
the first native of Australia called to the Aus-
tralian Bar. He was born at Hobart, and began
money-earning as associate to his father, Alfred
Stephen, at the age of 17. He received no Uni-
versity education, but studied classics under the
famous Cape, in what is now Sydney Grammar
School.
Forrest (YV.A.) and Braddon (Tas.) are
the only Australian Premiers entitled to draw
pensions when they fall from political grace. Both
will draw on Imperial warrant—Braddon for ser-
vices abroad, and Forrest for services in W.A. ere
Responsible Government was granted. The other
Australian Premiers will, when the evil day
comes, have to get back into collar and tackle the
world anew.
A Winimera district (Vic.) curiosity died
lately—Harry Franklin. He once held a good
slice of land just outside Warracknabeal. Having
no relatives bandy, and desiring that he should
leave no property behind him, it was his life
problem to make the proceeds from the sale of
the land just “ see him out.” He sold bit by bit;
if he got the idea that ho was booked for a long
life, he lived sparingly ; if he got that tired feel-
ing, he began to live as riotously as a hermit can.
In the end he didn’t make a thorough good job
of it; for when they took him to the hospital to
die, he still had his house and a patch near it.
When he wasn’t figuring out the probabilities of
the case Franklin was mostly reading Greek
authors in the original.
W. P. MacGregor, just deceased, for many
years chairman of B.H. Proprietary, personally
fixed up the first two of the three strikes which
the Barrier has had. The miners liked dealing
with him ; he was bluff, would drink whisky with
their representatives, and could box and “ wrastle”
and that went a long way. After promptly
settling the first strike, he invited the burly
Sleath, then miners’ president, to a bout; they
began it, but soon adjourned for drinks. ’ Mac-
Gregor was still on the board during the disas-
trously-prolonged strike of ’92, but was by that
time taking little part in the management. After
floating Silverton Tramway Co.’s Bill through
N.S.W. Parliament, he at once left the House,
taking with hitn an abiding contempt for politi-
cians ; but, then, he had only personal experience
of the unpaid variety—of the sort unpaid by the
State, anyhow. He made but one speech to his
constituents j that lasted about 10 minutes—and
was about anything but the burning political
questions of the moment.
Ex-Goveruor Bowen, whose
death at 78 is cabled, has stamped
his name, or that of his first wife on
many parts of Australia. Bowen
town, Bowen Creek, Bowen Mount;
Roma (his wife’s maiden name—she
was daughter of Count Roma, a
Greek senator); Diamantina, that
lady’s Christian name; and others.
The ex-Governor married, lately, a
Mrs. White, widow of a clergyman
and daughter of Thomas Luby, the
well-known Dean of Trinity College,
Dublin. Poor old Bowen ! Q.
backblocks tell no end of stories
(most apocryphal) about him, and
there are some lovely Melbourne
yarns about his adventures in quest
of petticoats. One in particular,
about a certain fracas in the Botani-
cal Gardens, is too well-known to
need printing.
It does not follow that because Indian
healers and quack doctors were turned away
in crowds from Kipling’s house in New
York, they had no reason to expect a wel-
come. As a matter of fact, Kipling had
immense faith both in the colored man’s
medicine and in his occult science. When in
Melb. he used to tell fortunes by the hand, and
tell them marvellously well; also describe strange
people’s pasts. When questioned upon this re-
markable power he used to reply : “I come from
India, you know. What I do is nothing, abso-
lutely nothing, to what a clever Hindoo can
accomplish.”

The late
David Blair
was one of a
band of a dozen young
men whom Dr. Lang im-
ported about 1850 for the
purpose of spreading cul-
ture and refinement over
Australia. Mr. Blair came
to N.S. W. ; but the knowing
young Irishman quickly dis-
covered either that it was hopeless to
cultivate the Cornstalk, or that it was
unnecessary to gild refined gold, so he
departed soon after to Melbourne, where
his subsequent career is a familiar story.
In illustration of his extraordinary energy and
capacity for grappling with several subjects at
once, the following is told by a personal friend of
his. While travelling in a railway carriage one
day with Mr. Blair, the latter bought a Ballarat
paper from a newsboy, opened it and exclaimed,
“Good! That’s seven.” “Seven what?” asked
his friend. “ Seven leading-articles by me to-day
in different Victorian newspapers,” was the proud
reply.
Isaac Hart, who died the other day, was
about the last survivor of Melbourne’s early
merchant capitalists. He is said to have landed
at Port Phillip in 1840, and to have retired from
business 14 years later with a big pile.
Sir John Cecil Read, Bart., died in Syd-
ney last week, aged 79. He had in his time dis-
pensed much hospitality, in his capacity as
Governor of Darlinghurst Gaol. He was a
humane, placid old gentleman, quite unspoilt by
his villainous surroundings ; but The Bulletin
could never quite size-up the strength of his
claim to a baronetcy, in that the one to which he
aspired seems to have become extinct long ago—
so extinct that an ancestor of deceased failed in a
legal attempt to revive it. Also, the old gentle-
man’s name is not given among the list of
baronets in the books generally consulted on such
subjects.

PILSENER LAGER BIER
Aboriginalities.
“ A.D.” : It was a lonely bullock-team
swearing at itself that made me wait while it
came over the bridge. “Whoa back, Darkie !
Gid up ! Whoa yer! !! ” The dray swung
Serenely over the joints of the bridge, keeping well
in the middle of the road. Not a sign of a driver
anywhere ! My wonderment turned to positive
awe at the sight of this phantom dray that cursed
and bully-damned itself over hard ruts and casual
bridges. Also, there wasn’t a tree or a bush
handy that might cover a ventriloquial bullocky.
A piercing whistle halted the lean, black team
within a yard of me. Peeped over into the dray
and saw a green African parrot cocking an inquisi-
tive eye at the sun. When I nodded and asked if
he wasn’t ashamed of hisself for swearing at the
tired “plates of meat,” he preened himself furi-
ously and screamed a discourteous blanky ; after
which he settled himself on the seat of the dray
and unloosed the vials of his wrath on the sweat-
ing team. Drawing aside, I permitted the beasts
of the field and the bird of the air to pass. Later
on a travelling cocky informed me that the dray
and parrot were owned by a disreputable sleeper-
getter named Jim, who stayed at the pub washing
hisself in beer while the poor, neglected bird took
his place on the dray and said things to the
bullocks.
* * * *
About 18 months ago the editor of a way-
back Vic. paper was misinformed of the death of
a far-out mallee farmer, an old identity named
Peter A . Peter’s history, incorporated with
that of the district and of the paper, was there-
upon hashed up in obituary-form, and a marked,
black-edged copy posted to the supposed widow.
Peter himself received the paper from the post
and was the first to read the harrowing account of
his own alleged illness and sacredly peaceful
demise. The effect was disastrous; he moped
around the pubs, for a few days “ like a lost soul,”
and one evening galloped out of town with a
heavy cargo of liquor aboard and a copy of the
fatal paper in his pocket, to be seen no more alive.
Next morning the horse reached home with the
rider’s dead body dangling from the stirrup.

The boatmen were pulling along the coast, and, the
bovs being knocked up, I stayed one night at the late
Jackv Sibley’s. We were made very welcome, and
ushered into the main room of the large thatched build-
ing. Tied to the roof-pole, a strapping native woman was
blubbering like a child. Seeing my look of surprise,
Jackv explained : “That’s my last wife. She wants to
run away, but I’m damned if she does—too much trouble
going after them. You see, my seventh cleared out last
week, and I can’t get her back '; but that’s all right, for I
bought her off a chief, and according to their law he has
to return her or her nearest female relative; that’s a
married sister, but I’ll have her—it’s the Law. “ You’ve
got a large familyV” I suggested, tentatively. “Four-
teen wives, all good workers. What’s a man to do for
labor in these parts if he don’t get women ? ”

“ Olaf Trygvasen ” re backblocks morality:
“ Central ” says (B. 25/2/’99) that he finds as a rule
Germans exhibit more laxity as to morals and decency
than Australians or Britishers, and in support relates how
parents and children of Germans sleep in one room up to
the age of 2 o or so—how the women dress in one garment
only _ and that he was once offered a bed, if he did not
m ; nd B i ee ping with the children of the house—a boy of 7,
ftnd a .j of 10 Although no Germanphile myself, Ido
not ]ike tQ see them libelled unju stly. To the pure every-
p Ure> hence the innocent and good-natured
German woman—ignorant of the impure and hypocritical
soul (?) that dwelt in “ Central’s ” body—offered him a bed
occupied by her son and daughter. Sleeping in one
room is undoubtedly reprehensible-apart from the moral
aspect of the case; but how many thousands of pool
Australians allow their boys and girls to sleep together in
one room, long after puberty, although they occupy
separate rooms themselves, and thus have no oversight
over their children at night. Look at the Maoris in the
King Country, and where they have not been con-
taminated by the Britishers. Several families often sleep
in one long room together, and “ immorality is unknown,
The references made by “Central to the clothing ot
German women show that to the impure everything is
impure ; no wonder crime is rampant in the bush while
such as “ Central ” are allowed to roam at large without
handcuffs and leg-irons.
WILD CAT ITEM.—WAITING FOR A RISE.
“ 0.P.” : “Old Forty” kept a shanty
atYarrawanga (Murray River) in the 60’s, and
boasted that no one with a cheque once within
reasonable range ever evaded him. On one occa-
sion a man named Bill was reported as under way
from a neighboring station with a three-figured
cheque. A later report announced Bill’s having
been sighted off Mother Burke’s, going strong.
Forty then bet a fiver with Bill’s overseer, who
had brought the report, that Bill would be no ex-
ception to his rule. Presently Bill was seen
rounding the bend, and Forty got behind the
door and peeped. Bill came on, looking neither
to right nor left. Bill was passing. Bill had
passed ! Forty rushed out and over, and down
went Bill with a thud. Slowly then he rose with
murder in his eye. Forty stood . apparently
astounded for a moment, and then cried, “ Good
Gawd !—why, it’s you, Bill. .Thought it was a
joker that owed me a fiver. Orful sorry, ole
man. Cumnavadrink.” And that was the finan-
cial end of Bill.

“ Terrick ” tells a
tracking story :
The Gatton outrage re-
calls an experience with
“ trackers.” I was taking
a mob of horses along the
Condamine, and, as usual,
allowed the pack-horse to
trot on ahead with the
others. They turned round
a clump of brigalow, and
when the darkie and I got
there they had vanished.
Attempted to trail them,
but found place one mass
of horse - tracks, old and new, for miles. So we
separated, to each look in a different direction. After
an all-day’s futile hunt, we went to a neighboring’
station for the night, and next morning, after a couple of
hours, came across the mob. The pack was underneath
the horse, and much damaged by stumps, but the tucker
was all right, so we boiled the billy. Were camping after
our meal when seven policemen appeared—only two in
uniform, and one of those a tracker. It appeared that,
on previous evening, a cockie had seen, as he thought, a
horse with a man dragging along underneath, and imme-
diately rode into the township and informed the police.
The conclusion was jumped to that the supposed man
was a young fellow who had started out some hours before
on a lively horse. The police accordingly set out with a
tracker, but not till next day did the latter drop on to a
bit of woollen stuff on a stick with horse-tracks on each
side. Another couple of hours’ tedious tracking brought
them to where the horse had evidently been stopped by
somebody, for there was the mark of a boot-heel, and,
further on, they came to where several horses and men
had been standing. Tracks then struck out in a straight
line and joined a lot more, and half-a-mile further on they
found _ u J s j That cockie basn > t heard the ]aBt 0 f that
•‘ man ”yet.

They passed the Helds of tillage,
And safely reached the village—
What a disappointment, no lights were in the hall;
For through the sports and races,
And “ hops” at other places,
The promoters decided to postpone the ball.
They did not long remain
Till they homewards turned again,
But, alas, their home they were doomed to see no more ;
They were murdered on the way,
There skull 3 broken where they lay,
And even their poor horse was lying in his gore.
Some demons them awaited,
And this crime perpetrated,
The hands of the poor girls behind their backs were tied;
Their bodies badly mangled,
’Twas shown, too, they were strangled,
And grossly insulted some time before they died.
Their mother, when they brought her
To this sad field of slaughter,
Went, first to the elder girl and addressed the dead.
Her words, I well remember,
Were only six in number :
“ Norah, my dear, who murdered you ?” sadly she said.
Then in midst of deep despair
She kneels down and says a prayer,
Bending over the poor lifeless form where it lay ;
To the others did the same,
Each in their turn, while her frame
Was all trembling just as if she would faint away.
’Tis God in whom our trust is,
Before the seat of justice
We pray the authors of this crime shall soon appear.
May their guilty hearts lead them
To where they’ll lose their freedom,
That they may be wafted to another sphere.

“Don’t associate with other dead-beats.”
“Kotobuki,” or “Good Luck,” souvenirs
in “ Geisha ” art jewellery are the latest thing out
in the way of elegant luxuries. The Goldsmiths’
and Silversmiths’ Alliance, of 90King-st., Sydney,
is always first in the field with the very latest and
up-to-date fashions. The window display made
by the Allianoe has now an additional attraction
in the shape of the “ Geisha ” novelties, specially
imported and unprocurable elsewhere in Australia.
They arrive at an opportune time, the opera being
just introduced to Sydney. The jewellery is in
quaint Japanese style, and is elegant, artistic, and
inexpensive.*

WHITE LABEL LAGER.
Slab of mixed Rougemont
and zoology from London paper :
In N.S.W. on the Murrumbidgee
Kiver there dwells a German selector,
who, not having much capital to put a
fence round his selection to keep off
kangaroos from eating his corn of a
moonlight night, used to sit on the
stump of a tree watching his oppor-
tunity for a shot at any kangaroos that
came in his way. But finding powder
and shot did not keep them away, he
resolved to work upon a different plan,
so accordingly set a number of traps,
which he visited every morning. Going
his usual round one morning he found
in one of the traps one of his unwel-
come night visilors, a big kangaroo.
Not knowing in what way to serve his
revenge, he put it in a small yard, and
every morning he brought it grass,
water, and bits of bread. As days
rolled by the selector began to look
upon his captive as a pet instead of
an enemy. When the winter came,
the kangaroo suffered with cold ; the
selector, w'ho h.id been a soldier in
his country, happened to have a sol-
dier’s suit, which was of no use to him,
and he made of it a kind of a suit
for the kangaroo, who, no doubt,
wondered what was coming next. One
morning, as the selector had just
finished giving his pet its morning
meal, he saw a large mob of kan- -
garoos feeding at some distance. A
happy thought came into his head,
which he immediately put into prac-
tice. He put a large bell round the
kangaroo’s neck, and let it go, with
the result that the kangaroo made for
the mob as fast as possible. The mob,
hearing the noise of the hell, upon
turning round, were terrified at the
sight, arid made quick their exit.
Bjiue days afterwards, while the selec-
tor was standing at his gate, he saw
the same mob of kangaroos pass at an
awful rate, followed in the rear by the
one in the soldier’s suit, with the bell
still dangling round its neck. He
was never bothered by kangaroos
again.
The Bulletin perfectly remem-
bers that ’roo. He is the fellow
that used, to keep us awake at
nights with his eternal warbling
Of “The Watch on the Rhine.”
The M,‘Cooey on albinism in Australia :
Dear Bulletin.— Can anybody account for the fact that
no other country is as rich in albinos as Australia ? Pre-
natal influence anything- to do with albinism ? Which is
not in Australia a thing- of to-day or yesterday. It was
common amongst N.S. VV. aborigines in the days of Banks
and bolander; and I can remember seeing a partial
albino aborigine in Burragorang Valley nearly 40 years
ago. Imb Bulletin, about 10 years ago, had much to say
about the Westralian police mistaking an albino gin for a
capti\ e white woman ; and allowing her, while conveying
her to the nearest settlement, to be thrown from horse-
back and killed. Capt. Cook, Sir Joseph Banks, Solander,
Latham, Grey, all speak of albinism as common among
Australian aborigines. Amongst birds and animals
it is so very frequent as to be scarcely interesting.
Amongst Australian reptiles, however, albinism is very
rare.- I have heard of a'bino snakes; but can anyone
produce an example? No use talking about the white
snakes of the Lower Macquarie. I have caught dozens
of them, and found them to be only abnormal forms of
the common brown snake. Nor must partial albinism be
attributed to the lace lizard, the transverse white bands
being normal in the young of this and other Australian
1 eptiles. In 89, at Germanton (N.S. W.), I saw an albino
tortoise with pure white carapace, head, neck, and feet,
the plastron also being white, tinged with yellow.—ll J
M‘Cooey. “ J

TO ARTISTIC CONTRIBUTORS.
Louis M. : Will reproduce one direct; one other
will serve as suggestion George Ralston : You don’t
say what it was, or when it appeared. Memo, posted
Bert de M. : Such old wheezes quite unavailable.
The question of review copies has been
causing heart-burning lately (says The Book-
fellow). Nearly all the metropolitan papers
receive a very precarious supply from the pub-
lishers ; and in order to eke out notices some of
them are glad to rely on booksellers' bounties.
That is bad ; for the bookseller has a keen eye to
his own interest, and is accustomed, when there
is dead stock on hand, to foist a copy of the Awful
Unsaleable upon the reviewer, as a bonus for
giving him a copy of the Fresh Saleable. “And
try and give us a real c/ood notice now!” We
have some knowledge in Australia of the class of
firms with a reputation for buying up book-
sellers’ remainders in London ; or surplus
copies of miss-fire novels ; at say Gd. each for 500
or so, and retailing them to a deluded public at
anything over 3s. Gd., thanks to a booming notice
by a nobbled reviewer. It’s “business”; but a
little rough on the people who see no difference
between a newspaper “criticism” and a Gospel
affirmed by two first-rate miracles and a third-rate
spook. Yet if the reviewer isn’t amenable, the
chances are that “ our only advance copy ” of the
next big book will go to the rival paper —which
isn’t pleasant. So the reviewer generally is
amenable ; and until lately he had the consolation
of collaring the books. But now some booksellers
are getting into a habit of lendinu a book for
review (“and please mention our name pro-
minently”), with an injunction to return at once
“ because the other paper wants it before Satur-
day.” The ox treads out their corn, and is
muzzled in defiance of Holy Writ. No wonder
book-selling pays.
Young M‘Kain, who shot himself and
wife, at Palmerston North, M.L. (photos, of the
pair are printed in this issue of The Bulletin),
after a three weeks’ honeymoon trip, may be
taken as an interesting specimen of the sex-
lunatic. Prior to the marriage he had made
some threats of shooting; his father said at the
inquest “he would not have been surprised had
the tragedy happened before marriage ” —on
account of the opposition of his wife’s mother to
the wedding. The bridegroom was young, vigor-
ous and willing to work, so that, although he had
come to the end of his money, he probably did
not “ shuffle off ” because of dread of want or
hardship. M‘Kain appears to have been just the
sort of man to go crazy over a woman, to go mad
with excess of love, and then form a fixed idea
that the “family feud ” over their wedding would
cause perpetual unhappiness ; from this to the
decision to kill his bride and himself was an easy
transition. His strong will would enable him to
persuade the wife in her tears that death was
better than life. Evidently the plan agreed upon
was that he should shoot her while she was asleep.
This, at any rate, he did, killing her instantly,
with a placid expression on her face. A second
shot into her head to make sure; an interval of
20 minutes (and what a 20 minutes ! for he pro-
bably realised after the act what he had not done
before, and spent those minutes in an agony that
left its impress upon his fearfully distorted
features), and then a sure bullet sent him in pur-
suit of his bride of three weeks into the Unknown.
Their dying request that they might be buried
one over the other was disregarded by the respec-
tive parents ! Such was the end of the love-idyll
in which a bride of 19 years gave herself trust-
fully up to sacrifice decreed by her sternly-deter-
mined husband of 22.


THE CAPITAL QUESTION.— AT GOSHEN FLAT.
At a public meeting held at Goshen Flat last week Alderman Panslab, J.P., moved the first and only resolution, viz.-"Whereas Goshen Flat, the Garden spot of New South Wales, ain't eligible to
be the Capital of Federated Australia owin' to bein' on the 150th degree of Longitood an' consekently too cluss to Sydney, it is hereby resolved that the 150th degree be voted be voted out of order and be dug up
and took away.” Seconded by Bullhide Joe and carried unanimously.

MELBOURNIAN A.


Concerning a Queensland general-election
episode :
Charles Powers has just consented to stand for Bunda-
berpf—-in the Dieksonian interest. The decision is in-
teresting from many standpoints. In the first place it,
indicates the utter hopelessness (in Power’s opinion) of
any decided success on the part of the so-called Oppo-
sitionists. Whether Drake, Groom and co. return with
one or two additional supporters or not Powers sees
quite clearly that portfolios will be the gift either of the
Dieksonian majority or the Labor majority, as the case
may be ; and, like a wise man, he determines to;draw his
sword and fight alongside the big guns, long purses, and
big battalions. Charles has addressed the electors of
Bundaberg in a speech eminently Democratic —and has
wound up his Democratic professions with the cheering
assertion that he intends to support the Dickson Ministry
' certainly a remarkable attitude for any Democrat. It
would be interesting to know how far the desire for ven-
geance on the Laborites who drove him out of politics a
few years ago has stimulated Powers to face Glassey on
the hustings. In any case, the present turn of events
only exemplifies the utter spinelessness of Powers’ demo-
cracy. Years and years ago, when that amiable politician
brought in a most commendable measure for the Simpli-
fication of Legal Procedure, it was remarked : “ It took
him seven years to hatch it, and he dropped it in seven
days.” Apparently his democracy is shed just as easily
and almost as speedily.
A question : Why should we import any mineral
waters when we have at Ballimore, near Dubbo, N.S. VV.,
the “ Zetz ” Natural Mineral Water Spring, yielding daily
thousands of gallons of naturally effervescing water,’con-
taining all the health-giving properties of the best im-
ported waters ? Zetz Spa ranks undoubtedly first as’a
Table Water. By its strong alkaline properties it abso-
lutely kills Uric Acid, the cause of so many complaints,
and as an every-day beverage is more pleasant than any
other. Try it with your meals; it positively cures indi-
gestion ;or with your stimulants. You never will take
any other.*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him.. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing Extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Arthur Russell, American dentist, 8 Bourk e-
street E., Melb., makes beautiful teeth or teeth
beautiful, whichever is necessary. Also painless
operations.*

The Rofe Case.
“ T. E. Rofe ” discourses concerning his well-
adverjfsed case :
Re article, “ Suffield and the Other Man,” in Bulletin,
11/2/’99, I will thank you to publish the following from
Thomas Ernest Rofe, “the other man.” Personally I
am unable, oving to not knowing the facts in Suffikld’s
case, to say whether there are huge discrepancies between
my case and his; but I sincerely hope, if his case ie in
anv way similar to mine so far as the injustice done me is
concerned, that justice will be speedily done him. Before
replying to your article, paragraph by paragraph, permit
me to say that fresh evidence uus adduced in support of
my application for an enquiry, and this fresh evidence,
as also the evidence adduced at my enquiry, I am pre-
pared to lend you, provided you will undertake to publish
same in your paper, because, so far as I am concerned,
I court the fullest enquiry and publicity, knowing the
result must be the complete vindication of my character,
the integrity of which I value above an3’thing else
in this world —a character which I can honestly say,
outside of this vile and unsustained charge, is unim-
peached. You say that Suffield was convicted on
the evidence of one girl, who contradicted herself on
every point. Now, I ask you to peruse the evidence taken
at Glebe police-court, Mrs. Butler v. Butler (a case
wherein Mrs. Butler charged her husband, the day after
the occurrence of that eventful night, with wife deser-
tion, having him arrested, and, while under arrest, got
possession of the key from the lockup-keeper, and
cleared the house of everything except Butler’s own
personal apparel), the divorce-suit, Butler v. Butler,
Mrs. Butler v. Williams, committal and trial of Butler,
Davis and Peake, committal and trial of myself, and say
whether your remarks do not equally apply to my case. I
am prepared to go fully into the different statements
made by witnesses (upon whose testimony I was con-
victed) at each of the above trials, and show how they
differ in respect to most material matters.
Rofe, you say, was convicted on convincing evidence,
and his own counsel threw up the sponge. I deny that
I was convicted on convincing evidence, nor did my
counsel throw up the sponge, as you say. My counsel
said I was innocent and still say that I am ; in fact, if
you will take the trouble to peruse the letter of the Hon.
Mr. Heydon, Q.C., written to the Minister for Justice, in
Nov., ’95, you will see therein stated that he believed I
was innocent, and is glad that Mr. Crick, one of the
solicitors acting in my defence, also agreed with him in
that belief. Mr. Heydon I have seen since the decision of
the Executive [to let the conviction stand] was an-
nounced, and he says now that his opinion is still the
same. Hon. C. E. Pilcher, Q.C., also says I am innocent
of any criminality ; Mr. Barton, Q.C., I am assured, says
the same. Dr. Coghlan and Mr. Augustus James, barris-
ters-at-law r , say I am innocent; and so does Mr. Levien.
There has been, and is still, a grave doubt about my
guilt, and such doubt is supported by the opinions of
every one of my counsel and solicitors above-named,
who have fully perused the papers in the case ; and, by-
the-bye, I may state that the papers found on me in con-
nection with the case were very voluminous, the instruc-
tion papers, which contained chronologically, day by day,
my doings in the case comprised some three or four sheets
of fcp. closely written, two columns on each side. All my
papers were intact. If I had been a conspirator, as I am
alleged to be, would I have kept, or preserved, a complete
record of the case, and my doings therein, when I could,
without raising any suspicion against me, have destroyed,
burnt, or made away with them ? Would I have shown
these, my papers, to the Crown Prosecutor, and have
offered to leave them with him? Would I have volun-
tarily produced them in Court, when I was not even sub-
p®naed~to do so ? No, no, I say, and I believe anyone
who looks at the case in an unbiassed light will say the
same. I am prepared to go fully into the facts, and I feel
convinced the outcome will be in your and the public’s
opinion the complete vindication of _my character from
as vile a crime as anyone could commit.
You say that I got my enquiry, yet Suffi-buD was told
that he could not get his without fresh evidence.
Strange, yet true, this was the very reply I received on
3rd March, '9B, to a letter of mine to the then Minister of
Justice, wherein he stated that no enquiry could be
granted in my case unless I adduced fresh evidence, and
this evidence, after some considerable trouble, I obtained,
and comprised some 195 sheets of foolscap closely typed.
This fresh evidence was forwarded the Minister, and re-
turned by him, owing, as he stated, to its containing a
lot of matter immaterial. I, personally, refused to re-
duce the fresh evidence, and handed same, with the
Minister’s letters, to Mr. Augustus Jambs, and he himself
eliminated, without any assistance from me, the so-called
immaterial evidenoe. The evidence as reduced was sworn
to, as also was a declaration by myself, a letter was signed
by Messrs. Barton, Q. 0., Heydon, Q.O.,and Dr. Coghlan,
my counsel at my trial, stating that I particularly de-
sired the prisoners, Butler and Davis, called as wit-
nesses. But they, my counsel, refused to do so, and Dr.
Coghlan added thereto that he, personally, knew that I
was extremely anxious that the prisoners should be called,
and even after they, my counsel, had refused to, I had
writs of habeas corpus issued to compel their attendance,
in the hope that my counsel would change their mind and
call the prisoners. The above documents, together with a
petition signed by myself, were forwarded to the Minister
for Justice, and after some considerable time the enquiry
was granted. Copies of these papers I am prepared to
hand you for publication, so that the public can see that
there were strong grounds for the enquiry, and a very
grave doubt as to my guilt, if not positive proof of my
innocence.
As to the order suppressing the evidence : I did not
desire this order, and again say I court full publicity,
and hope you will give me the same publicity as you gave
Suffield. I have been treated in the same way as Suffield,
but, fortunately, I was able to get fresh evidence which
certainly supported my contention that I was and am an
innocent man
I note your closing remarks anent Dean, and if ever a
man cursed Dean I did and do. My trial, unfortunately,
took place while the whole country were highly inflamed
against the legal profession, and the Dean case of con-
spiracy was set down for trial. To that fact I
can account for the erroneous verdict returned against
me, coupled with, in my opinion, the mistake
made, against my express wish, of not calling
Butler and Davis at my trial. To show you how
important this non-calling was, I will briefly refer to the
address of the Crown Prosecutor to the jury after the
evidence was closed, his opening remarks being : “Gentle-
men of the jury, the prisoner Rofe is charged with con-
spiring with Butler, Davis, and Peake, &c., &c. . . . and
these very men, Butler and Davis, with whom Rofe is
charged, are actually under that dock waiting to be called,
yet the prisoner has not dared to call them. Why ? Be-
cause he knows they would incriminate him.” The Judge
in his summing-up also referred to the fact of Butler and
Davis not being called by me to give evidence. Very
hard on me, seeing that I wanted them called and fought
hard to have them called, but without success, and that
I had been the means of having these men under the
dock by reason of my w'rits of habeas corpus. I think
you, after perusing this and the papers on the facts,
will come to the conclusion that there are no two laws,
one for rich and one for poor; but you will agree with
me, and many others, that the decision of the Executive
should have been different to tvhat it has been in my case,
and I believe it would have been different only for that
cry about the rich and poor. In fact, I would rather be a
poor man with my character than possess no character
and yet own the wealth of Crcesus. In conclusion, my
candid opinion is : God help the unfortunate man who
happens to be wrongly convicted, as I have been ; because
he has no chance of ever clearing himself, no matter how
clear any fresh evidence adduced may be.
[The Bulletin publishes this letter because it is de-
sirous that Mr. T. E. Rofe should have an opportunity of
stating his views of his own case—an opportunity which
might elsewhere be denied him. The whole of the fore-
going letter could, however, easily be summarised in a
single paragraph. To publish a column so utterly devoid
of point wrings tears of blood from The Bulletin.]

Where is the Federal capital to be ? This
is certain, wherever the F. capital may be planted
the capital place for acquiring a radiant exterior,
such as the Federal capital in all the glories of its
dazzling new raiment might envy, will still be
the London and American Supply Stores, Bourke-
street, Melb. Write for catalogue of stylish
suits, head, neck, foot-wear, &c. Our capital rule
is : Prices moderate, satisfaction immoderate.
Premier Skddon’s remarks against the con-
tinuation of the M.L. Grand Jury system as a
class institution would have carried more weight
had they not followed a certain expression of
opinion from the Grand Jury at last Welling-
ton sessions. In connection with the indictment
against Capt. Allman, charged with false re-
presentations in re an examination for a master
mariner’s certificate, the jury notified its
“strong disapproval of the evident interference
by the Minister of Marine with the conduct
of the routine of the examination forming the
subject of the indictment.” The foreman of
the Graud Jury was Mr. Foster, chairman of
the B.N.Z. Asseis-Realisation Board (in which,
by the way, the Govt, is largely interested,
and on which it has a representative), and
the Minister implicated is Hall-Jones, one
of the weaklings added to make up for the loss
of two of the strong men of the Seddon
Ministry— Ward and Reeves. But, neverthe-
less, Seddon is right about the working of the
Grand Jury system. There are on record
flagrant cases where men of prominent
social position have escaped prosecution for
fraudulent bankruptcy, &c., through their
friends’ judicious manipulation of the Grand
Jurymen. In fact, the thing got so bad that
the Govt, stepped in and prohibited the names
of Grand Jurors from being published in ad-
vance as had been customary, in order, as far
as possible, to prevent their being “nobbled.”
Seddon tried to abolish the juries last year,
but he went too far for the Conservatives by
lumping together grand and special juries.
Business people were alarmed at the suggestion
to place intricate book-keeping accounts before
common jurors, and the “ Lords ” struck out
the chief clauses.
F. Croson writes :
Your “ Blackmailing in Albert Park” par. (8., 14/l/’99)
is pretty, and in accordance with eternal fitness, but it
doesn’t always pan out that way. Recently a couple
were surprised by two men who threatened arrest, then
demanded hush-money. This is the man’s yarn, reported
to the police subsequently, and a very choice and valiant
sort of youth it shows him to be. He handed over 65.,
and was himself released, leaving the girl still in the
custody of the men. He apparently stayed near at hand,
and, finding the blackmailers were proceeding to ill-treat
his companion, demanded that they should both be taken
to the watch-house, whereupon the men decamped. That
is his narrative. The men have not yet been heard from ;
neither are they likely to be. On previous evening another
couple were surprised in a public garden by two con-
stables, employed for the purpose. Their names were taken,
by separate interrogation, and they were then threatened
with arrest, but the pleading of the girl and the sooth-
ing effect of £2 overcame the officers’ stern sense of duty.
The arresters in this case were not blackmailers posing as
policemen, but constables still in the force. Is there any
connection between the two cases ? As further evidence
of the disorganisation existing in the Melb. police force,
take the case of the constable lately dismissed—after a
private inquiry—who was suspected of being mixed up in
robberies committed by a night watchman in the same
suburb, and in whose possession were said to have been
found alleged stolen goods for which he refused to account.
A local resident had previously laid a charge against this
man, yet not until the watchman was convicted was any
action taken, and even then it merely resulted in aforesaid
private inquiry and dismissal. The main cause of this
disorganisation and degeneration is the retention of old,
worn-out, and often incapable officials to save the expense
of their pensions. Meanwhile the younger men are kept
back, the force deteriorates, and the public suffers.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Premier Reid rode for a fall —the fall of
his followers.
Barton’s luck, dead out for many months,
is rushing in at last. Nobody is attacking him
just now.
The Q. elections seem to be turning on
the colored-alien question. Dickson’s Kanaka
groom may be a blessing to the Piebald province.
Archbishop O’Keily has publicly disap-
proved of the candidature of Southern Cross
(Adelaide R.C. organ) Editor Denny for W.
Adelaide against Premier Kingston and Labor-
leader Batchelor. He does not want to offend
either Ministry or Labor-party ; also he regards
Denny as too young for Parliament yet.
John Downer, who affects to lead what is
left of S.A. Opposition, has decided to try for
Parliament again next month, despite his recent
announcement of withdrawal. He fears that
otherwise "he will be forgotten ere the Federal
election, and he dearly desires to renew his
political youth in the Federal Parliament.
The futility of patriot Haynes’ double-
headed referendum ! “Will you or will you not
take this?” was the referendum proper. Haynes
wanted it to be, Whether you will take this or
that ? But numerous batches of equally pure
patriots wanted different thats, and were as much
entitled to have them put under the referendum
as was John.
Brisbane Telegraph is about the meanest
rag south of the equator. For some time it has
been voluminously reporting the inane tommy-rot
of Parliamentary candidates of all brands ; but
when the leader of the Independent Opposition
—J. G. Drake, certainly one of the most
creditable men in the public life of Leperland—
addressed his constituents the miserable Tele-
graph did not even mention the fact in a one-inch
paragraph. Brisbane conservative Courier, on the
other hand, gave the popular democrat a good two-
column report. And the Telegraph calls itself a
“Liberal Journal ” ! There is a big opening for
a brainy and inoderately-honest evening paper in
Brisbane.
That wild provmcial-
ist, M‘Laughlin, of Ra-
leigh, told N.S.W. Parlia-
ment, during the recent
Federal debate, that the
Melbourne people were so
sure that they would keep
the Federal capital there
permanently that the Melb.
landlords were already giv-
ing their tenants notice to
quit or submit to a rise in
rents. Then Henry Cope-
land rose and explained
that it was he who had
{jiven the shriekful Mac his
information. But what he
had said was that he had
met one Melbourne man—
only one—whose landlord
was going to raise his rent
when the lease expired.
And the solitary person
didn’t know why his rent
was going to be raised, but
thought that perhaps Federation had
something to do with it. M'Laughlin
didn’t look in the least ashamed of
having his huge invention shown up in
this fashion.
There was a scandalous example
of magisterial incompetence the other
day at Blackall (Q.). The Revision
Court struck off 112 names from the elec-
toral list on the ground that residence quali-
fications were defective. Several of the dis-
qualified appealed on the ground that they had
resided continuously in the electorate, though not
always in the same portion thereof, for the
requisite time. Result the Supreme Court
ordered the names of the parties appealing to be
restored to the roll. And the remainder—over 100
persons—not having the money to appeal are dis-
franchised for the coming elections. If Q. Govt,
win back this seat it can confidently attribute its
gain to the dirty trick above narrated.
The chief interest of Q. elections lies in
the first batch. Excitement circles mainly round
the Bundaberg, Toowong, Valley and Clermont
campaigns—all close fights and of vital import to
the two great parties concerned. The Central
District is sure to go almost solid for Labor-cum-
Separation, and the North will probably remain
much as before.
In face of Premier Dickson’s repeated
assertions that the gate is closed on the Jap., how
comes it that Torres Straits Pilot reported
the landing of 40 Japanese on Thursday Island
some six weeks ago? ... Is it a fact that the re-
strictions on immigration from Japan, about which
Q. Govt, has made so much brag and bounce, are
restrictions on formally-indented labor only ?
Dreary Dickson, Premier of Queensland,
is going to start his immigration policy by intro-
ducing domestic servants and agricultural laborers
into the over-stocked labor-market at the rate of
100 a month. Even if these people were really
wanted, it would merely mean that the Dickson
crowd had managed to make Queensland a place
that people would only come to when they got
paid for coming.
Q. Opposition-party is making a stand to
capture one of the Brisbane seats, and has put up
a decidedly brainy young lawyer named St. Ledger
to do tho battling. St. L. is a first-rate speaker, a
Q. native, and editor of the local education jour-
nal. Being slender of physique and pungent of
argument, he makes a striking contrast to his
three Ministerial opponents, who are all bulky
men of conspicuous brainlessness.
Brisbane Telegraph, mostly run by squar-
sons, offers up its penny wail against Federation,
and uses any peg to hang an inane and Ananias-
like screed upon. For instance, the little tin gods
of Q. Post and Telegraphs have been long at open
variance, and the jelly-fish C.S. Board is
frightened to put its foot down. Whereupon the
Brisbane ’Graph asserts that the coming Federa-
tion will shift half the staff, also the rank-and-file,
to make room for the cheap Melbourne article.
A factor that will materially aid in securing
a majority in the forthcoming N.S.W. Referen-
dum will undoubtedly be the scramble for the
capital. Every district with a claim for the
distinction will naturally try to return the largest
majority for Federation, and several of them,
Goulburn and Moss Vale, for instance, declared
against the ’9B Bill. Humiliating, perhaps, to
argue thus, but the votes so cast will count just as
well as those given in the most patriotic spirit.
The local Labor committee at Sturt (S.A.)
has refused to support Healy, whom the central
executive wished to run against Works-Minister
Jenkins. The ground is Healy’s advocacy of the
educational oapitaticn grant, which, although an
open question with the party, is extremely dis-
tasteful in the Sturt. That was the electorate
which, long ago, sent Chief-Justice Way and
Syinon, Q.C., to Parliament, but it has now been
Radical for years, and evidently means to stay so.

Re Federal-Capital map in last issue. Couldn’t find the
asterisk at the end ; looked for Doan, Warner, or
Clements. Not even Rassmussen. Yet full of egos. Felt
I suffered from nervousness, specks in my eyes, nasty
brown taste and tired feeling, yet to what end ? Who in
the name of all the fetishes of humanity is “Alex.
Wilson,” and why, when we ask for a remedy, does he
give us a map? “Alex.” “ suggests,” “ sees no reason,”
“proceeds to indicate,” “ deals with it first.” Is he the
new editor of my dear old Bulletin, or only a pill-person
after all?

Though (he said) opposed to woman’s franchise, he will

capitation grant; and he will instruct both Catholic men
and women to vote against the man who refuses to place

Catholic children of South Australia.

Barrister Lilley, eldest son of Queensland’s
Grand o ld Democrat, has much of his parent’s
brains but none of his courage. The young man
was warmly invited to contest the premier con-
gtituency by an influential deputation of Brisbane
e l ee tors, and declined. He prefers to harangue
tbe du ll- w itted cockies of Oxley, where he thinks
his chances are safer. And seeing that Lilley is
trying to run out another democratic candidate,
a number of very enthusiastic Radicals wouldn’t
mind if he falls in the mud.

absolutely refused to flow a little below Wilcan-

the river for their drinking water, the residents
had already thrown a row of sand-bags across the
bed, and were lapping up the water from the pool
behind. Fortunately Queensland rains— Queens-
land rains, please note —have resulted in a fair
“fresh.”
Labor-member Holman (N.S.W.) :
What was it that had led to the rejection of the Federal
Bill last year except the strong and general feeling all
over N.S. W. that it was a machine in which the central
pivot was defective, &c. ?
That is an easy conundrum. What led to the
dropping of the Bill last year was the fact that
the majority which cordially accepted it was dis-
qualified and disfranchised by a gang of political
robbers. And Holman, with his pretence of “ re-
jection,” is an apologist for the robbers and an
accessory after the fact.
The N.S.W. Cabbage-tree Mob is going to
make a great effort in the Upper House to get the
Federal referendum postponed for six months, in
the.hope that something of an anti-Billious nature
may happen meanwhile. It also says, per medium
of Sydney Telegraph, that public opinion is with
it —but it doesn’t believe what it says. If it did,
it would want to have the Federal referendum at
once, and to have the joy of wiping the Billites off
the face of the earth. The party that says it is
dead sure it can win easily, and then bolts for its
life, is a familiar fraud.
N.S.W. Speaker Abbott on the present
state of Maoriland —the province that is being
“ ruined ” by socialistic legislation, and old age
pensions, and Protection, and similar fads : “I
was through the greater part of the province, and
visited both islands, and everywhere the place
struck me as being wonderfully prosperous. . . .
During my eight weeks’ tour I did not come across
a single beggar.” And eleven years ago, in the
latter days of the Tory regime, Maoriland was
covered with relief works where able-bodied single
men worked for 2s 6d. a day.
Old W. H. Groom, the father and grand-
father and general old-man-in-a-hurry of Queens-
land Parliament, fills 10| columns of Toowoomba
Chronicle with his boiling-hot curse against
Federation. Groom’s tale of anguish is mainly
that Federation is a scheme on the part of the
poor stricken province next door (N.S.W.) to get
hold of Queensland’s wealth, and that Queensland
is to prop up the other provinces, and pay their
debts, and let them annex her trade, and some-
thing spectral and misty is to fall on the piebald
province, and it is to be overrun by the bunyip
and the blue-moulded unicorn. The enthusiasm
among the provincialists of N.S.W. about their
“ natural ally ” Queensland isn’t reciprocated up
North. The Bananaland provincialists seem to
mostly regard N.S.W. as some kind of highway
robber.
The political Jeremiahs who moan about
the cast-iron character of the Federal Constitution
are, so far as controversy is concerned, lineal
descendants of the plutocratic rings who banded
themselves to resist the Constitution Act of
N.S.W. from 1853 to 1856. That Act was also
called “cast-iron,” and thousands were gulled into
the belief that once they accepted it they
practically became slaves under it for ever. Yet
in 12 months after the establishment of Responsible
Government a Bill was passed amending the Con-
stitution Act in that section which excluded
clergymen from holding seats in Parliament. In
other words, when the people, rightly or wrongly,
judged that clergymen should be allowed to hold
seats they repealed the much-vaunted cast-iron
section and had their way, and that, too, while
the famous Constitution Act was in prime vigor.
The three prominent questions at the
approaching B.A. Assembly election will be
Federation, suffrage-reform, and the capitation
grant. The two former will be decided by
referendum, but the latter will
greatly sway the result of many
contests. R.C. Archbishop
O’Reily lately announced that
he and his priests will “ fight
to the death for a capita-
tion grant of <£lo,ooo a
year,” on the ground that
the 5000 children now
educated in R.C. schools
save the State .£20,000
per annum. All the
other denominations, save
only the Anglicans, are
up in arms with depu-
tations to Government,
and a lovely faction fight
is promised. The sud-
den prominence given to
the movement is awk-
ward for the Kingston
Govt., as Lands Minister
O’Longhlin and Chief
Secretary O’Loghlin
are staunch R.C.’s,and
Kingston himself once
supported a capitation
grant. Any Ministry
which favored such a
scheme would, however,
bring certain defeat upon
itself.
“A.W.B.,”St, George, Wesrern Queens-
land
I don’t quite understand the talk re Referendum and
joint sittings of both Houses —let the politicians chatter
about and settle that matter among themselves. But
when it comes to joint ownership of railways we are on
solid ground, and if Federation do nothing else but
abolish differential railway-rates it will be an unmixed
good. We want to send our produce to the nearest port
or market by the most convenient natural way ; we must
not be made send stuff hundreds of miles of a round to
increase the importance of some town or city because it
happens to be on a certain side of some imaginary boun-
dary line. For every unnecessary mile we have to carry
wool or other produce means that we are debarred from
putting an equal amount of labor into developing the
country, improving its resources, and, by benefiting Aus-
tralia, at the same time bettering the condition of Aus-
tralians. It is a hard country, is Australia ; it is not a
country where one can sit down and let things grow and
fatten and fall into one’s lap, but a place where one has
to graft, and graft hard, to make a decent living. So,
when so much necessary work is here to be done, we must
not carry thousands of bales of wool hundreds of unneces-
sary miles to bolster up the provincial fads of self-seeking
city traders. That is needless toil and needless expense.

With an ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heel CC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a scissors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities- a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a fit. The proprietor, with a
view of introducing his new and simple system into the
country districts where practical bootmaking is unknown,
has decided to supply his patrons at the lowest cash price.

DR. MORSE’S INDIAN ROOT PILLS
CURE PERMAKTEKrXLY
BILIOUSNESS, INDIGESTION & CONSTIPATION.
They Stand Alone as a Woman’s Best Friend!
A Miracle Wrought
MRS. LANG’S TERRIBLE CONDITION-
THREE LEADING SPECIALISTS FOR
FEMALE AILMENTS BAFFLED—HOS-
PITALS FAIL — The SURGEON’S KNIFE
ADDS TO HER AGONY. DR. MORSE’S
INDIAN ROOT PILLS CURE HER
PERMANENTLY AND COMPLETELY.
Miracles have been chronicled of the past, and
it does not appear as if the word fits into our
present-day vocabulary ; but no other word can
properly express the marvellous recovery of Mrs.
Lang, 16 Little Albion-street, Surry Hills, Syd-
ney : “ I had quite made up my mind that I
would die,” said Mrs. Lang to a reporter who had
been called to investigate her astonishing cure.
“ I had undergone operation after operation, each
of which would only give me slight relief from my
dreadful sufferings. In fact, the agony which I
endured seemed more than any human being could
live under, and yet, somehow, I held unto life
when death would have been a blessing.
“ Had you seen me at that time you would have
taken me for a Chinese, my skin was so yellow.
Only I had not the almond eyes, many Chinese
would have looked Europeans beside me. The
complaint I suffered from lasted eight years, and
they were the most wretched years a mortal ever
live through, if I can give the name of living to
such an existence. I had been on the operating-
table in the hospital more than once, and under
the treatment of three of the best specialists for
female ailments in the city, and there were silver
tubes inserted in my body to carry off the matter
which the ailment created. I was growing weaker
and more hopeless every day, and was thoroughly
resigned to my end, only it seemed too slow in
coming.
“ But I was not yet to die. It seemed by the
merest chance that my life was saved. I had been
taken home from the hospital by my husband in a
cab, and was in such torture that I lay on the
floor of the cab with my head on the seat. From
then he was forced to carry me upstairs and down-
stairs, as I could not walk. While lying on the
sofa one afternoon a little book was thrown in
through the open door. My husband picked it
up, and read of the astonishing cures Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills had made. He carefully read
it through, and was so much impressed by what
he read that he urged I should try them as a last
hope. At first I scorned the idea that any medi-
cine could accomplish what leading doctors and
the hospitals had utterly failed to do, but at last
I acted on his advice and tried Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills.”
At this stage Mrs. Lang produced her photo.,
taken when her friends thought she was dying.
It was the picture of a woman with a drawn face
and the expression of intense suffering. She then
said : “The difference you see between that
photo, and the perfect specimen of health and
strength which you now see before you is solely
due to the use of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills.
The pills went quietly and painlessly to work and
cleared my system of the impurities, and I began
to mend rapidly. My appetite is now splendid.,
and I gained 28 pounds in three months after
starting the use of the pills. I suppose I had
been attended by at least half-a-dozen doctors
during my illness, besides trying all the patent
medicines that I thought would help me ; in fact,
I spent nearly half a fortune in trying to be cured.
A few bottles of the pills have caused a complete
cure in my case, when you might say that death was
staring me. You are at liberty to make the facts
known, and 1 will gladly endorse my statements
if anyone doubting will call on me. I consider it
my duty to make the facts known. lam in per-
fect health, and can walk any distance, thanks to
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, as they alone have
saved my life.”
Tormented by Disease-
DR. MORSE’S INDIAN ROOT PILLS PEED
HIS VITAL SPARK.
Mr. Vine is a well-known greengrocer, residing
in Denison-street, Kingston, Sydney. He has a
few words to say about his suffering and cure :
“ For some considerable time,” he said, “ I have
had a bad set-to with acute indigestion, which at
last took me to a doctor. The pains would catch
me sharply in the pit of the stomach, and with
such severity that when the doctor told me what
my complaint was I could scarcely believe him,
thinking that indigestion could never use a man
so badly. His advice and medicine, however, did
me no good, and I was advised by friends to try
different remedies. I followed this one’s advice
and that; but my complaint was still as bad as
ever, when I resolved to give Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills a trial, as I had heard them praised so
much by many I knew who had used them. From
the first dose they acted very well upon me, giving
me more relief than anything I had taken, and I
can recommend them to all who suffer as I have
done.”
Suffered Since Girlhood.
MEDICAL MEN FAILED,
BUT DR. MORSE’S INDIAN ROOT PILLS
FAILED NOT.
Mrs. A. Everingham, living on the Nana-road,
Grafton, N.S.W., tells her story in a few words :
“ Since girlhood I have been a victim to severe in-
digestion and pains in the back, chest, and sides,
which at times were almost unendurable, and
caused me to think I could not live an hour. I
tried many remedies and consulted several medical
men, but they did not cure me. lam now 49
years of age, and suffered for many years. A few
weeks ago I started to use Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills. The first bottle caused me to feel in
good health. I continued their use for a short
time, and can now say they are the best remedy I
have taken. I recommend them to all my friends,
and they appear to do good to all.”

DR. MORSE’S INDIAN ROOT PILLS.

What remedies I used did me no good. I dreaded
the approach of night as a time of indescribable
misery. Then I heard of Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills, and I took one dose. They acted like
a charm, for that night I slept soundly, and the
next day was quite refreshed and fit for work.
They are a real good pill, and I believe in them.”

How is Your Breath ?
Foul breath is always an indication of poor
health—bad digestion. It is the staring-point of
many very serious maladies. Upon the healthy
action of the digestive organs the blood depends
for its richness and purity. If digestion stops,
poisonous matter accumulates and is forced into
the blood—there is no place else for it to go.
The bad breath is a danger-signal. Look out
for it ! If you have it, or any other symptom of
indigestion, take a bottle or two of Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills. They will straighten out the
trouble, make your blood pure and healthy, and
full of nutriment for the tissues. It is a strong
statement, but a true one.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills
HAVE CURED THOUSANDS, AND
WILL CURE YOU.
One of the roots from which these Pills are made
is a sudorific, which opens the pores of the skin,
and assists nature in throwing out the finer parts
of the corruption within. The second is a plant
which is an expectorant that opens and unclogs
the passage to the lungs, and thus, in a soothing
manner, performs its duty by throwing off the
phlegm and other humors from the lungs by
copious spitting. The third is a diuretic, which
gives ease and double strength to the kidneys ;
thus encouraged, they draw large amounts of im-
purity from the blood, which is then thrown out
bountifully by the urinary or water passage, and
which could not have been discharged in any
other way. The fourth is a cathartic, and accom-
panies the other properties of the Pills while en-
gaged in purifying the blood ; the coarser particles
of impurities, which cannot pass by other outlets,
are thus taken up and conveyed off in great
quantities by the bowels.
