There appears to be a general lack of knowledge
as to the whereabouts of the Sydney Safe Deposit, and
it is desirable to state that it is situated at the back
of Paling’s, 338 George-street, Sydney, and is reached by
the lane alongside Paling’s establishment. Since the
recent invitation of the manager to Bulletin readers to
visit the vaults there has been a constant stream of
visitors who are greatly impressed with the massiveness
of the place There is no doubt that for the safe storage
ot documents and valuables it is unsurpassed. It is
impregnable.*
Harry Wilson, many years chief engineer
to the B.H. Proprietary Co’s mines, has joined
the Austral Otis Engineering Co. (South Mel-
bourne), and will in future be associated with Mr.
E. J. Rigby in the management. Mr. Wilson’s
la,rge experience as a mining engineer should find
plenty of scope for usefulness in his new capacity.*
Ihe Brassey family still maintains its
reputation for accidents. When Mrs. Freeman-
Ihomas was hunting at Eastbourne (Eng.) the
other week, her horse fell and broke its neck. The
lady fell too, according to the best traditions of the
family, but, strange to say, escaped without injury.
As a rule, it is the Brassey that gets damaged,
and the horse or bike that escapes unhurt.
Anti-Federalists find an easy target for
their jibes in the converted Australian Star.
Verily, curses come home to roost, and a column
from to-day’s Star looks certainly grotesque
printed parallel with one of the diatribes against
the Bill in which that print indulged last year.
If it wanted to, Sydney Daily Telegraph would
not dare to change front so suddenly ; as matters
stand, it has hardly time to gracefully gyrate
right round the compass. No syndicate paper,
where a lot of directors pull different ways, can
ever be edited consistently, unless the editor is
mad e Boss by d eed. A p ropes, com pare 11 1 e Federal
courses steered by S.M. Herald and Sydney E.
News (private-proprietary) with those of the pub-
lic-company-owned Sydney D.T. and Australian
Star.
The new Federal Bill was carried through
N.S.W. Assembly ‘by huge majorities —on the
second reading, in fact, its opponents didn’t even
venture to call for a division. Yet, next morning,
Sydney Telegraph arose quite fresh to state that
the bulk of the Assembly was against it all the
same. The party which carried the 80,000 mini-
mum vote last time tried to assert itself again, but
the tide of battle swept it off its feet—in a House
of 89 it could only muster 7 votes. It was Labor-
meinber Fegan who proposed loading the dice this
time; he wanted a minimum of about 140,000, so
that, if there were 130,000 votes for the Bill and
one against it, the one would carry the day. And
yet Fegan belongs to the party which advocates
the absolute referendum,” and which believes in
majority-rule (!) for all it is worth.
Yet another “peril of the sea.” The
ancient coal-ship from N.S.W. continues to bob
up in various ports. The barque Kosciusko, from
Newcastle to Shanghai, put into Hongkong lately,
after being 65 days at sea, leaking too badly to
proceed further on her way. The pumps had
been kept going continually throughout the
voyage, the vessel making in moderate weather,
at times, 12in. per hour. Fortunately, she was
provided with a donkey-boiler, which was in-
valuable in getting rid' of the leakage. Pre-
vious to her departure from N.S.W., the vessel
was surveyed by three gentlemen whose duty it
was to see that the Navigation Act clauses re sea-
worthiness had been complied with. The Kosciusko
came up to the so-called requirements, therefore it
is to be assumed that, with the actual state of the
ship before them, anyone of the surveyors would
have accepted the command of her for the pro-
posed voyage.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Iredale s innings of 85 was, undoubtedly, the
best show of batting in the first innings of the Australian
XI; he was unlucky in being run out when going so
strong for his century. Clem Hill made the crowd laugh
by snicking Giffen’s off-theory balls to the fenoe at leg.
Noble, who got 111, was shaky for a long time, but his
luck stuck to him and he played better cricket on the
second day. Next to Iredale, “Ironbark" Jim Kelly
played best, and M‘Leod was shaping well when Giffen
and Johns bluffed him out. He missed a fast ball and
Johns knocked the bails off and appealed to Bannerman,
who said “ not out.” Then Giffen appealed to Crockett,
who gave M'Leod out caught and he had to leave. Johns
admitted afterwards that Mac. didn’t touch the ball. The
second time that “ Lightning ” Charlie has been bluffed
out on Sydney Ground.
(filler succeeded in spoiling what hope he had
of going 'Ome by his first innings effort against Austra-
lian XI. He and Reed man started batting for the Next
Eleven, and in the first hour Giller amassed no less than
17 by spirited pokings. Then he stopped at 17 for 25min.,
during vvhich time he was caught, off a “ no ball,” and
badly missed by Jones ; and the crowd cheered loudly
when he made another run. They cheered a lot louder
soon after when Gregory took an easy catch, and sent the
dreamy potterer out to make room for Trumper. Reed-
man knocked up 50 whilst Giller was getting 20 —and he
is anything but a fast scorer.
Trumper, after his first-innings failure to score
against the Eleven, and his rather poor luck in second
innings, appears to have little chance of a trip to
Hingland this year, as it is rather much to expect
Trumble and Darling to pick a player they have never
seen do anything when there are so many consistent all-
round men to choose from. Still, if Trumper does some-
thing phenomenal in Melb. and Adelaide they may agree
to take him. Fully half the big crowd at the match last
Saturday went there to see Trumper bat, and when at 6
he was caught by his Paddington clubmate, Kelly, off
clubmate Noble, lots went away disgusted. Kelly took
the catch as if he didn’t want it, and appealed with evi-
dent reluctance, but “Mary Ann” seemed to be less
sorrowful.
When Noble and M'Leod were batting in the
first innings of Australian XI Noble put a ball away
through the slips, and M'Leod, whose place it was to call,
seeing it was a safe run, called him and ran down the
pitch. Noble, however, stood with his feet firmly planted
well inside the crease, and Mac. had to turn round and
sprint back home. That it was a safe run was proved by
M'Leod being able to run down the pitch and back again,
and as Noble is notable for the earnestness with which he
runs out his own hits, his refusal to do so in this instance
looks rather unpromising.
Trumper’s evil luck in first-class matches con-
tinues. In his second innings against “ the Eleven” he
was run-out for 46; and upstairs, from where the
whole crease is visible, he certainly appeared to be
“home.” It was a close thing, anyhow. The perfect
manner in which he ran up his 46 should silence those
who allege that he can only score against second-rate
teams.
* • * *
“ Enthu.” : Has S.M. Herald joined the small
and select coterie who don’t want to see Trumper “ go
home”? Writer sent a letter to that organ in reply to
one “agin him”; it was not published, but two from
persons asking for Duff were. In the report on first day’s
play in M.L. match, no praise was given for Trumper’s 81
not out (afterwards increased to 253), and two chances
are alleged against him. Well, they were chances, but
this scribe would not care to shake hands with the bowler
who could take them. In commenting on the selected 14,
the Herald barracks for Donnan, and in treating of
Trumper’s chances says: “This batsman, like Donnan,
takes no chances.” Like Donnan! Great Scott! Trumper
hits everything hard and clean, with strokes all round the
wicket; Donnan potters along with his two strokes, and
merely blocks everything he can’t get them in on.
* * * *
If Syd. Gregory had to be picked on this season’s
form he certainly would not be in the Eleven.
* * * *
George Giffen was strongly disinclined to play
in the first match, when the chances were all against his
being in the first team ; but his friends urged him to do
so, and he changed his mind at the last moment.
Strange, by the way, how “Giff.’s” popularity ebbs and
flows ! His stoutest friends now are Adelaide cricketers,
who could not find words to express their disgust at the
ex-champion’s attitude last season.
* * • *
Australia’s three millions or so of people are
Just now wrestling with the colossal Giffen problem.
Until it is settled whether Giffen is picked for England
or goes on sorting letters at Adelaide G.P.0., the Federal
problem must sit back. So far, three Australian States
are undecided as to George’s claims.
* • • *
Jack Worrall, a comp, by original persuasion, is
only an occasional grafter since his selection as an Aus-
tralian Eleven certainty. He drops into Melb. Age office
and does some piece-work when so disposed.
* * * *
“ E.”: Saw a Sydney alleged blind man— a
street vocalist—enjoying the cricket on Friday last. The
youngster with him led him through the crowd and down
to the seats, over which he stumbled like any other sight-
less person, but when he sat down and gazed thought-
fully at the field the crowd began to have doubts about
his blindness. He didn’t give himself away altogether
until M'Leod was given out to a doubtful decision, and
then he loudly expressed the opinion that Mac. wasn’t
out at all, and that it was a d d shame. Umpire
Crockett’s decision must have been a bad one when even
a blind man could see that it was wrong. After the
match saw him at the gate with all the appearance of the
blind, warbling “Just tell them that you saw me.” Am
now wondering if he really can see, or whether he is a bit
of a humorist.
• * • *
The Newmarket Handicap resulted, as usual,
in a violent upsetting of all calculations based on trials by
the watch. Pretty well every one of the 23 starters was
alleged to be capable of getting over the distance in
lmin. 15sec., but the race fell to the rankest of outsiders,
who, with a clear track in front of him all the way, made
the slowest time (lmin. 16sec.) registered since Bungebah
bolted from his field in ’9l. Most of them ought to have
beaten Forest, but none of them did it.
The public had no cause to grumble at the
Forest coup. Miller’s Racing Chronicle shows that Up-
hill’s gelding: ran four sprint races during the last fort-
night in January, and won easily each time. He wasn’t
running in good company, but still he “ exposed his
by always getting home first, and if the publio
didn’t choose to be at all impressed by his consistent
performances, that was their look-out. Owner Uphill
thought the horse could win the Newmarket. Whether
Forest won on his merits solely, or was helped by his
jockey’s policy in running him on the extreme outside (as
it is called), is an interesting question. The “ outside ’’
position in a race down Flemington straight is, strictly
speaking, the inside position. Probably a horse isadvan-
taged in galloping on the untrodden side of the course
and finishing under the judge’s box, as Scorn did in
Maribyrnong Plate. Forest, on Saturday, was so near to
the judge’s box that he swerved somewhat at the finish a 3
though he thought it was time he turned to the left.
Lee-Metford swerved just as he was asked to
make an effort to beat Bobadil in the Leger. The same
nasty trick lost him a race at V.R.C. Spring Meeting,
when, with 101 b. the best of the weights, the Carbine colt
beat Cocos and Bobadil.
Wait-a-Bit had lost 17 races in succession pre-
vious to winning Essendon Stakes last Saturday.
Every bookmaker is an actor in his way. A
rather dramatic inoident was the “ I’ll pay Forest! ” by
one Abel Webb. Two or three people came along with
tickets—representing a very small percentage of Abel’s
receipts and the bookie made a really magnificent
demonstration of meeting his little liabilities. The voice
of Abel counting out the cash was audible for 50
yards around. One of the few lucky punters had
taken £lOO to £2 about Forest, and Abel (having by
this time gathered around him a great multi-
tude of sorrowing punters who wanted to know what
all the excitement was about) proceeded to count out the
£lO2 in “ quids”—one !—two I—three ! and so on, awfully
loudly, at a funereal pace. As a means of suggesting
that the public were heavy winners by Forest’s victory,
the system of paying £lOO in specie with a pause between
each sovereign proved a brilliant success. All the his-
trionic Ikey Moes adopted it, with more or less impressive
effect.
Ikey had two astounding turns-up on Saturday.
Carevie, a 5-year-old mare by Carbine-Evie, went out
absolutely unbacked in the last race of the day, and
landed the stake for J. M‘Sweeney.
It is a peculiarity of Mr. Bill Long’s stable that
its inmates come in for more criticism at the public
hands than the elect of other stables. The Newmarket
Handicap candidates proved no exception to the rule.
The reversals of form by Hopscotch and Beachcomber
before the Newmarket naturally brought those horses
under comment in the press, and when a Sydney daily
said that a stable-commission of some thousands had
•been executed about Hopscotch, it brought even the
taciturn Mr. Bill to the scratch with an indignant denial.
“Doubtless,” said Mr. Long, “some unprincipled
persons, to serve their own ends, have wilfully misled
your representative.”
And it really takes something out of the common
to rouse Mr. Hill Long. Some years since writer made a
voyage in the same ship with him, and one morning
all was excitement over the capture of a very big shark.
Most of the passengers rushed to have a look at the
brute, and the steward went down post-haste to tell Mr
Long, who was lying in his bunk. “ Riirht; tell the cook
to fry it for breakfast,” said the Hon. Bill, turning* round
for another snooze.
* ♦ * *
Punters, after all said and done, are likely to
get very much misled by applying A. J.O. time-tests to
the chances of Sydney horses engaged in a Newmarket
Handicap. True, Amiable last year made “Sydney
time” when she ran the Flemington six furlongs in lmin.
14sec., but this was the record for a Newmarket Handi-
cap. Flyers like Malua, Hova, Lochiel, Sedition (who
had Lochiel and Carbine in the places behind her), Bun-
gebah, and Fortunatus each took more than 1.15 to do
the journey, or 2sec. longer than Kirry required for win-
ning the 6 furlongs Challenge Stakes at Randwiek the
other month. Kirry, on paper, would be faster than any
of the flyers aforesaid if the time-test were a reliable
guide to “ form.”
* * • ♦
Backers of George Frederick for the Hawks-
burn Handicap at Caulfield, t’other Saturday, couldn’t
account for his suddenly dropping back just as he began
to look like a probable winner. The explanation is
humorous. George F., when in Redfearn’s stable, had
been accustomed to knock off work in the mornings at a
given spot, and it was there that he tried to stop in the
Hawksburn Handicap race. He thought his task had
been duly completed. Before Pearson could get the big
horse going again he had lost fully a dozen lengths.
* * * *
A Bulletin man backed a double at Caulfield
t’other Saturday—2s to 1 about Stand Off for Oakleigh
Plate and Valorous for Oakleigh Purse. Another Bulle-
tin man applied to Mossy Isaacs for the same double and
was offered, with much theatrical emphasis, “ Twen-tv to
one ! ” Luckily it didn’t tempt him. Stand Off (victorious)
started at 7 to 1, and Valorous (defeated) went out at 10’s
The backer, by putting a sov. on Stand Off, and investing
the money on the double principle, would have stood to
(instead of £26) if Valorous had got home.
Which explains why Mossy and co. can afford to hurl
strong language at quibbling punters.
* * ■* *
The Law of Victoria prohibits Tattersall from
conducting sweeps within the province, but it permits
Melb. Age to publish in its betting reports the lament of
Ikey Mo, as follows : “ The market was unusually dull
yesterday, and it is to be feared there will be little
speculation of consequence until the card is called at the
Victoria Club.”
* * • *
Hon. Hugh Mosman (Q.), now settled in Maori-
land, has acquired an extensive string of racers, and
intends an Australian invasion next year. The “ four-
figure ” youngster, snapped up at last Wellington Park
sale, is the gem of a costly equine collection.
• m * *
According to postal officials, Maoriland’s con-
* j l'* on “ Tatt.’s ” sweeps is materially decreasing.
And no wonder. People grow sick of drawing horses
that never start, merely because drawers will not con-
ciliate the owner of the prad, the trainer, the jockey, the
lad that leads the ’oss round the paddock, and the cat
that sleeps in the prad’s loose-box ; and the far-off sub-
scriber, not having the same opportunity as the
one on the spot to “ conciliate ” horse-owners,
gets left very badly. No, it won't do, and a
change is inevitable. “Tattersall" has to finally and
decisively choose between the people who subscribe the
money for his sweeps and the horse-owners who have
so far managed to suck their blood. “ Tutt.” is gradually
coining round to the public ; soon he will announce, Thb
Bulletin ventures to predict, that he will pay not one
cent of any prize to anyone but the owner of the ticket;
also, most of the public money will be in sweeps drawn
on the day of the race.
• • * •
The best performance at Randwiek on Saturday
was put up by the St. Albans-bred mare, The Prize, by
Robinson Crusoe, who, on a slow course, carried 7.6 and
won the Denison Stakes from 18 starters, running the
six furlongs in 1.16. The Prize (she was one of the prizes
in St. Alban’s lottery) is a speedy-looking customer, and
has won her two races in Sydney in great style.
* » * »
Young Bill Kelso just missed scooping five races
on Saturday. At Randwiek, Oarldale was beaten by a
neck in the Denison Stakes, and Blanche Florence by a
length in the big race; whilst at Flemington, Lauri
won and North British and Strathroy got seconds in their
respective races. A fairly tantalising run of luck for an
afternoon.
* * * *
The Sharkey-M‘Coy fight once more showed
that science alone will not carry a man through against
bulldogs of the Sharkey and Goddard type. Joe God-
dard received blows from Choynski and Dooley that
would have finished any ordinary man, but he came again
and knoeked-out his more-scienced opponents. And so
it was with Sharkey. M‘Coy knocked the sailor down
and bashed him all over the stage, but punishment which
would have utterly “stopped” the ordinary fighter only
seemed to urge the sailor on. M‘Coy showed superb
science whilst he lasted, but the inevitable came when he
began to tire, and Sharkey fairly hurled himself at his
opponent, beat down all opposition, and knocked M‘Coy
out of time in the tenth round.
• * * *
now wants to fight Sharkey. Such
a match would be a big draw. Last time they met Fitz.
gave the sailor a terrible drubbing, but the decision wa
given to Sharkey on an alleged foul. Sharkey was then a
comparative novice, and has since improved out of recog-
nition, whilst Fitz. has been leading rather an easy life.

‘‘ Kid ” M‘Coy was christened Norman
Selby. Why he changed his name has yet to be

* * * *
Victorian Bill Doherty knocked out Sydney’s
heavy-weight piior of more than a decade back
(Mick Dooley) in six rounds at Kalgoorlie (W.A.) on
Saturday week. Mick, though much overburdened
with fat, and long since a back number at the game, has
been having a fairly successful time in the West with
has bee ns . of an inferior order (such as Jim Fogarty,
the whilom jaw-breaker), and several aspiring novices,
who thought they were made of world-beating stuff
Dooley, in the palmy days of the Australian ring, was as
clever as they made ’em, but seemed to have been born
under an unlucky star, as he formed the stepping-stone to
championships for Peter Jackson, Frank Slavin and Joe
Goddard, who, among them, punched much of the heart
out of Mick. Naturally of nervous temperament—not
cowardly—Dooley could rarely get quite going until two
or three rounds had elapsed, and then it was too late.
Steve O Donnell, the big Australian now in America, was
another of the same order.
• * * *
Ebon-hued Ike Stewart and Ted Porter (heavy-
weights) banged each other in great style at Golden Gate
Athletic Club (Sydney) on Saturday night. The black got
all the worst of the deal, and was sent to the boards seven
times in the first round. Went to his corner limp as a
wet rag—dead to the world, apparently—but he pulled
himself together wonderfully during his spell, and was
again receiver-general in the second round at the end of
which two very tired bruisers retired to their chairs. In
round three, though the darkey was made the target for
further thumps he managed to get a few home, and
during some wild fighting the white man’s “point”
stopped a weighty swing aimed at no particular place, but
simply sent out on general principles ; down he went,
and he had to be carried off the “ field.” No greater
display of real right-down grit than that shown by the
colored man has been seen in Sydney for many a day. It
looked 100 to 1 against him almost from the start.
* • * *
Yet another world’s 220yds. swimming record
was wiped out last Saturday, when at Farmer's Domain
Baths (Sydney) Australia’s latest swimming marvel,
Freddy Lane, eclipsed Gormley’s great performance (2min.’
28Jsec.) of seven years ago. These figures have several
times been attacked by English and American swimmers,
but they remained undisturbed until Lane felt himself
capable of successfully tackling the task, and he scored
at the first attempt. Eight watches took the time and,
though some of them differed, all were under the pre-
vious best. A mean was struck, with the result that
2min. 27 4-ssec. supplants ex-champion Gorinly’s record.
Lane seemed to take matters a bit easy over the first
hundred ; had he been all out from the word “go ” it is
quite possible that seconds instead of fractions thereof
would have been chipped off the figures assailed.
* • * •
Regarding Australasian champion Lane’s recent
record-breaking feats. Present writer has a notion that
they won’t go in England, as the conditions under which
they were accomplished are opposed to that country’s
rules, which insist upon absolutely still water such as
that found in a lake or city bath. Over in ALL. the other
day the swimmer who won the 100yds. championship put
up a record for the province, but the local A.S.A. with-
held its endorsement because the race took place in tidal
waters—Wellington Harbor to wit. There would seem to
be sound reason for this, too, as at Bathurst baths (still
water) Bishop licked Lane in the 100yds. championship in
lmin. 5 l-ssec., and repeated the beating at Redfern
baths (still water again) in lmin. 3 4-ssec. ; yet Lane
comes along at Farmer’s Domain baths (tidal waters) last
Saturday week and covers the 100yds., with a slight go
on the starter, in lmin. 2J4sec.— much quicker time than
that of either of the two previous contests wherein he
suffered defeat. Further, conditions (barring the water)
were not so favorable at Farmer’s baths, for there only
two “ kicks-off ” could be availed of, while at Bathurst
and Redfern at least three were necessary to cover the
courses, and no swimmer needs telling what a great aid
to fast time the shove-off from the basin’s end is.

A large proportion of the second edition
(2000 copies) of Will Ogilvie’s poems, published by
The Bulletin, were ordered in advance. Single
copies will be sent post free, from Bulletin office,
to any address, for 4s. 6d. in stamps. “ The most
charming book of Australian verse that has yet
appeared,” writes Victor Daley.
Hapless Brisbane Courier is so hard-up
for a stone to throw at the Labor dogs that it is
actually compelled to revive the old “Socialism
in our Time ” bogie. A few months ago, before
the change of editors, the same organ admitted
that the cry just mentioned frightened electors no
longer. ... And now it’s putting up the old scare-
crow again with a few fresh rags and feathers.
Sydney Telegraph draws public attention
to the shocking fact that in 1852 -47 years ago—
the old Leg. Council of Victoria claimed that
Melbourne ought to be the capital of Australia.
No doubt it did, but where does the shock come
in ? Are not Sydney, Bathurst, Goulburn, Albury,
Bombala, Robertson, Armidale, Blayney, Moss
Vale, Bowral, Mittagong, Wagga, Junee, and
goodness knows how many other places doing
exactly the same thing to-day? And it is the
place that is yelling for the capital to-day that is
a matter of present interest—not the one which
wanted it 47 years ago.
Premier Reid perorates in the debate on
the second reading of the Federal Enabling Bill:
He could not believe that any man in either House
would seek to put the Government in a position of break-
ing faith. No man in the House would do it. The
Government was bound by every sense of honor to the
other Governments of Australia to push this matter to the
bitter end (cheers), to exhaust every power in the Consti-
tution (cheers) to keep faith with the sister colonies.
This to the men who deliberately, and with the
connivance of the same Reid, loaded the last
Enabling Bill with the 80,000-limit clause, in
wanton disregard of every principle of loyalty and
faith. And it is reported that they cheered.
That very useful work, the “Year Book
of Australia,” for 1899, is to hand. It covers so
many Australian subjects that it is difficult to
spot the things that are missed. Yet there are a
few casual defects that strike the eye here and
there. The Ballarat Banking Co. is persistently
omitted from the list of banks. Maoriland is
always represented as having four fire-insurance
companies; the Farmers’ Co-operative Co., though
eight years in existence, has never yet been
mentioned. The City Mutual Fire Co. is
still put down as a Sydney office, though
it was taken over by the London and Lan-
cashire a year ago, and the Southern still
appears as an independent Vic. office, and the
dead-and-gone Cornwall is included among Tas-
manian co’s., while the very-much-alive Mutual of
Tasmania isn’t. The list of life-offices shows at
least one omission. These are a good many dis-
crepancies in one small department of the work.
In former days the. Year-Book used to contain
political and other information re Tasmania, but
that province is quite left out nowadays. The
dramatic summary, once a very useful feature of
the work, has also dropped out. A lot of valuable
new information has been included, and the Year-
Book is distinctly the best work of reference in
Australasia. Still it might be made better.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.* 1 *

THE ILLUSTRATED LONDON NEWS of
Sept. 28, 1895, saya:
“ If I were asked which single medicine I should
prefer to take abroad with me, as likely to be
most generally useful, to the exclusion of all
others, I should say Chlorodyne. I never travel
without it, and its general applicability to the
relief of a large number of simple ailments forms
its best recommendation.”
“The resignation of that attractive
preacher, Canon Eyton,” writes The Bulletin s
London correspondent, “is the most serious, and
also the saddest, tragedy the Established Church
has ever known. He held the Canonry of West-
minster Abbey and the rectory of St. Margaret’s
adjoining, worth .£l5OO p.a., and a Gothic resi-
dence. Lord Rosebery made the appointment in
’95, and later the office of sub-almoner to the
Queen followed as her Majesty’s own gift. All
has been surrendered, and with a Times announce-
ment of broken health, mental strain, &c., the
Canon has gone abroad, unlikely to ever come
back. He was immensely wealthy, his ample for-
tune having been increased by about .£lOO,OOO,
left him by the late Mr. Yilliers, ‘Father of the
House of Commons.’ His one hobby was his
choir-boys, whom he invited frequently to his
residence at Broadstairs and entertained in a
manner worthy of an Emperor or a Sultan.”
Victoria has an “Inebriates Board” on
hand just now—a body created by Parliament
with loose general instructions to roam over the
wide fields of Drink, and consider liquor in all its
aspects, and its effects on everybody, and the
probable effect on everybody if there wasn’t any,
and whether it should be abolished, and how ;
also, what the place would be like without it, and
whether drunkenness is a crime or a disease, or
partly both, and, if so, where the crime ends and
the disease begins, and how to treat it, and other
things. It is an immense order, and the Board has
pottered along in the usual rut. Its witnesses are
mostly liquor-dealers who don’t drink much them-
selves else they would lose their licenses, and who
can’t take an unbiassed view of the fluid which is
their bread and cheese ; and teetotal parsons who
don’t drink either. And when a valued Bulletin
contributor offered himself as a witness, on the
ground that he was an experienced boozer of long
standing, and could explain all about the effects
of alcohol, his testimony was refused with
derision. The idea of taking the evidence of a
man who used the thing that all the row was
about struck the old barnacles of the board as
wildly absurd. These old gentlemen who mostly
don’t drink themselves just pottered along as
before, taking more evidence on Drink from
people who don’t drink, and the Respectable
papers backed them up, and treated the offer
of the one experienced witness with elephantine
scorn.

the desert without committing outrages on the
dead—to say nothing of defiling the drinkit g
water of the living—it isn’t the Britain it used to
be.

Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. Malyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
; Dear Sir, —For years past (about ten), I have been
troubled with “ Acne” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “ Ligmolliene,”
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same “Cum Grano Salis,” and was in-
clined not to use it ; eventually I tried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing “ Ligomolliene ” all the success it richly deserves,
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O'REGAN.

Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21 st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record progress
with regard to my old enemy. “ Eczema Capitis,” from
which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treatment for
some years under the prescription of Professor M'Call
Anderson and other eminent dermatologists, with only
partial and temporary relief. When I met you I com-
menced somewhat sceptically your ointment Ligmolliene,
and after about two months’ treatment I may honestly say
my enemy is defeated, and I am perfectly well. I will only
add that since I commenced the treatment of myself I have
prescribed it to others, and am still doing so with brilliant
results. Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain yours
sincerely, THOS. LE GAY HOLTIIOUSE, M.R.C.S., L.

Mr. THOMAS MUDIMAN,
The well-known Tailor, of 327 Elizabeth-street, Sydney,
states, on oath, how his shattered nervous system was
completely cured and restored.
Statutory
Declaration.
I, THOS. MUDIMAN, Master Tailor, of 327 Elizabeth-
street, Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
hereby solemnly and sincerely declare as follows:
Whereas I suffered severely from a very troublesome
affection of the NERVES, which affected me so much that
I was quite unable to attend to my business. I tried
several doctors and many remedies, but all of no avail.
Whereas I, as a last hope, obtained some of Herr Rass-
mussen’s Purely Herbal ALFALINE VITALITY PILLS,
which I hereby solemnly and sincerely declare COM-
PLETELY CURED ME within a few weeks, and although
this is over five years ago I have remained well ever since.
The ALFALINE PILLS also cured my wife, and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing- the same
to be true.
Declared at Sydney this 29th day of April, 1898.
THOMAS MUDIMAN.

Heame’s Bronchitis Cure.
Those who have taken this medicine are amazed at its
wonderful influence. Sufferers from any form of bron-
chitis, distressing cough, difficulty in breathing, hoarse-
ness, pain or soreness in the chest, experience delightful
and immediate relief, and to those who are subject to
colds on the chest it is invaluable, as it effects a complete
cure. It is most comforting in allaying tickling and irri-
tation in the throat, and.giving strength to the voice, and
it never allows a cough or asthma to become chronic. No
house should be without it, as, taken at the beginning, a
dose is generally sufficient, and a complete cure is certain.
“ Hr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —I had a bad attack
of Bronchitis for six months, with dreadful hacking cough
day and night and great quantity of phlegm. Night
sweats came on and pains in my chest, and I got so weak
that I could not leave my bed. At this stage I was pro-
nounced to be in consumption. I had a lot of medicines
of different kinds, but nothing did me any good until,
reading in the Age of your ‘ Bronchitis Cure,’ I got a
bottle, and after taking two doses I was much improved.
After taking two bottles the cough and night sweats left
me, my appetite returned, and I was able to get up ; and
am now in good health, for which I am truly grateful, and
shall recommend your Medicine to all my friends.—Yours
truly, E. SMITH, Halpin Park, Nhill, Victoria.”

A Fever-Stricken Ship
NEW GUINEA FEVER
Clements Tonic a Specific
FOR
ISLAND FEVER AND AGUE.
Statement by
CAPTAIN NEAVES.
(From the “Newtown Chronicle.”)
Most people will remember the voyage of
the fever-stricken' steamer, Mount Kembla.
In the middle of last April she started for
New Guinea, and on the first day of the
following June arrived back in Sydney.
But we will let the first mate, Captain
Neaves, describe the terrible sufferings of
the captain and the crew in Ins own words.
“ When we arrived in Sydney," said Mr.
Neaves, who is living in Marmion Street,
Nordi Kingston, to "a reporter from the
“ Newtown "Chronicle,” who recently inter
viewed him, “ I was so weak I could
scarcely stand through New Guinea fever.
The plague came upon us three days home-
ward bound from New Guinea. First, two
of the sailors fell sick, then next day three
more, and so on, the fever went through the
ship. 'We swallowed large quantities of
quinine from the medicine chest, but with
very little benefit. We all fought against
the disease as hard as we could ; we didn't
like the idea of lying down, as we feared, if
we did, we should never get up again. There
was sometimes but two of us able to be on
deck at a time to navigate, steer, and keep
a 1< ok-out, and the engineers below were as
short handed as we were. The sea was dead
against us, we were running short of coal
and medicine; the skipper was laid up
with the fever in his bunk, and on the way
down we were forced to run into Moiton
Bay to get supplies and it is a matter of
regret to me now that I wasn t aware of the
fine properties of Clements lonic, for if I
had but known of that remedy 1 should
certainly have taken in a good stock of it at
Brisbane, when we should have been much
better able to combat the fever which had
seized upon us. However, 1 didn t get any
Clements Tonic till we reached Sydney.
Our skipper was seized on a Friday, and
died on the following Tuesday, so you can
form some judgment as to the rapidity of its
effects. Yes,” continued Captain Neaves,
“ the disease clings to you even after you
have arrived in the bracing winter climate of
Sydney, and many a poor fever-stricken
fellow has lasted all the way down the coast
but to die like our skipper did on reaching
Sydney.”
“ What is this fever like ? '
“ It is ordinary fever and ague, sometimes
called malaria, low, or intermittent fever,
which is very prevalent in tropical climates.
It generally comes on with headaches, lassi-
tude, and shaking ; this agu fit is then
followed by an exceedingly high temperature,
and the sufferer often loses consciousness.
The periods of fever and shakes get closer to-
gether, the sick man gets weaker and weaker
as the hours roll on, and if he doesn t take
the turn for the better, he dies very shortly
of sheer weakness and want of vitality. Its
weakening effects are very rapid. It knocks
amanall to pieces ina very short time, andeven
if hegetsover the disease, the after effects will
very often lasthim therestcfhi dife. Usually,
months elapse before a sufferer resumes any-
thing like his ordinary health and vigor.
Any day in the week you may go up into the
Domain and see some of these poor fellows
sitting about—waiting to recover their
strength. They look like so many skeletons,
with their lean and crippled figures, parch-
ment-like faces, and dull eyes. They are
simply suffering from the remains of island
fever.”
“ How did you get on ? .
“Well, as I said, when I arrived in
Sydney I was suffering from after effects of
New Guinea fever. I was almost too weak
to stand. Continual headaches oppressed
me. Usually, after one has got round the
turning point of an illness, a convalescent
appetite may be expected. But th s was iai
from the case with me. My appetite was com-
pletely lost. I was absolutely indifferent
whether I ate anything or not. 1 ielt dun
and languid the whole day long, and instead o.
getting in some useful sleep at night 1 10 ie
uneasily about, never able to get an horn s
solid rest the night through. I tried all
sorts of things that were recommended to
me on all sides, and I took many different
strengthening medicines that I saw ad vcr tisei ,
but to no purpose, and asforquinine, I took so
much that 1 must ha\ e been fairly saturated
withit. My vitality waspretty low down, lean
assure you. It was my landlord that told
me to try Clements Tonic. ‘ It will pull you
through,’ said he. I told him plainly I
didn’t believe it would, but as he made a
point of it, I gave it a trial. lam not ex-
aggerating one iota when I say that half a
bottle of Clements Tonic benefited me to an
extent that was as marvellous as unantici-
pated. At the best I had hoped to recover
my health and vigor but slowly, say in the
course of weeks ; but here in three or four
days a very welcome change for the better
had been wrought in me by Clements Tonic.
It was astonishing, after the failure of so
many medicines. My appetite came back in
double force. I felt strung up all over.
The moment 1 tumbled into bed I was
asleep, and slept the whole night through
that dreamless, refreshing sleep which only
a sailor can sleep. A buoyancy of spirits
took the place of my dull and languid
feelings, and it co.ddn’t have been mote than
a week after commencing to take Clements
Tonic when I was asking myself if l ever
felt better in my life. And I never did feel
more healthy. I was thoroughly toned up,
and had made a perfect recovery from one
of the most enervating diseases. lam now
sound as a bell, and willing, if necessary, to
sailonceagaiuround the malarial coast of New
Guinea—that is, if the ship’s medicine chest
contained a goodly supply of Clements Tonic.
And you may depend upon it when I do go
to sea again I shall take good care to see
that remedy provided.” f
“Will you let us put what you ve
said in the ‘ Chronicle ’ ? ”
“ You needn’t ask that question; of
course you can. 1 believe a proper know-
ledge of the properties of Clements T onic
ought to be brought home to all that go into
marshy places or tropical climates, and if
your publication of what I ve told you has
that desirable effect, I shall be exceedingly
pleased.”

A Sad Example.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was Dr. M'Glynn, unto Father O’Flynn,
Said, “Yer Reverence seems to be getting too
thin,
And you’re gapin’ and yawnin’,
Ye’d better take warnin’,
And have every mornin’ a drop of hot gin ! ”
“ I’d be willing to try,” said the priest in reply,
“ But I dare not attempt it —I’ll tell you for why :
I am pledged to abstaining,
In hope of restraining
My people from draining their pockets all dry ! ”
“ And, dear Dr. M'Glynn,” added Father
O’Flynn,
“ My example has led them to see it’s a sin.
Do you think that the Hooleys,
The Hogans and Dooleys
Would mind what the rule is were I to begin ? ”
“ A convenient place for containing a care
You could find ! ” said thedoctor. “ Well, saving
your grace,
There’s hot water for shaving
You’re bound to be having,
Or else you’d be lavin’ a beard on your face ! ”
“And,”said Dr. McGlynn unto Father O’Flynn,
“ You can have the hot toddy when no one is in ;
Just a small appetiser,
Sure I’m your adviser,
And who’ll be the wiser? ” said Dr. M'Glynn.
Now M'Glynn had to go for a fortnight or so
To the neighboring township of Neversayno,
And he met on returning
That person discerning,
In horticult learning, the gardener Joe.
“How is Father O’Flynn?” called out Dr.
M'Glynn.
Joseph paused and considered, while rubbing his
chin.
“ Is he well since I treated ? ”
The Doctor repeated,
And these were the words that Joe answered him
in:
“Well, his health’s pretty gay, I am happy to
say,
But I’m thinkin’ his mind must be goin’ astray.
From the way he’s behavin’,
I fear he’ll go ravin’—
He’s taken to sharin’ six times in the day 1”
W. T. GOODGE.
Archibald Forbes’ “ Life of Napoleon III.”
has an occurrence not generally known. When
the Great Napoleon departed for his last coxnpaign,
little Louis Napoleon cluQg to him crying, and
said, “You shan’t go. jpfiey will make you
prisoner.” Nap. said to Hortense, “Take care of
him. He may come round to my throne.” Yet
there was Napoleon’s own son in the way. This
saying of the Emperor afforded the prime element
of Louis’s confidence in his star.
On Our Selection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was the year we put the bottom paddock under
potatoes. Dad was standing contemplating the
tops which were withering for want of rain. . . .
He shifted his gaze to the ten acres sown with
corn. A dozen stalks or so were looking well; a
few more, 10 or 12 inches high, were coming in
cob; the rest hadn’t made an appearance.
Dad sighed and turned away from the awful
prospect. He went and looked into the water-
cask. Two butterflies, a frog or two, and some
charcoal were at the bottom. No water. He
sighed again, took the yoke and two kerosene-tins,
and went off to the springs.
About an hour and a half after he returned with
two half-tins of muddy, milky-looking water —the
balance had been splashed out as he got through
the fences— and said to mother (wiping the sweat
off his face with his shirt-sleeve) —
“Don’t know, I’m sure, what things are going
t’ come t’; . . . no use doing anything .
there’s no rain ... no si ” He lifted his foot
and with cool exactness took a place-kick at the
dog which was trying to fall into one of the
kerosene-tins, head first, and sent it and the water
flying. Oh you ! ” The rest is omitted in the
interests of Poetry.
* * * *
Day after. Fearful heat ; not a breath of air;
fowl and beast sought the shade; everything
silent; the great Bush slept. In the west a stray
cloud or two that had been hanging about gathered,
thickened, darkened.
The air changed. Fowl and beast left the
shade ; tree-tops began to stir—to bend —to sway
violently. Small branches flew down and rolled
before the wind. Presently it thundered—afar off.
Mother and Sal ran out and gathered the clothes,
and fixed the spout, and looked cheerfully up at
the sky.
Joe sat in the chimney-corner thumping the ribs
of a cattle-pup, and pinching its ears to make it
savage. He had been training that pup ever since
its arrival that morning.
The plough-horses, yoked to the plough, stood
in the middle of the paddock, beating the flies off
with their tails and leaning against each other.
Dad stood at the stock-yard—his brown arms
and bearded chin resting on a middle-rail—-pas-
sively watching Dave and Paddy Maloney break-
ing-in a colt for Callaghan—a weedy, wild, her-"
ring-gutted brute that might have been worth 15
shillings. Dave was to have him to hack about for
six months for the breaking-in.
* * » *
They had been at “Callaghan”—as they christ-
ened the colt—since day-light, pretty well, and had
crippled old Bess and lamed Maloney’s Dandy,
and knocked up two they borrowed from Anderson
—-yarding the rubbish ; and there wasn’t a fence
within miles of the place that he hadn’t tumbled
over and smashed. But when they did get him
in they lost no time commencing to quieten him.
They cursed eloquently, and threw the bridle at
him, and used up all the missiles and bits of hard
mud and sticks about the yard, pelting him because ;
he wouldn’t stand.
Dave essayed to rope him “the first shot,” and
nearly poked his eye out with the pole; and Paddy
Maloney, in attempting to persuade the affrighted
beast to come out of the cow-bail, knocked the cap
of its hip down with the milking-block. They
caught him then and put the saddle on. Callaghan
trembled. When the girths were tightened they
put the reins under the leathers, and threw their
hats at him, and shouted, and “hooshed” him
round the yard, expecting he would buck with the
saddle. But Callaghan only trotted into a corner
and snorted. Usually, a horse that won’t buck
with a saddle is a “ snag.” Dave knew it. The
chestnut he tackled for Brown did nothing with
the saddle. He was a snag. Dave remembered
him and reflected. Callaghan walked boldly up
to Dave, with his head high in the air, and snorted
at him. He was a sorry-looking animal—cuts
and scars all over him ; hip down ; patches and
streaks of skin and hair missing from his head.
• * * •
“No buck in him!” unctuously observed Dad,
without lifting his chin off the rail.
“Ain’t there?” said Paddy Maloney, grinning
cynically. “Just you wait! ”
It seemed to take the heart out of Dave, but he
said nothing. He hitched his pants and made a
brave effort to spit—several efforts. And he
turned pale.
Paddy was now holding Callaghan’s head at
arm’s-length by the bridle and one ear, for Dave
to mount.
A sharp crack of thunder went off right over-
head. Dave didn’t hear it.
“ Hello ! ” Dad said, “we’re going to have it—
hurry up. ”
Dave didn’t hear him. He approached the
horse’s side and nervously tried the surcingle—a
greenhide one of Dad’s workmanship. “Think
that’ll hold ? ” he mumbled meekly.
“Pshaw!” Dad blurted through the rails —
“ Hold! Of course it ’ll hold—-hold a team o’ bul-
locks, boy.”
“’S all right, Dave; ’s all right—git on.” From
Paddy Maloney, impatiently.
Paddy, an out-and-out cur amongst horses him-
self, was anxious to be relieved of the colt’s head.
Young horses sometimes knock down the man
who is holding them. Paddy was aware of it.
Dave took the reins carefully, and was about to
place his foot in the stirrup when his restless eye
settled on a wire-splice in the crupper—also Dad’s
handiwork. He hesitated and commenced a re-
mark. But Dad was restless ; Paddy Maloney
anxious (as regarded himself); besides, the storm
was coming.
Dad said: “Damn it, what are y’ ’fraid o’
boy? That’ll hold —jump on.”
Paddy said: “Now, Dave, while I’ve ’is ’ead
round.”
Joe (just arrived with the cattle-pup) chipped in.
He said : “Wot, is he fuf-fuf-fuf-f-Wkent of
him, Dad ? ”
Dave heard them. A tear like a hailstone
dropped out of his eye.
“ It’s all damn well t’ talk, ” he fired off ; “ come
in and ride th’ horse then, if y’ s’ game ! ”
A dead silence.
• * • *
The cattle - pup broke away from Joe and
strolled into the yard. It barked feebly at Cal-
laghan, then proceeded to worry his heels. It
seemed to take Callaghan for a calf. Callaghan
kicked it up against the rails. It must have taken
him for a cow then.
Dave’s blood was up. He was desperate. He
grabbed the reins roughly, put his foot in the
stirrup, gripped the side of the pommel, and was
on before you could say “ Woolloongabba.”
With equal alacrity, Paddy let the colt’s head
go and made tracks, chuckling. The turn things
had taken delighted him. Excitement (and
pumpkin) was all that kept Paddy alive. But
Callaghan didn’t budge—at least not until Dave
dug both heels into him. Then he made a blind
rush and knocked out a panel of the yard—and
got away with Dave. Off he went, plunging, gal-
oping) pig-jumping, breaking loose limbs and
bark off trees with Dave’s legs. A wire fence was
in his way. It parted like the Bed Sea when he
came to it —he crashed into it and rolled over.
The saddle was dangling under his belly when he
got up ; Dave and the bridle were under the fence.
But the storm had come, and such a storm ! Hail-
stones as big as apples nearly—first one here and
there, and next moment in thousands.
• * * •
Paddy Maloney and Joe ran for the house;
Dave, with an injured ankle and a cut head, limped
painfully in the same direction ; but Dad saw the
plough-horses turning and twisting about in their
chains and set out for them. He might as well
have started off to cross the continent. A hail-
stone, large enough to kill a cow, fell with a thud
a yard or two in advance of him, and he slewed
like a hare and made for the house also. He was
getting it hot. Now and again his hands would
go up to protect his head, but he couldn’t run that
way—he couldn’t run much any way.
The others reached the house and watched Dad
make from the back-door. Mother called to him
to “Run, run I” Poor Dad! he was running.
Paddy Maloney was joyful. He danced about
and laughed vociferously at the hail bouncing off
Dad. Once Dad staggered—a hail-boulder had
struck him behind the ear—and he looked like drop-
ping. Paddy hit himself on the leg, and vehe-
mently invited Dave to “Look, look at him!”
But Dad battled along to the haystack, buried his
head in it, and stayed there till the storm was
over—wriggling and moving his feet as though he
were tramping chaff.
Shingles were dislodged from the roof of the
house, and huge hailstones pelted in and put the
fire out, and split the table, and fell on the sofa
and the beds.
Rain fell also, but we didn’t catch any in the
cask—the wind blew the spout away. It was a
curled piece of bark. Nevertheless, the storm did
good. We didn’t lose all the potatoes. We got
some out of them. We had them for dinner one
&uncla y.
STELE RUDD.
There is room enough to drop the Moon
into the unexplored regions of the Antarctic,
Dr. Neild, the Shakespearian post-mor-
temist of Melbourne, claims that during a medical
career of 55 years he has never lost a case through
the action of chloroform ; but he takes to himself
no especial credit for the record. What a contrast
is the work of the old man, commencing at a time
when anaesthetics were but little understood, with
the administration of chloroform by the youthful
sawbones in Australian metropolitan hospitals.
Deaths under chloroform are of almost weekly
occurrence in these institutions, although the
newly-fledged surgeons are specially trained by an
expert in anaesthetics, and have the advantage of
the accumulated wisdom of scores of years. The
question of death under chloroform is one which
the Australian public will wake up to one of these
days, and then there will be trouble, for many ex-
perienced men aver that such demises are wholly
preventible.

Every drop of our blood parses through the kidneys several times a day. It is the work of the kidneys
to filter out the impurities.
When healthy our kidneys remove about 500 grains of impure matter each day. They work all the
time When unhealthy some share of these impurities is left to poison the blood. These, poisons may
bring on any of the following symptoms of diseases : Pain in the back or sides, headac’ae, rheumatism,
gout, lumbago, nervousness, Bright’s Disease, dark-colored urine, deposits, too much or too little urine,
gravel, dizziness, irregular heart, general debility, etc., etc., etc. In fact these poisons are the foundation
and cause of a very large class of diseases. And they are curable—through the kidneys—if taken in time.
If the kidneys are put in a healthy condition, they will take these poisons out of the blood, and thus re-
move the cause of disease. The kidneys are too little understood. Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills are a
kidney medicine—and a good, reliable, kidney medicine, as hundreds here in these colonies know. Here is
an example :
Mr. Joseph Greve is a well-known coal and wood merchant, living at No. 18 Albion-street, Surry Hills, Sydney. He has been a sufferer from kidney dis-
order for the past five or six years. He says : “ The symptoms were severe backache and an irregularity in the secretions from the kidneys. The pains had such
complete mastery over me at times that I could not rise from my bed. I tried doctors, I tried medicines, but without appreciable benefit. One day, having seen a

sense of relief almost from the first. My back pains gradually grew less and disappeared, as did also the other symptoms of kidney disorder.
Kidney disease is caused by excitement, overwork, nervous tension, fevers, the use of alcohol. The kidneys break down under
the burden of good living, and the strain of worry, excitement, and excessive labor. Kidney disease is the frequent result of
dyspepsia.
Kidney diseases are extremely fatal, and more people die from them than from any other class of diseases. Weak kidneys
leave the poison in the. blood .that healthy kidneys remove from it, and they also allow the nutriment of the blood to leak out. This
double effect is rapidly destructive.

Tossed manes and sweeping tails,
Our thirty head unbroken
Are safe behind the rails ;
Hard-won from stony ridges
And waving bluegrass plains,
By gashes from the gidyeas,
Red spurs and foamy reins !
( Woh ! Blood and Beauty ! These prizes for our
pains.)
We woke with stars a-eluster,
And rode with breaking day ;
We’vo made a right good muster
With not one colt away.
Oh ! Loth they were at leaving,
And twice they broke for home,
AYid Blue Light’s flanks are heaving
And Brownlock’s white with foam.
( Woh ! Blood and Beauty ! You d — d welt had
to come.)
By Snowden’s son—Gray River —
The best blood in the land ;
No finer draft has ever
Upheld the station brand.
They’ll get no chance of hiding
Fenced in the Mile-by-Mile,
We won them by hard riding—
We’ll hold them now by guile !
(Woh! Blood and Beauty! We want you for a
while.)
The bay colt’s there from Blossom,
By Jove ! the beggar’s grown.
That steel-gray out of Possum
Is the best the old mare’s thrown ;
And here’s the brown from Lo-Lo,
That beggar ought to race ;
What price that chap for polo
With the white streak down the face?
( Woh ! Blood and Beauty ! A credit to the place.)
That big chap by the cedar
Full-brother to The Gleam,
We’ll “ mouth ” him for a leader
To adorn the Boss’s team ;
Those browns across the corner
Will make a ripping pair ;
That chestnut out of Lorna
Takes his kicking from the mare !
( Woh ! Blood and Beauty ! No broken leys to spare.)
Jump down there ! —What a scatter !
Get out the ropes and gear ;
We have never broken better
Than the colts we’ll break this year.
Look out the bits and rollers,
The halters and the rest —
This year they’ll know our colors
On the township tracks out West !
(Woh! Blood and Beauty! We'll battle with the
best.)
WILL H. OGILVIE.
Snuggo.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the first place, his name was Snuggs, hut his
pals in Kent-street, and even in Princes-street, as
far as the Queen’s Arms, called him Snuggo. The
only other member of his “ establishment ” was
a ripe and spruce young woman whom he usually
alluded to as Snuggie, thus distorting a distortion.
Snuggo suffered from periodical fits of sobriety,
which, however, were never known to interfere
with his business. That business was chiefly
fried fish and potato-chips, and was carried on in
a long, plain room, odorous with the smell of oily,
evil cooking.
Snuggie, otherwise Jin, was a very plausible
argument in favor of Snuggo having an artistic
strain among his other undisplayed qualities. She
was charming in a black-eyed, black-haired way,
and possessed a deep brunette voice, slightly
tinged with grey, due no doubt to the smell of the
oiled fish and other vices. Jin desired it to be
understood that she was Snuggo’s niece. He,
like his shop, was long and plain, and, in appear-
ance, just the sort of man who might, with all
propriety, have such a pretty niece, and yet there
was an instinctive impression that the relation-
ship was doubtful. It was not so much that
she called him Snuggo, as the way she
caressed him with her large eyes whenever she
looked at him. No young girl could maintain a
reputation for modesty who could say such things
as Jin’s glances said. Her eyes completely re-
pudiated the uncleship.
* * • •
The last of the customers had been bundled out,
and Jin was fitfully wiping down the little rough
tables.
“Snuggo,” she suddenly called out; “come
here. Stand so,” and she placed him under a
kerosene light, and, standing on tip-toes, looked
ea rnestly into his eyes.
“I was trying,” she said, as she moved away
from him, “to find out why I’m so struck on
you.”
“ Bather late, isn’t it?” he asked. “You’ve
been struck on me for two years without needing
any reason.”
' “ Yes, and should have been struck on you for
twenty-two years just the same way, only— ”
She paused, aud a tear fell on her cheek.
“ Only— ” he repeated.
“ Only that I’ve found you to be such a low,
mean, underhanded wretch !” The tear had dried
a 3 quickly as it had fallen.
“Hullo! What’s the game now?” he said.
“ Ain't I always treated you fair ?”
“ Yes, you’ve been as right as rain, and I think
you love me more, lately, than you used to. You
haven’t hit me once this month, and to-day’s the
16th. But I’ve found you out. I didn’t mind
you knocking me about a bit when you had a
drop in; but it was wrong of you, Snuggo, to
be such a thundering, blazing liar ! ”
“ By the Lord, I won’t stand you calling me no
liar ! ” he yelled, grasping a walking-stick with a
brutal flourish and stepping towards her.
She put her hands behind her back and faced
him with hardly a quiver.
“ Don’t be afraid ; I won’t scream,” she said.
“Strike me ; I want yon to.”
“ You want me to ? ” ho said, looking at her in
amazement.
“Yes,” she replied, slowly, “ I want you to. I
want to see if I still love you.”
He dropped the stick and put an arm on her
shoulder.
“ Look here, girlie,” he said, “ tell me why you
think I am a liar.”
“ ’Cause your wife is dead,” she answered,
struggling away from him.
“ Dead! ” He was evidently more startled
than regretful. “Holy sailor! When did she
die?” How do you know ? ”
“ She died up at Oreswick two years ago. She
was kicked to death. It’s no usu trying to tell me
that you know nothing about it.”
“Me! S’elp me, Snuggie, I don't know. I’ll
take me oath I never laid no hand on her.”
“ I know you didn’t, Snuggo. You didn’t care
enough for her. She was kicked to death by a
horse, not by a man ”
“ Oh ! there must be some mistake, Jin. How
d’you know all this ?”
“There ain’t no mistake about it,” she said.
“ Joe the Lumper told me, and he ought to
know.”
“H’m —ye-es,” said Snuggo, reflectively. “I
s’pose Joe’d know. I must see him, and if she’s
dead —then ”
“ There’ll be no ‘ then,’ ” she interrupted,
answering his look. “I’m going to clear out to-
morrow morning.”
“What? Goin’ to leave me?” he said, in-
credulously.
“ Yes ; you’ve told me fifty times that your
missus was alive. You’re too much of a liar,
Snuggo, for me to stick-to any longer,” she
replied, determinedly.
“Lord love you, Jin, can’t you believe that I
was always dead sure she was alive ? ’ he asked.
“I don’t believe it, and you can’t make me,”
the girl replied. “You knew she was dead all
the time, and yet you lied about it.”
“But,” said Snuggo, brightening, “if she is
dead you ought to feel all right, ’cause ”
“Look here, Snug,” she broke in, “have I ever
worried you about that ? Ain’t I been content to
be your niece ? ”
“ Yes, you’ve been as good as gold about that,
Jin, and that’s why I can’t make out why you
should want to leave me now when I can make it
up to you—if she is dead.”
“ What a thundering ass you are ! ” and she
stamped passionately. “ That ain’t it at all. I’m
going to leave you ’cause I couldn’t love a liar.
Don’t you think I’ve got some sense of honor ! ”
“ Hay ? ” It wasn’t much to say, but for all
that she angrily struck him on the cheek with her
clenched hand.
“ Curse you ! ” he said (or words to that effect),
getting angry in turn. “ Go, if you want-to.
I’ll see you in the gutter 'fore I’ll do anythin’ for
you again.”
“ My Sunday troubles on you ! You forget that
I can work for my living.”
“Oh, ye 3,” he sneered, “ I s’pose you’ll go on
the bloomin’ stage, hay ?”
“ Not much. I think a bit too much of myself
to show my legs to a crowd of fellows for fifteen
shillings a week.”
Snuggo could find nothing to say to that; he
could only stare fluently.
“Look here, mitey,” he said at length;
“ lemme know just why you’re goin’ to chuck me
over. Is it ’cause you think my missus has been
dead, and you should have been more’n my niece
these two years. Is that the trouble ?”
“That!” she said, scornfully. “Why can’t
you get away from that idea? It’s’cause you’ve
been so mean with me ; so mis-dis-dishon’able —
’cause you’ve been such a liar !”
“Nothin’else, Snuggie, straight?”
“Nothing else. My blooming troubles about
anything else.”
Next morning she had disappeared.
* * * *
She had hot seen Snuggo for four weeks. Then
one mqrping he knocked at the door of her lodg-
ings in Little .Vlontgomery-street, and a inin ite
later was with her in . he shabby little front i co n.
Snuggo looked triumphant, and even essayed to
be jocular.
“ Well, Jin,” he began, “How’s that bloomin’
sense of honor gettin’ on ?”
“ What’s your game,” she said, with evidently-
assumed inlifference. “What have you come
round here for ?”
“ Just to take you back to the shop, that’s all.”
“ You mean you want to marry me. I won’t— ”
“Oh, don't flurry yourself, Jin. You needn’t
be afraid of that. laint goin’ to splice you if you
don’t want me to. I just want you to come back
to your—uncle.”
“I’ll see you in the gutter first, and then I
won’t,” she said, blazing her black eyes at him.
“Well, I’m dead certain you will,” he responded,
coolly. “ Look here, Snuggie, old girl, you left'
me ’cause I was a liar; will you come back if I
aint •
* If you ain’t?’ she repeated. “What do you
mean, Snug?” It was surprising how wistful she
had become in ah instant,
“ I mean that I ain’t no liar. You did make a
mistake. The woman who was kic ed to death
at C res wick was not my wife, ’cause she's alive.
She’s just been given two years for pick-pocket-
>n?-
“So help you, Snuggo?”
“ S’elp, me, Jin.”
“ Snug— ’ she tiptoed, and placed her hands
on his shoulders— “kiss your-your niece !”
4 ‘ Tell us first, Jin, how’s your sense of honor
now, hay?”
“Satisfied, Snuggo, quite satisfied.”
And Jin is back among the fried evils and
potato-chips.
Melbourne.
